GUMTREC
EOBERT BARR, the Scotc-
h novelist, has
been endeavouring to
boom Ambrose Bierce in
England. He suggests Bierce’s lite-
rary equality with Kipling; but
misses the vital point in which
Kipling is superior—humanity.
For intellectual strength Bierce’s
stories are better than Kipling’s;
but they are colder, harder, less
vital. They impress the head : Kip-
ling has a knack of touching the
heart as well.
Jt,
Barr incidentally tells how Kip-
ling, in his early English career,
suppressed “The Book of the
Forty-five Mornings ” sketches
and stories which Barr considered
as good as “Plain Tales.” Kipling re-
torted: “As good isn’t good enough. It
must be better, or it won’t be published.”
And it is only now, when Kipling’s ill-
ness has thrown him behind with his work, that
the book is to be issued under the title “From
Sea to Sea.”
oJ* &
And Barr continues—
On Kipling’s first London desk he carved with his jack-
knife the words which the galley slave cut into the handle
of his oar, “Oft was! weary when I toiled at thee.” He
believed in downright dogged hard work, and the result
appeared to give him no concern. He accepted no con-
tracts when they began to pour in upon him, wrote
exactly what he wanted to write, and left the business
end of the industry to a business man.
Now an author seems to me like a carter with several
loads of stone which he dumps into a pond. The first
load goes plump into the water and disappears ; likewise
the second and the third, leaving no sign, and the work
seems rather hopeless. But by-and-bv a load is dumped
that remains above the surface, and lies there in the sight
of all men. Now the last load would not have been in
the sight of anyone were it not for the other loads that
preceded it. If Kipling had contented himself with one
book, he might have gone back to India, written
“Recessional,” and the poem would never have been
heard of.
Which is true. For the “Recessional ” was heard
of firstly, because Kipling wrote it; secondly, be-
cause it was cleverly composed to suit the
English—it waved their Jingo flag while it smote
their Nonconformist conscience; and only thirdly,
because it was decent verse. It is said that
“ Recessional ” was rescued by Mrs. Kipling from
the basket where the dissatisfied author had
thrown it; and if the story is true, it shows that
K. is a better judge of his own “poetry ’’ than are
many of the people who praise him.
Barr’s conclusion is (in effect) that Bierce is not
popular because he is too fond of writing as he
pleases to care about _ conciliating the public.
“ Bierce is too full of original Hell to succeed in a
Presbyterian world.”
JS jt
“ An Irish priest’s sermon to his congregation:
‘I have come this morning to say three things to
you. The first is something which I know, but
which you cannot know, and that is: I tore my
cassock climbing through the gap in the hedge.
The second is something which I do not know,
but which you all know, and that is : how much
you will give me towards buying a new one. The
third is what none of us know, and that is : how
much the spalpeen of a Protestant tailor will
charge for making it.’ ” As this is taken from The
Outlook, it seems fair to say that The Outlook has
been more interesting, lately; and I would not
willingly read without it.
The new sixpenny novel will doubtfully pay
English authors and publishers as well as the old
six-shilling one. The Outlook gives an estimate
and some figures—
Say, for a minimum output of 50,000 copies (gross
price 6d): —Production and advertising, 2d. Ten per
cent, to the author absorbs |d. For this the publisher
receives from the trade, 3{4cl. Leaving him as profit—Jd.
Examples: R. L. Stevenson’s “Treasure Island” for
the six months to December 31,1898, sold 43,323 copies ;
profit to author, £99 19s. 6d. “ Kidnapped ” in less than
six months sold 59,150 ; profit £136 10s. “Catriona” to
date has sold about 50,000 ; and “ King Solomon’s Mines’*
120,000—0 f course, with corresponding profits.
And the conclusion is that, considering how the
market is swamped with literature, for new novels
the old price would pay better—the large sale does
not make up for the small profit.
In spite of the much-increased cost of printing
and binding, the local author, in respect of the new
style of publication recently introduced, does not
fare so badly if he can command a reasonable sale.
The cost of producing 1000 copies of a book like
Brady’s “The Ways of Many Waters” is about
Fl4O ; of which about FIOO goes for printing,
paper, and stereotyping, and about £4O for bind-
ing and binders’ cloth. Allowing for deductions
and discounts, about 3s. per copy for 950 copies is
received from the booksellers, and about 50 copies
are given for review. Receipts : £142 10s.
Thus the production of 1000 copies barely covers
expenses ; and the persons who chiefly profit from
the author’s work are the local printer and binder
and bookseller. If the book continues to sell,
however, the cost is reduced by printing from
stereotype plates ; and *with the second and
succeeding thousands the- author and publisher
begin to receive some reward for their labour. Of
the ss. which the public pay for a successful book
like Paterson’s “ The Man from Snowy River ” the
wholesale and retail booksellers take the lion’s
share of 2s. per copy ; printing costs about 9d. per
copy ; binding about 7d. ; and advertising and in-
cidentals 2d. or 3d. ; so that author and publisher
usually divide Is. 6d. per copy—after having
nourished a considerable number of local indus-
tries. Ninepence per copy on a ss. book is
equivalent to a royalty of 15 per cent., which is
very rarely paid in England on an author’s first
venture. English royalties to an author like
Kipling are reputed to run as high as 25 per cent.;
but from 10 to 121 is the average; and it needs
a popular book, or a high price, to pay for pub-
lishing at anything beyond 15 per cent. —even
allowing for the cheapness of English production.
t
London’s newer Sunday journalism was a shrewd
knock to the Old Order of English things, and the
Old Order knocked back as hard as it knows
how. One clergyman solemnly burnt one heathen
sheet in his pulpit: the h.s. wished all the
others would buy and burn too. Exeter Hall
was hysterical, and a large section of the
13.1. supported Exeter on grounds less of reli-
gion than of weekly rest. The Daily Tele-
graph, with the more Respectable constituency,
climbed down first ; the Daily Mail continued
longer—with no particular benefit to a reader at
this.distance, though doubtloss to tli6 great con-
venience of many Londoners. Sooner or later, of
course, the attempt is bound to be re-made ; and
there is no more reason why a modern man should
go without his Sunday newspaper than without
his Sunday dinner.
“Don’t write my life,” said Thackeray. And
his daughter wouldn’t, anil his publishers didn’t;
though the prefaces to the Biographical edition of
T’s works infringe the spirit of the injunction, if not
the letter. But, inasmuch as a life is wanted, into
the field comes Richmond—a tyro author, Lewis
Melville, with two big volumes for 325., ,and some
cheerful chapter headings—“ The Tragedy of
Thackeray s Married Life,” “The Quarrel with
Edmund Fates ’’—the very things which Thackeray
wished to bury fathoms deep. And, says a London
correspondent, there is a very pretty row in pros-
pect.

was said to have helped Sydneyite Gilbert Murray
to secure the Greek Professorship in Glasgow
University which he has just vacated (he won both
the daughter and the chair in his 21th year); but
his Greek alone might have served—there seemed

to be no end to his Oxford scholarships. George
Gilbert Aime Murray is third son of the late
Terence Aubrey ditto, 1862-73 Pres, of N.S.W.

a consensus of tired feelings after burning both

Sample of criticism by Dr. Robertson Nicoll,
editor of Eng. Bookman and log-roller who walketh
in darkness—“ the most memorable work of a new
writer I have read during the year is to be found
in the last seventy or eighty pages of ‘ Zack’s ’ Life
is Life. The most serious and important book in
belles-lettres I have seen is Mr. Hardy’s Wessex
Poems.”
“ Treb ” complains—
Why does Roderic Quinn, in a recent “Red
Page ” sketch, sneer at the native salmon ? —a
common error. Salmon is certainly not as good
as schnapper or whiting, but it is by no means to
be despised. Another much-maligned fish is the
leather-jacket, which nearly every fisherman
throws overboard. If they only knew it, leather-
jacket is one of the very best fisli we have.

E.H.H. writes —
Re Landor’s quoted “ best quatrain,” surely a better
is—
I strove with none, for none was worth my strife ;
Nature I loved, and next to Nature Art ;
I warmed both hands before the fire of life ;
It sinks, and I am ready to depart.
True ; these are four splendid lines, but are they
not rather a personal utterance than a Quatrain in
the wide yet special sense ?

In regard to W. Murdoch’s stupid criticism of Ogilvie’s
verses, about Daley’s book, noted in your issue of June 10,
compare the standing motto of the E'amily Herald Sup-
plement —
If a tenth part of the felicities that are enjoyed in the
region of imagination cbuld lie imported; into regions ter-
restrial, wliar. a delightful f hing it would be to awake each
morning to see such a world mice more !
I think that is almost precisely what Ogilvie meant—and
said—when he wrote
And I would be;content, content
With such sweet somts and such gold gleams,
Could all my dole of days he spent
In Daley’s land of Dreams.
THE BOOKFELLOW.
Sea Songs and Chanties.
L. J. Brady, in “ The Ways of Many Waters,”
has essayed a difficult task. It is more or less
easy to pen, an varying degrees of fluency, rhymes
of conventional correctness. It is extremely diffi-
cult to express emotion in the argot foreign to
one s own methods of thought and speech. Brady
has attempted this, and has, on the whole, suc-
ceeded. When the illiterate endeavor to imitate
the words peculiar to the educated, they are
seldom consistent in their corruptions. The Cock-
ney almost invariably says a ’orse ; but in moments
of extreme excitement he pronounces more
euphoniously and says an ’orse. Brady has
not forgotten this. His language is just
consistent enough to give assurance of actu-
ality—no more. Of course, when it is done
properly, diaiect furnishes a suit of piquant Doric
lit to the limbs of a well-proportioned thought-
body ; but it is a danger of subtle seductiveness to
clothe, not live thoughts, but hollow wire effigies
and frames of thought in the dress of dialect.
Jllow much of the alleged quaint philosophy cf
Josh Billings would outlive that phonetic sage’s
translation into the English, say, of Walter Pater?
What of humor there is in Brady is grim
enough, but it is the humor neither of dialect nor
ot verbal exaggeration. It is the humor of pessi-
mism mainly, of fatalism always ; but the pessi-
mism is charged with a certain cynical cheerful-
ness, and the fatalism takes its gruel with a manly
courage, washes down the fiat of the gods with a
. long-sleever of colonial “ swankey,” and - sings
swan s-song to the accompaniment of a pipe—
of fo csle-twist tobacco.
‘‘M‘Fee of Aberdeen” is the high-water mark
ot Brady’s lie.icon. This is the very model of a
sea-ballad - and it is all alive. The atmosj(here of
it is subtly preserved, and is touched with an old-
worldliness of ancient, birnacied craft and sailor--
men of a past fashion. One can see the ship of
the storm -battered Scots skipper, as in the rough
Atlantic waste “her bowsprit stabs the night”;
or when the sun goes down, goes down, and
turns her canvas red. Brady is prodigal of his
pictures : “ the darkened seas',” .the stars o’erliead
“ that splash the skies with sparkling
silver spray, out in the Great Un-
fathomed Deep, away, and still
away.” There is a delightful lilt in
the quaint old ballad repetition :
“They’ve seen the sun go down, go
down “ from morn to noon, from
noon to night ”; “ the wars may come,
the Wars may end, and crowns be
lost or won, he rolls around the roll-
ing world that rolls around the sun
“Aye, round the world, and round
the world, where’er his owners will”;
“ She’ll roll along, she’ll pitch along,
she’ll taek, and turn, and drive ”
and so on.
And what a sketch McFee is ! A
veritable descendant of the days when
‘‘ sailor-men( were 'sailor men, not sea-
sick engineers ! ” /The sailor and the
sailor’s singer are inspired by the three
things that sweeten life with pleasant
sin and make of Death a portal to per-
dition—drink, “wiminin,” and fight,
and Me Pee is the apotheosised type
of the poet’s hero. That craft of his,
tarry of rope and patched of sail,
“ he’ll take her out and bring her home,
or sink her, will McFee. ” He still
believes in the old boat built of teak,
though “men may write most wondrous
books, and men may count the stars, his
aim in life is -still to get all sail upon
his spars.” Creature comforts with him
are not many, but they are strong for
strong deeds in days of distress and death-th mi tun-
ings. A lusty glass of smoky Scotch, a pipe of
“duty-free” from the cabin jar, and the bland-
ishments of the light-o’-love in the port haven.
When he comes to talk of girls, Modesty shuts
hastily-shocked ears, for sailor-men are sailor-
men, “and Neptune rules the rolling deep, but
Venus reigns ashore,” and it’s “rest assured that
Venus is—as Venus was of yore.”
Brady, in his volume of thirty-four sea-songs
and chanties, seldom rings so true a note as an
“ MTee.” He writes with a swinging lilt—the
poems seem often to have written themselves.
Perhaps some of the verses should have been
omitted. The chanty of “The \ ■ Packet”
is not worthy of a volume a
which appears “MTee,’’and “W.
Viking Poray ” —a poem that sings
with a note like • cutlasses clashing,
The stevedoring verses are grimy
and sweaty actualities—all true
songs of unlovely labour. It’s
“yacker, yacker, yacker”for bread
and beer and ffiacoa, with the
grim necessity to graft ever present
in a rough, untutored yearning for
the welfare of the home and the mis-
sus and the kids. There is no liter-
ary finiekry in these axe-hewn
chants of work and worry and
wastrel carouse. Nor is there much
in the love-ditties of the tarry sons
of the seas, in their coarse apprecia-
tions of Sues and Loos and Nancy
Poos. Live and labour, live and
lieker, live and laugh and sail
away ; for the flesh is weak, and the
seas are deep and wide. There is
tragedy, too, as in “What the
Bottle Said;” recklessness and win . wine-{repre-
sented by deep potations of gin and rum and
beer) and eternal woman—of a certain class.
“The Ways of Many Waters,” the poem which
gives its name to the book, is a descriptive
panorama of historical-ewni.-geographical-gazetteer
description. It does not give Brady’s best poeti-
cal value, albeit it contains sun-gilt pictures of
visionary splendor. Brady is essentially the. singer
of the wharves and the ocean tramps and the un-
washed sailor-man in his least alluring moods.
The truth of the poet is his passport to public
favor. He gives, with no ungenerous hand, sea-
spume and ocean spray, and grease and dirt and
stench galore. “Hides and Tallow,” “Lost and
Given Over,” “The Whaler’s Pig,” “Wool, Ho!”
“ With Coal to Callao,” “ Roll the Cotton Down,”
“Nets Below the Gangway,” “Which His Weak-
ness is Women ” —not much material here for the
dilettante of the school della Crusca ! Brady
spells vigor, and his verse is meat of the strongest
—aye, salt-junk from the harness - cask, seldom
sufficiently cooked ; but, such as he is, he is thrice-
welcome in the decadent days of the over-refined
poets of the minor school.
CONTBIBUTED.
Newly Published.
The creator of “Mr. Dooley,” as most people
interested in books have by this time learnt, is
Peter Dunn, Irish-American managing-editor of
the Chicago Journal. And Mr. Dooley evens
odds by becoming Peter Dunn’s literary creator.
The root of the matter is the quaint commentary
of a Chicago “character,” an Irish saloon-keeper
mixing drinks near the Journal office ; but
Mr. Dunn has grown all the branches. Mr.
Dooley in Peace and War is a little too
American for the universal palate; the coming
book of “Mr. Dooley in the Hearts of his Country-
men ” (he mocks George Washington !) should be
better. There is no denying the advent of a
New Humourist, salted for some time, if not for
eternity. Por tasting, consider Mr. Dooley “ On
Books—
“ They ’re on’y three books in th’ wurruld wort’ read in’,’’
said Mr. Doole.y—“ Shakespeare, th’ Bible, an’ Mike
Ahearp’s Histhry iv Chicago. I have Shakespeare on
thrust, Father Kelly r-reads th’ Bible f’r me, an’ I didn’t
buy Mike Ahearn’s Histhry because I’ve seen more thin
he cud put into it. Books is th’ ruin iv people, specially
novels. Whin I was a young man, th’ parish priest used
to preach again them ; but nobody knowed what he
me uit. At that time Willum Joyce had th’on’y library
in the Sixth Wa-ard. Th’ mayor gave him th’ bound
volumes iv th’ council proceedings, an’ they was a very
handsome set. Th’ on’y books I seen was th’ kind that
has th’ life iv th’ Pope on th’ outside an’ a set iv dominos
on th’inside. They’re good readin’. Nawthin’ cud be
better f’r a man whin he’s tired out afther a day’s wurruk
thin to go to his library an’ take down wan iv th’ gr-reat
wurruks iv lithratchoor an’ play a game iv dominos f’r th’
dhrinks out iv it. Anny other kind iv r-readin’, barrin’
th’ newspapers, which will niver hurt anny onedycated
man, is destructive iv morals.
“ I had it out with Father Kelly, th’ other day, in this
very matther. He was coinin’ up fr’m down town with
an ar-rmful iv books f’r prizes at th’ school. ‘ Have ye
th’Key to Heaven there?,’says I. ‘No,’ says he, ‘ th’
child her that ’ll get. these books don’t need no key. They
go in under th’ turnstile,’ he says, laughin’. ‘ Have ye
th’Lives iv th’ Saints, or th’ Christyan Dooty, or th’
Story iv Saint Rose iv Lima?’ I says. ‘ I have not,’ says
he. ‘I have some good story-books. T'd rather th
kids’d r-read Charles Dickens than anny iv th’ tales iv
thim holy men that was burned in lie or et up be lines.’
be says. ‘ It does no good in these degin’rate days to
prove that th’ best that can come to a man f’r behavin’
himself is to be cooked in apotordi-gisted be aline,’he says.
Ye ’re wrong,’ says I. ‘ Beggin’ ye’er riv’rince’s pardon,
ye ’re wrong,' I says. ‘ What ar-re ye goin to do with
thim young wans ? Ye ’re goin’ to make thim near-sighted
an’ round-shouldered,’ I says. 1 Ye ’re goin’ to have thim
believe that, if they behave thimsiives an’ lead a virchous
life, they 'll marry rich an’ go to Congress. They’ll wake
up some daj', an’ find out that gettin’ money an’ behavin’
ye’ersilf don’t always go together,’I says. ‘ Some iv th’
wickedest men in th’ vvur-ruld have marrid rich,’ I says.
‘Ye’re goin’ to teach thim that a man doesn’t have to
use an axe to get along in th’ wur-ruld. Ye’re goin’ to
teach thim that a la-ad with a cuilin’ black moustache
an’ smokin’ a cigayeet is always a villvan, whin he's more
often a barber w ith a lar-rge family. Life ! ’ says I.
‘.There’s no life in a book. If ye want to show thim what
life is, tell thim to look around thim.’ ”
(Grant Richards ; 2s. 6d.)

THE KITCHEN LANCERS.—By D. H. Souter.
The-third number of The Australian Maga-
zine is the most generally interesting yet pub-
lished. I). H. Souter is well represented in the
drawing of “The Kitchen Lancers,” reduced for
reproduction on this page. Henry Lawson has
all the tragedy of a bushwoman’s life in some fine
verses, “Past Carin’” ; and G. W. Lambert sup-
plies fitting illustration by re-drawing the model
who served him for the illustration of “Jim’s
Whip,” in Boake’s book of verses. W. B.
Beattie’s serial story exhibits some good touches.
( Win. Brooks ; 9d.)

Bayswater, Sydney, October, 1898.
W. H. Malyox, Esq., Man. Chemist, Ballarat.
Dear Sir,—For years past (about ten), I have been
troubled with “ Acne ” in its worst form, and have sought
advice from the best Australian and London doctors, with
only fair results. A Victorian friend of mine, some four
or five weeks ago, sent me a tin of your “ Ligmolliene,"
and speaking very highly of its wonderful curative powers.
I naturally took same “Cum Grano Solis," and was in-
clined not to use it ; eventually I tried your ointment
with 1 ruly marvellous results, and am now entirely cured ;
and think people who suffer from skin diseases are greatly
indebted to you for your wonderful remedy; I shall
advise all my friends with skin troubles to use same'."
Wishing “ Ligomolliene ” all the success it richly deserves,
Yours faithfully, GEOItGE J. O REGAN.

Doveton-street, Ballarat,
My dear Mr. Malyon,— August 21st, 1898.
With great pleasure I now sit down to record progress
with regard to my old enemy, “ Eczema Capitis,” from
which I suffieted.Jio.twJthstamlma' constant treatment for
some years under the prescription of Professor M‘Call
Anderson and other eminent dermatologists, with only
partial and temporary relief. When I met you I com-
menced somewhat sceptically your ointment Ligmolliehe,
anil after about two months’ treatment I may honestly say
my enemy is defeated, and I am perfectly well. I will only-
add that since I commenced the treatment of myself I have
prescribed It to others, and am still doing so with brilliant
results. Please accept my hearty thanks, and remain yours
sincerely, TUGS. LE GAY HOLTIIOUSE, M.R.C.S., L.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Asserted once more that phthisis is cur-
able. This time it is the Berlin Science Congress
which is responsible for an announcement that
turns up regularly every few years. But the
slaughter never slackens !
Miss Kate Piety, of New Norfolk
is said to have discovered some long-lost relatives
by means of a note « rapped round an apple winch
she packed for exportation to Hingland. the
case of apples was sold to a man in Buckingham-
shire, who gave Miss Kate’s note to the Press,
and the news that a “ very dark, good-looking
girl would be pleased to see any of her kinsmen
who might chance to strike Tasmania was pub-
lished with enthusiasm by the Daily Mail. Two
days later a Piety family wrote from Maidstone,
Kent, announcing their relationship to the fair—
no, the very dark—apple-packer. A pretty little
incident illustrating the fact that \\ oman is still,
mixed up with apples, and considers her personal
appearance the only thing worth talking about to
I a mixed audience.
Jock M'Sorley's Mare.
Well, his name was not M'Sorley, we have just
adopted that,
But he was a famous publican on early Ballarat.
He was rubicund and cheerful, and he seemed
without a care,
As he drove along
And hummed a song
Behind his creamy mare.
From authenticated history M‘Sorley, it appears,
Had attended every funeral for over twenty years.
Be the party friend or enemy, the weather foul or
fair,
You could bet your purse
Behind the hearse
Was Jock M'Sorley’s mare.
Jock M'Sorley’s mare was ancient, she was
“ rising twenty-two ”
When he swapped her for a bullock to a burly
butcher, who
There is reason for assuming, was entirely un-
aware
Of the length of days
And whims and ways
Of Jock M'Sorley’s mare.
Now the butchers held a picnic every year at
Ballarat,
Which the knights of steel and cleaver got
extremely jolly at ;
And they took their wives and sweethearts to the
annual aJair,
And they made, of course,
A picnic horse
Of Jock M'Sorley’s mare 1
It was rough on Rosinante, with the pace she used
to keep —
Not a danger of her bolting if she d dn’t fall
asleep 1
But she jogged along serenely, and she never
turned a hair;
She was sure and slow—
Extremely so,
Was Jock M'Sorley's mare.
As the picnickers proceeded there was nothing
very wrong,
Till a funeral procession from the country came
akmg;
And you should have seen the mourners and the
undertakers stare
At the consequent
Bewilderment
Of Jock M'Sorley’s mare !
Then she joined in the procession with decorum
and with grace,
All the flogging in creation couldn’t shift her from
her place ;
And the scene was most disgraceful with the oaths
that tilled the air ;
Oh, they beat the world,
The curses hurled
At Jock M'Sorley’s mare !
Well, she followed the procession to the cemetery
gate,
And the starting of that picnic was particularly
late ;
And the mare was never seen again, but Balla-
raters swear
That the butcher-boys
Made saveloys
Of Jock M'Sorley’s mare !
W. T. GOODGE.
Town and Country.
Scene, upon a country roadside,
And a squatter rides along,
Meets the cookies, shearers, whalers,
And a miscellaneous throng,
All the stranger, bush-bred legion.
Yet he nods, and “ Very dry ! ”
Or, “ Good morning ! ” “ Want a man, sir?
And the squatter passes by.
Scene, a crowded city street, and
Time is just about at noon.
On the path, the, crowd is jostling :
“ Hurry up ! ” “ You’ll get there soon ! ”
In the street the traffic’s blocked, ’midst
“ Are yer drunk ? ” and 1 * Can t yer di ive ?
“ Curse yer, git out of the way there !
That’s amidst a city’s hive.

yankee Doodle
IN AMERICA.
The Legend.
IT was in the summer of 1778, Wash-
ington, with his army ever active,
determined to oppose the British
general, Sir Henry Clinton, who, having
evacuated Philadelphia, was retreating
through New Jersey on New York. The
forces met on 28th June at Monmouth.
La Fayette conducted the attack. The
heat was so intense that some refused to
fight, and many fell dead from exposure
to the sun’s rays. Both sides fought
desperately. As the day waned, and the
flagging spirits of La Fayette’s soldiers
were at a low ebb, a strange sight aroused
them. Marching with drum and fife
was an old man, Jeremiah Wynton, and
his son, and grandson. The old father,
with silvery flowing locks, sturdily strode
through the field. The son with ban-
daged head fifing lustily, the youth,
catching the spirit of his grandsire, rolled
out with him the soul-inspiring rattle of
the drum. Father, son, and graiidson
bad aecompanied the troops as volunteers,
and' were ever in the forefront of the
fight. The fierce light of battle was in
their eyes, they knew no fear, and many
a wild cheer arose, as on' that fateful
day Yankee Doodle was heard as never
was heard before or since.

The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘Correspondence” column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 8000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, JUNE 17, 1899.
Sydney versus N.S. Wales.
Next week’s vote will go a long way to de-
cide whether Sydney owns New South Wales,
or New South Wales owns Sydney. The
question as to which is the tail and which is
the dog is awaiting settlement. The anti-
Federal movement in N.S.W. is almost
wholly the uprising of Sydney against the
country. When N.S.W. asked the other
provinces, for the fifth time in the last 20
years, to come in and federate, they assented,
despite the fact that N.S.W. had already
gone back on its invitation four times. When
N.S.W. informed them that they were all
politically-infected and politically-accursed
spots in which no clean and respectable
Federal capital could possibjy be allowed to
exist, they took the insult with a better
grace than might have been expected. When
N.S.W. demanded the capital for itself, they
gave way peaceably. The only stipulation
they made was that the Federal City must
not be in Sydney, or within 100 miles of it;
and thereupon the fury of the Sydney land-
lords and dealers and importers rose to fever-
heat. Inside that 100-mile radius there is a
N.S.W. population of about 650,000. Out-
side it there is a N.S.W. population of about
670,000. As the infuriated property-owners
of Sydney put it, the stipulation with re-
gard to the 100-mile radius “deprived
650,000 N.S.W. citizens of the chance of
having the capital among them.” And when
the Sydney property-owners demand that
the capital shall be in their city, and nowhere
else, they insist on depriving about 900,000
N.S.W. citizens of the chance of having the
capital among them. But the 900,000 are
the country people of N.S.W., and in the
eyes of the Sydney plutocrats they are of
absolutely no account.
♦ * * *
There are the best possible reasons why
the Federal Convention should (at the in-
stigation of Premier Turner of Victoria)
have barred all the existing capitals from
being the Federal City. There was also the
best of reasons why the Premiers’ Conference
should have renewed the stipulation. The
Federal Capital will inevitably attract a
large population—probably, in a few years,
a population of from 50,000 to 100, (Mb
That will mean the creation of wealth in the
shape of land values over all the Federal
district to the extent of probably twenty
millions. The Constitution, if the anti-
-1 ederal C. G. Heydon’s legal opinion of
the clause is worth anything, wisely provides
that the capital shall be built in some place
where the Commonwealth can acquire 100
square miles of soil, so that this immense
access of wealth will be the property of the
Australian people. If the capital were
planted in Sydney the land would not be
the property of the Australian people—the
Commonwealth couldn’t buv Sydnev even
if N.S.W. would sell it, So all the extra
millions of values created by the increase of
population would be simply so many millions
added to the wealth of the Sydney land-
holders. The people of N.S.W., as the
largest partners in the Commonwealth will
own about £8,000,000 of the value of the
Federal district. The city landlords, who
would cheerfully see the N.S.W. people
hanged first, ask them to throw this away
and stipulate for a Federal capital where
all the increment will go to the big
earth - monopolists of the metropolis.
And to further persuade them to this
end they lament over the wrong done
to N.S. Wales by the Premiers’ Confer-
ence when it fixed the 100-mile limit, and
thereby “ debarred 670,000 of the people of
N.S. W. from having the capital among them. ”
And about the wrong which they want to
do when they demand that Sydney alone
shall be eligible, and that the rest of the
province shall be declared an infected region
and for ever unfit to possess the Federal city,
and that 900,000 of their own countrymen
shall be debarred from possessing the same,
they say absolutely nothing.
* * * *
The Federal district is to consist of 100
square miles—an area 10 miles long by 10
broad. This will be the property of Aus-
tralia in general. When a large population
gathers upon it, as it inevitably will, this
area, probably worth now from £1 to £3 an
acre, may easily grow to be worth £20,000,000,
of which N.S. W.’s share will be £7,000,000
or £8,000,000. That will be so much
clear profit to N.S. Wales in general—
the profit which the private landlords of
Sydney are hungering after. If the capital
is planted in the centre of the Federal pro-
perty, everything distant over five miles from
the middle of the new city will be on N.S.W.
soil. The advent of a large population will
vastly improve land-values over the border
of the Federal district in N.S.W., and that
increase, though created by the expenditure
of the money of all the provinces, will belong
to N.S.W. exclusively. The construction of
the new city must supply a lot of work to
N.S.W. citizens. They can’t expect to get all
of it, but as the person on the spot the
N.S.W. man must get a large share. The
city which is built with the cash of all
the Commonwealth must furnish employ-
ment to the adjacent N.S.W. agricultur-
ist in growing provisions for it, and the
N.S.W. mines in providing it with fuel,
and the N.S.W. railways in carrying its
various supplies. And the Sydney traders
ask N.S.W. to forego these advantages also,
and to insist that there shall be no Federa-
tion unless the provisions for the new popu-
lation are bought from the Sydney shops,
and the extra demand helps to boost up the
value of agricultural land in the county of
Cumberland, and to urge on the importation
of American and other provender at the
Sydney wharves. Also, the Sydney traders
incidentally ask the N.S.W. country folks to
declare themselves an infected race and quite
unfit to have the Federal capital among them.
If the new capital is anywhere within
reach of the sea either to the north or south,
it will probably, some day, want a nearer
seaport than Sydney and direct railway com-
munication therewith, whereby N.S.W. will
come into possession of another big port at
Jervis Bay or Grafton or elsewhere. This
will be good for N.S.W. in general—it is
good for any State to have three big ports
instead of two—but the Sydney traders
would gladly abolish every port in N.S.W.
in order to drag all trade to their own centre.
Wherever the capital is it will ooen up the
interior of N.S.W. with the money of all
the six provinces, for the only thing which
keeps the interior from opening up is the
want of an adjacent market. With a
sufficiently good market at hand it
would pay to irrigate the worst
desert in Cornstalk Land and make it
blossom like the rose. The theoretical
desire to open up the interior is on the lips
of every Sydney patriot, but when the
chance presents itself the only place he
really wants to open up is George-street,
with a bit of Pitt-street and King-street and
Circular Quay, and he asks the man who
battles in the lone wilderness Out Back, and
hardly ever sees a city or a gleam of civilisa-
tion, to vote down the union which will'
bring a new city and a civilisation to his
door. Also he is asked to rise up and object
to the coming customers, and to tell them
that the only price at which he will federate
is that they don’t come but make for Sydney
instead. Likewise he is requested to state
that he doesn’t want a job at helping to
build the Federal capital ; and that he is
scared lest its proximity increases the value
of his dry, bear-haunted selection and the
proceeds lead him astray ; and that in any
case he loathes the idea of a capital built on
land-nationalisation principles and can’t die
happy under his hot galvanised iron roof
unless the Sydney landlord gets the profits.
* * * *
He is invited to say all these things on the
20th, but he won’t. And it will be a good
thing for the masses in Sydney, and a good
thing for all N.S.W., if he doesn’t. If the
Federal capital were planted in Sydney the
increase of population would be a good
thing for the Sydney city shopkeepers and
the importers ; but then only a few people
have the luck to be city shopkeepers and
importers. It would raise the value of land
and consequently it would raise rents, but
few people own land, and most people pay
rents. And if the capital isn’t at Sydney
that city really loses nothing. It doesn’t
lose the Federal capital, for no place can
lose what it never had. The man doesn’t
lose threepence of his own when he only
misses the chance to steal somebody else’s
threepence. And for N.S.W. in general
there will be a new 7 city to seek work in,
and to build railways to, and to grow food
for. Its existence will make it possible and
profitable to build lines and make roads
where otherwise they wouldn’t pay for
six generations. It will bring a new mar-
ket to the door of the out-back agriculturist
—not to the Sydney importer or middleman.
It will create a haven of refuge in the howl-
ing solitudes, and a streak of civilisation
and a place where the long, cool beer is only
threepence, in a region where all these
things are much lacking. It will confer the
blessings of literature and the drama and the
variety leg-show on a country that lias long
pined for them in vain. It will move the
curse of loneliness and emptiness a long
stage further back, and shift the dog-leg
fence another degree towards the sunset; it
will make irrigation possible where now
there is only drought, and substitute live
people for the dead agriculturists who walk
about not knowing they are dead, and create
cathedrals and public libraries where the
gum-tree and the ant-heap are now, and
make great newspapers where the chief
knowledge of passing events is based on what
Bill heard at the roadside pub. And it will
open the way for the grandest experiment in
land nationalisation the world can show, if
0. G. Heydon’s legal opinion as to the mean-
ing of clause 125 is worth anything—the
capital of a great continent owned by the
people instead of by the land-grabber, and
the boomster, and the jerry-builder—a
model city rising in the wilderness of empti-
ness and great spaces and loony bullocks. If
anyone knows of any other way by which a
town of 50,000 or 100,000 people is likely to
be created in the interior of Australia any
time within the next hundred years—well,
Australia is waiting to hear from him.
♦ * * *
And the people of N.S.W. are requested
to burn down this coming metropolis next
week, because it will cost the Telegraph
£5OOO a year to wire its Parliamentary re-
ports to its office in King-street, Sydney, if
the Federal Parliament is located anywhere
beyond a tram-ride of its premises. Also,
because the Sydney property-owners want to
sell allotments at three times their value to
build the new Houses of Parliament on, and
to let their empty tenements to the Federal
State officials, and make a land-boom and
charge higher rents; and because the
Sydney traders want to sell food and drink
and boots to the new population. Therefore
the Telegraph and MacLagrin and G.
Dibbs —the long man who looks like an
unreliable report balanced on its end—rave
exceedingly. And next Tuesday N.S.W. is
cordially invited by these patriots to cut its
throat in their private quarrel, and be proud
to offer up its severed jugular in their
interests.
The Blessings of the Blot.
Now that the Referendum is at hand, the anti-
Federal party has settled down to concentrate its
best lying power on two principal points—the
equal State representation in the Federal Senate,
and the awful effects of the Braddon “Blot.”
The average person never seriously studies the
Bill, but gathers his impressions from scraps of
oratory, and bits of half-remembered conversations,
and vague recollections about something he
thought he saw in a newspaper; and the average
person has a bad memory, and mixes up these
hazy recollections with cricket and football items.
Therefore, even at this late hour, there are plenty
of people who give credit to the tremendous lying
of the Telegraph, and fancy that the Braddon
clause is a scheme whereby the Federal Govern-
ment is pledged to raise £4 in customs revenue
when only £1 is wanted, and to drink itself into a
lunatic asylum with the other £3. So it is worth
while to state the whole case for the Blot over
again, and (what the Telegraph and the other
anti-Federal perjurers never do) to give chapter
and verse from the Bill and from official publica-
tions for every statement; so that the public can
verify the assertions for themselves.
* * * *
The Blot itself is contained in clause 87 of the
amended Bill —the one which any elector can
obtain gratis on application at a post-office—and
reads thiis:
During a period of ten years after the establishment
of the Commonwealth, and thereafter until the Parlia-
ment otherwise provides, of the net revenue of the Com-
monwealth from duties of customs and excise not more
than one-fourth shall be applied annually by the Com-
monwealth towards its expenditure.
Tlie balance shall, in accordance with this constitution,
be paid to the several States, or applied towards the pay-
ment of interest on debts of the several States taken over
by the Commonwealth.
» * * *
The Bulletin urges the public not to
listen to the bald assertions of the Tele-
graph (which has £5OOO a year to save by pro-
curing the defeat of any Federation Bill 'which
doesn’t fix the capital in Sydney), or Dr. Mac-
XiAURiN, of the Colonial Sugar Refining Co. (whose
cheap nigger-grown Fijian sugar will almost cer-
tainly be shut out under Federation in favor or
an Australian product grown by white men); but
to read the above clause carefully 18 times, and
then see for themselves if the following statements
are, or are not, exactly true :
(1) The clause does not fix any tariff, large, small, or
medium, all the Sydney Sunday rag's yells about the
“Braddon Blot Tariff” notwithstanding. It says that
the Federal Government is to have one-fourth and the
State Governments three-fourths of the customs and
excise revenue, but it doesn’t say whether that revenue is
to be £1 a year for all Australia, or 2s. 6d., or £9,000,000.
It leaves the question of high or low tariff absolutely
open.
(2) It doesn't even indirectly commit the Federal
Government to a high tariff. It doesn’t say that the
Federal Government is to pay all its expenses out of the
one-fourth of customs and excise allotted to it, and there-
fore when it wants 1:250,000 for itself it needn’t raise a
million and hand £750,000 back to the States. The
Federal Government may raise what money it wants, if it
feels so inclined, by land-tax, income-tax, probate-duties,
stamp-duties, totalisator-tax, poll-tax, dividend-tax, or in
any other way, and the whole of the money raised from
these sources goes to the Federal Treasury.
(3) The Freetrade party proposes, of course, to de-
mand that there shall be no customs or excise and that
the Federal Government shall raise all its revenue by
direct taxation. A clause which says that the customs
and excise revenue (if there is any) is to be applied in a
certain fashion can’t hamper the party which, if it gets
into power, isn’t going to have any customs and excise.
The fact that the Federal Government must give up
three-fourths of its tariff receipts to the States doesn’t
matter to a party which, if it gets into power, is going to
give up the whole of the tariff receipts in any case. The
situation is just the same as if the law said that three-
fourths of all money stolen must be given to the Church.
The honest man would reply that, as he never stole any
money, the clause didn’t affect him.
* * * »
This is the situation while the refund provision
exists. But the period of its existence is strictly
limited. In fact, it is limited in three different
ways:
(1) The refund to the States stops as soon as the
Commonwealth makes arrangements for taking over the
State debts. Then the Commonwealth retains the three-
fourths of the tariff revenue (if there is any) to help pay
interest with. This provision may abolish the whole
refund business within a year.
(2) If the Commonwealth doesn’t take over the State
debts (though it is almost sure to do so), and doesn’t
make the clause of no account by going in for Freetrade
and thus having no tariff revenue to divide, still the
Braddon clause may be abolished at any time by the
ordinary process of amending the Constitution. The
Constitution may be amended (clause 128) by the consent
of one House of the Federal Parliament, and the assent
of a bare majority of the people voting and a bare
majority of the States. The Braddon clause says that the
arrangement shall last ten years, but that statement can
be amended out of the Constitution like any other
statement.
(3) After ten years the Braddon clause can be struck
out by Parliament without going through the process of
amending the Constitution ; when the ten years is up it
merely remains in the Constitution “ until Parliament
otherwise provides.” If the small and allegedly “ neces-
sitous ” States persuade the Senate to object to its aboli-
tion it can be abolished at a Joint Sitting of the two
Federal Houses at which Joint Sitting N.S.W. and Vic-
toria can command a big majority.
**‘ * *
The Braddon clause is, therefore, an exceed-
ingly temporary concern at the best. Still, it is
almost certain to be in force for a little while, and
it is interesting to consider the allegedly awful
results that may rise while it lasts. At present,
the six provinces (Queensland, N.S.W., Victoria,
Tasmania, Westralia, and S.A.) raise nearly
=£7,000,000 a year in customs and excise revenue,
and the interest alone (exclusive of other heavy
charges for redemption, &c.) on their public debts,
is over =£7,000,000. Therefore every one of them
is dependent on its customs and excise revenue to
pay its interest bill and keep it out of bankruptcy.
The exact figures, taken from Coghlan’s “ Statis-
tics of the Seven Colonies of Australasia ” for
1897-98 (tables 48 and 55) are these :
Customs and Interest on
Excise. Debt.
N.S. Wales £1,529,830 ... £2,298,974
S. Australia 607,226 ... 953,119
Queensland 1,323,094 ... 1,274,244
Tasmania 371,215 ... 318,731
Victoria 2,110,866 ... 1,842,976
Westralia 1,017,724 ... 325,994
£6,959,955 £7,014,038
The Constitution does not definitely hand over the
State debts to the Commonwealth. If it had
done so, the State creditors would have got the
benefit of the better security of the Common-
wealth gratis —would have still received the rate
of interest they bargained for while getting
much better security than they bargained for. By
making the transfer optional, the Common wealth
can say to the holders of the .£1,000,000 of 6
per cent, stock, and the £2,500,000 of 5 per cent,
securities, and the £5,000,000 of per cents., and
the £9(5,000,000 of 4 per cents. : “If you are pre-
pared to accept 3 per cent, stocks in exchange for
your present holdings you can have the vastly im-
proved security of the Commonwealth ; if not,
you must do without it, ” whereas, by making the
transfer absolute and compulsory, these creditors
would have got the extra security and still received
their exorbitant 4 and 6 and 6 per cent. There-
fore, for purposes of economy, the -transfer of the
State debts to the Commonwealth was not made
compulsory.
* * * *
And this opened up a new danger, to meet
which the Braddon clause was devised. For the
Commonwealth might not take over the State
debts, or it might not do so for years. And in the
meantime each State would have handed over to
the Commonwealth the customs and excise on
which it wholly relied to meet its interest bill,
and would still be responsible for the interest bill.
And the Federal Government, it was feared,
might raise, say, £6,000,000 a year in customs and
excise, and though it needed less than £1,000,000,
might “blew ” the whole lot in riotous living and
leave the States utterly bankrupt, every one. Of
course the country would make an end of such a
Government at next election, but every State
might have gone broke before next election. Any-
how, if the Commonwealth started under a law
which made the States solely responsible for their
debts, yet deliberately empowered the Federal Par-
liament (which wasn’t responsible for the debts) to
deprive them of all means to pay their interest, it
would be formally authorising repudiation.
* * * *
The Braddon clause doesn’t definitely provide
the States with sufficient money to pay their
interest bills. - But it at least provides that the
Federal Government shall not raise £6,000,000 a
year by the handy customs and excise expedient
and spend the whole of it in riotous living at the.
Federal capital. It prevents the other provinces
snatching at N.S.W.’s alleged surplus, by provid-
ing that N.S.W. shall get back its own surplus.
It offers a certain guarantee to the public creditor.
And if there had been no Blot the Bill would have
provided :
(1) That the States, which had borrowed £190,000,000,
woutd hand over to the Federal Government the only
fund out of which they have hitherto been able to pay
interest on this sum, without making any stipulation
that the interest would he provided for.
(2) That the Federal Government, which only wants
about £750,000 for its own expenditure, could raise
£7,000,000, or any other large sum, by customs and excise,
and spend it all in riot and dissipation, with no obligation


whatever to provide for paying interest on the public
the other provinces could grab N.S.W.’s sur-
plus, or anybody’s surplus, if they could get it.
The Blot was put in as a plain statement that
Australia intended to pay its interest bill honestly,
and that no province meant to rob another pro-
vince. Its duration was limited, not because Aus-
tralia meant to leave off being honest, but because
there is always a possibility of some day improving
the mechanism of the arrangement. The anti-
Bederalists of N.S.W., who demand that the
good old Blot shall be struck out, ask that there
be no provision for paying interest on the public
debt, and that the other provinces shall not pro-
mise to hand back N.S.W. its surplus honestly and
fairly. And, if there were no Blot, these same
people would preach furiously that the want of a
Blot made Federation utterly impossible, _ and
showed that the motives of the other provinces
were unspeakably bad and sinister.
* • • *
If Australia means to pay the interest on its
public debt honestly, then the Blot makes no
difference. Australia, as a whole, is working
under the Braddon clause now, only in a more
aggravated form: instead of setting aside three-
fourths of its customs and excise revenue to pay
interest on its debt, it sets aside the whole of it and
£50,000 more. Every separate province, except
Westralia, is working under the Braddon clause
without knowing if —particularly N.S.W., which,
instead of setting aside three-fourths of its customs
and excise to pay interest with, as required by the
Blot, sets aside all of it and £700,000 more.
N.S.W. has been working under the Braddon
clause without knowing it ever since 188 G.
Even if the Commonwealth Government heaped
up its tariff to 48s. per head per annum, every pro-
vince, bar W.A., would still have to work under
the Braddon clause —or else give up pay-
ing interest on its debt. And if the Braddon
clause is thrown out of the Constitution 1300
times, Australia will still go on working under it—
unless it repudiates its debts, The one reason
why Australia will set aside three-fourths of its
customs and excise revenue to pay interest with is,
not that the Braddon clause says so, but that it
takes more than three-fourths of the biggest tariff
revenue any Treasurer is ever likely to get out of
Australia to pay the interest.
<* * * *
The customary anti-Biot argument is that,
under this famous clause, “ the Federal Govern-
ment must raise four times the tariff revenue it
requires.” Which is half the truth. The whole
truth is that the Braddon clause will compel it
to raise four times the tariff revenue which it
WOULD REQUIRE IP IT.STOPPED PAYING INTEREST ON
the public debt. And that isn’t nearly so bad as
the fact that N.S.W. is now compelled to raise
several million times the tariff revenue which it
would require if it didn’t pay interest on its public
debt.
* • * *
The Bulletin isn’t apologising for the Braddon
clause ; that requires no apology. Nobody apolo-
gises for his virtues, as a rule, or asks people to
try to put up with his good qualities till he can
get rid of them. This paper simply asks N.S.W.
to rise as one man on the 20th of June and vote
for the Federal Constitution because, among its
other good qualities, the Braddon clause is in it.
It asks them to have sufficient sense to recognise
a good clause when they see it, and to act accord-
ingly. And it proposes three cheers for the good,
honest, far-seeing, sagacious Blot and for its
author.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Nigger Apparition.
Sydney Telegraph invented a great nigger lie
and apparition in its issue of Bth June, as still
another reason why Australia should not
federate. It was a convulsion in six fits, and
the respective fits were as follows :
Fit 1: The Commonwealth Bill provides (clause 51,
section 26) that the Federal Parliament shall have power
to make laws dealing- with “ the people of any race, other
than the aboriginal race in any State, for whom it is
deemed necessary to make special laws.” (Aboriginals,
being regarded as ordinary citizens, are left under the
control of the States.) This gives the Commonwealth
absolute power to exclude all niggers, Chinamen and
other aliens. The Telegraph says the object of the
clause is obviously to admit niggers and form a nigger
province in the Northern Territory. If the framers of the
Constitution had wanted to admit niggers, the easy way
to do it would have been by giving no power of exclusion
and just letting the niggers come. In the frantic lexicon
of the D.T., the fact that they gave to the Commonwealth
special powers to exclude niggers shows that they don’t
want to exclude niggers !
Convulsion No. 2 : The Telegraph says that B. R.
Wise is supposed to be, and that ex-Govt. Resident
, Northern Territory is, in favor of a nigger

accepts the Bill on the 20th. In its own mind,
or home for incurable delusions, it sees, as one
looking darkly through a bottle, that the white

will inevitably elect a Federal Parliament
which will start nigger importation on a huge

it knows by instinct that ships will give up
coming to Sydney and Melbourne and Ade-
laide (where there are 3,000,000 people to trade
with), and insist on going to the Northern
Territory (where there are 4898 people scat-
tered over an area larger than N.S.W., Vic-
toria, and Tasmania all put together), and all
our goods will come down by rail just as all
N.S.W.’s imports are landed now in the Gulf
of Carpentaria, and brought down by go-cart
and gradual perambulator. Which is a Terrible
Outlook.

The Anti-Billious Bill.
Ninety-nine per cent, or more of the N.S.W.
people who are not too tired or too intoxicated
to say something about Federation assert that
they believe in Australian union with all their
heart and soul. Of the remaining fraction
about one-hundredth part says it is against any
kind of union. The other 99 parts refuse to
say anything because if they allowed their
attention to be distracted they would lose their
hold of the lamp-post, and if they lost their
hold of the lamp-post they would fall down.
Every man and newspaper who orates on the
anti-Federal side, and hollers for disunion,
swears with tremendous force that he believes
in union, and yearns after union, and would
even sacrifice his mother-in-law to bring it
about. Only he is against this Bill and wants
to see it thrown out so that a better one may
take its place. Therefore N.S.W. runs up
against these three alternatives :
(1) It may take'this Bill.
(2) It may wait for the better Bill that is always
promised and which is never forthcoming.
(3) It may do with no Bill at all.
Considering that there are only about two men
(and one of them is George Pile’s dog) in all
N.S.W. who say they are wholly against
union the latter alternative may be dropped.
It becomes a question of taking this Bill, or
waiting for the better anti-Billious Bill which
is never shown to the public. Neither Dibbs,
nor the Heydons, nor Pilcher, nor the Tele-
graph, nor the Sabbath Shellback, nor the
howling destructionist Norton, nor the
Colonists anti-Bill League, nor J. Want has
ever drafted or published that better Bill
which they all say they are ready to work for
as soon as the present one is defeated. The
Bulletin, two years ago (on 23rd January,
1897), before even the Convention met, pub-
lished its idea of a Draft Federal Constitution
in full, and it has defended it ever since. The
Federal party has issued its Bill, which is
now before the country. And the anti-
Billious party—the Telegraph, Sabbath tip-
ster, Dibbs, Heydon, Pilcher, Want, Mac-
Laurin, “Brolly” Hughes, and the rest—
address the country thus :
If you reject this Bill we will help you to get a better
one. We refuse to show you the better one ; you must
take our word for it that there will be a better one. We
are the benevolent confidence-men of N.S.W., and you
must exhibit your confidence in us by throwing away
this Bill first. Please drop the bone, and you have our
unsupported assurance that the shadow will be forth-
coming later on. We say that we know how to draft a
Federal scheme which will be acceptable all round, but
we never do it—not in public, anyhow. We say that
every man of sense knows how the financial clauses, and
those dealing with representation in the Senate, and all
the other disputed matters, ought to read, but we never
publish these clauses as they ought t.o be, so that the
public can see how beautiful and simple they are.
On the 20th you have to vote either to accept
the Bill you see or to wait for the alleged
future Bill that you don’t see. Please vote for
the Bill that you don’t see. We don’t produce our
improved Bill—not because we haven’t one -but solely
because it is so perfect and so beautiful that the
sight of it would break you all up. The other side has
produced its Bill, and we say with much blast ami curse
that it is bad. No two of us agree as to the exact nature
of its badness, but it is bad. And if you will kindly throw
it away before seeing our greatly improved Bill, we beg
to assure you that when you do see our greatly improved
Bill you will be astonished. As a matter of fact, you will
never see it, and there isn’t any, and if yon did see it
before the Referendum you would set us down as lunatics
and never listen to anything we say again. The other
party consists of the people who do things. We
are the people who stand round and say that we
can do the things a great deal better, and are
going to do them a great deal better some day.
The fact that not one of us has ever done anything to
improve the N.S.W. Constitution proves that we know
exactly how to make a perfect Constitution. Please
empty out the present water (which we say is dirty)
before you get a cleaner sample. If we showed you the
Bill that we are prepared to fight for, then you could
decide on the 20th whether it was better to pin your
faith to Barton’s Bill, or to wait on the chance of getting
ours, or to give up the idea of union altogether. But we
haven’t sufficient faith in any Bill we could construct to
let you see it. Also if Norton and Dibbs and Heydon
and Tommy Dodd ever got together in one room to con-
struct anything, the furniture would all be smashed and
somebody’s hair and ear would presently be fired out of
the window. Amen 1

v\ in sit in the Senate.— “Brolly” Hughes.

Lyne’s New Ghost.
Even at this late hour the Sydney anti-Federal
party, per medium of dull Bill Lyne, has con-
trived to invent a new bogey—this at a time
when it looked as if every possible lie was used
up. Says William John (S.M. Herald re-
port, Bth June) :
Notwithstanding: the fact that it had been calculated
that bv 1921 the population of N.S.W. would have
doubled, it would only be entitled to be represented by
two more members in the Lower House. This was
another example of the manner in which the Bill pro-
vided for minority rule.
BiIIJjYNE is hereby warned that even though
the population of N.S.W. has trebled by 1921,
it may not be entitled to any more representa-
tion at all—and even then minority rule will
have nothing to do with it. What the Bill
says is that the House of Representatives shall,
unless the law is altered some day, be, as
nearly as possible, twice as numerous as the
Senate, and
The number of members chosen in the respective States
shall be in proportion to the respective numbers of
their people. —Clause 3U.
Therefore, while the population of N.S.W. is
1,330,000 and that of the Commonwealth
3,700,000, as at present, N.S.W. will have 25
members. And if the population of N.S.W.
doubles by 1921, while that of the other pro-
vinces remains exactly stationary, then N.S.W.
will have 36 members, and the other provinces
will have fewer than they have now. But if
the other provinces are so beastly inconsiderate
as to double their population also, then N.S. W.
will still have 25 members as before—in fact,
the provinces will all remain as before. And
if N.S. W. doubles its population and the other
provinces treble theirs—well, the chances are
that N.S. W. will only have 16 members. And
yet there will be no minority representation
about it. Poor old William John’s idea is
that N.S.W. is the only province that can pos-
sibly increase in population. Or else that,
when there are 72 members to divide among
the States, and N.S.W. is entitled to 25 now
while the other provinces are entitled to 47,
therefore when N.S.W. doubles its population
it should have 50 members. And, even though
the other provinces also double their popula-
tion, they should be content with the other 22
members among them.

The 12| per Cent. MacLaurin Tariff.
The unhappy MacLaurin, who was a
member of the Dibbs Govt, which put on the
one heavy tariff in N.S.W. history, and who
goes round using terrible language about heavy
tariffs, is driven to great straits to explain
himself. One very cheering assertion is this,
in a recent letter to the press :
I calculated that, if N.S.W. gave her surplus to the
other colonies, an ad valorem of about 15 per cent, on all
over-sea imports, taxable and uritaxable, would suffice.
This calculation included Queensland, whose require-
ments have to be provided for, and which now raises duties
on all merchandise, including intercolonial goods, equiva-
lent to an ad valorem of 16.72 per cent.
The Dibbs duties, I may add, only represented an ad
valorem of 9'77 per cent, during the four years they were
in force. The highest year was 1894, when they were
equivalent to 10'68 per cent. So that you will see the
Dibbs duties were much lower than the Federal tariff
would be under the most favorable circumstances, should
the present Bill be accepted.
In reply, The Bulletin begs to present the
miserably inaccurate doctor with these facts
about his own Ministry’s tariff. The highest
gross taxation was in 1892. In that year the
Dibbs customs tariff produced £2,593,839
neb (“ Coghlan’s Statistics ” for 1897,
page 812). The imports that year were
£20,776,526 (same volume, page 126). That
was over 12£ per cent, on imports not
about 10J per cent., as the doctor alleges.
In 1894 the imports were £15,801,941, and the
import duties £2,015,695 —also over 12J per
cent. In other words, the N.S.W. Govt,
charged almost as much on foreign and inter-
provincial imports as Dr. MacLaurin says the
Federal Govt, will have to charge on foreign
imports alone. And yet these were years of
cheapness, when prices were lower than they
had been for many previous years. And Dr.
M‘L.’s 12J per cent, all-round tariff brought
about none of the horrors which, he says, a 15
per cent, tariff on foreign imports only must
create. And Dr. MacLaurin’s gruesome appa-
rition of ruin didn’t stalk the earth, and none
of the dreadful things which he says must
happen showed any signs of happening. In
fact, barring an imaginary girl who wrote to
Sydney Telegraph, saying that her sick
brother’s cod-liver oil had gone up, there were
no complaints that this paper remembers.

Some More Precedents.
Considering all the jeremiads about the inevit-
able awful results to everybody and the
destruction of everything if the Australian
Commonwealth is built on any principle save
that of representation in Lhe Senate according
to population, the following is worth noting.
Among all the Federations in this world there
is not one which has representation in its Upper
House according to population. There isn’t
even one which has proposed such a thing.
Some examples of how the discrepancy works
are as follows :
Australia: N.S.W. (1,346,000 people), W.A. (168,000),
each 6 senators ; W.A. has eight times its mathematical
proportion.
Canada : Ontario (2,114,000), 24 senators ; Prince Ed-
ward Island (109,000), 4 senators; P.E. Island has 3J
times its correct proportion.
Germany : Prussia (31,800,000), 17 senators ; Schaum-
burg-Lippe (41,000), 1 senator ; Schaumburg-Lippe has 45
times its correct representation.
Switzerland : Zurich (351,000), 2 senators; Uri (17,000),
2 senators; Uri has over 20 times its exact share of repre-
sentation.
United States: New York (6,000,000), 2 senators;
Nevada (46,000), 2 senators ; Nevada has 130 times its
fair share of representation as compared with New York.
Mexico : Jalisco (1,107,000), 2 senators ; Campeche
(88,000), 2 senators. CampSche has times its fair
share as compared with Jalisco.
Brazil: Minas Geraes (3,184,000), 3 senators ; Matto
Grosso (93,000), 3 senators ; latter has 34 times the- re-
presentation per head of Minas Geraes.
This list is too long to be given in full. But
every Federation not included above is built on
the principle of equal State representation in
the Senate. And if there is any Federation
wherein the principle has been found to be a
failure, or where any party has tried to sup-
press it, or where there is any demand for its
suppression, the address thereof isn’t known in
Australia. The men who built all these States
travelled on the idea that it was to the interest
of the country as a whole to make special
efforts to develope the empty and neglected
parts of its territory, and that representation
strictly according to population meant sacrific-
ing the country at large for the benefit of a
few overwhelming cities. They believed in
leaving some of the grease for the lean hog,
and they all, barring property-vote and Tory-
ruled Canada, carried the process much further
thau it is proposed to do in Australia. And
now, at this latter end of the 19th century,
George Pile’s dog rises to say that he knows
a great deal more than George Washington
ever did about building a Constitution ; and
Porfirio Diaz is requested to stand by and
gather wisdom from Tommy Dodd; and the
Swiss republic, after braving the storms of five
centuries, and being held up as the brightest
example in modern history of a free and demo-
cratic nation, learns from G. Republican Dibbs
that it has been wrong from the beginning.
And even “ Old To-morrow,” who raised up
the only federated community in the British
empire, hears in his grave that he was an ass
after all, and that if he had only asked C. G.
Heydon the latter could have shown him the-
right way to do it in ah instant.
The Federal ’Bus-Conductor and the
Old Lady.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Now ’urry, Mrs. New South Wales, and come
along of us,
We’re all a-goin’ ridin’ in the Federation ’bus.
A fam’ly party, don’t you know—yes, Queens-
land’s cornin’, too.
You can’t afford it! Go along ! We’ve kep’ box-
seat for you.
The very one of all the lot that can afford it best,
You’ll only have to pay your share the same as all
the rest. >
You say your sons is workin’ men, and can’t afford
to ride!
Well, all our sons is workin’ men, a-smokin’ up
outside.
You think you might be drove to smash by some
unskilful bloke!
Well, ain’t we all got necks ourselves ? And we
don’t want ’em broke.
You bet your life we’re not such fools but what
we’ll do our best
To keep from harm—for harm to one is harm to
all the rest.
Now, don’t go trudgin’ on alone, but get aboard
the trap;
That basket, labelled “ Capital,” you take it in
your lap!
It’s nearly time we made a start, so let’s ’ave no
more talk ;
You ’urry up and get aboard, or else stop out and
walk.
We’ve got a flag; we’ve got a band; our ’orses
travels fast;
Ho ! Right away, Bill! Let ’em go ! The old ’un’s
come at last 1
THE BANJO.
Good Lord, Deliver Us!
Oh ! save this suffering country from the anti-
Federal blare,
With stalwart arm protect us from the turmoil in
the air,
Anri scatter the parochial mob whose moaning
blatherskite
Runs riot through the country seeking victims left
and right;
Let the Federal Referendum
To the lowest Hades send ’em
Where old Vulcan still is toiling and the fire burns
ever bright.
Oh ! spare Thy cornstalk folks the Sabbath
Dingo’s awful cry ;
And spare them just a little space the daily inky
lie.
Where the Daily Penny Crammer holds forth
from “witness-box,”
And strives to blind its jury with par. and para-
dox ;
Soon the Federal Referendum
Will duly recommend ’em
To surrender at discretion when their ship is on
the rocks.
Oh ! save us from the ghosts that gibber in their
mental fog,
Let Dibbs’s big cigar go out, and please slay G.
Pile’s dog.
Just gag the agitators who speak of “Braddon
Blot,”
And dub our fatted Premier the new Iscariot;
For the Federal Referendum
Doth mightily offend ’em
Because George went “nap” for Sydney, and
failed to get the lot.
Oh! save the civil servants from the threatened
Federal “sack,”
Just tell our Labor members that their screws
they will not lack ;
They who think, like poor Othello, that their
occupation’s lost
And dream of pick and shovel, by cruel tyrant
bossed,
Let the Federal Referendum
A crumb of comfort send ’em,
And prove that all such visions are unreal as any
ghost.

Suburban Tour Dante the Great.
Nothing now has happened in the Sydney
show business this week. 11 very theatre is
mumbling the dusty tail-end of a programme that
is about as old as the tale of Lot’s wife. But
on the coming Saturday Harry Conor’s new
American co. appears at Her Majesty’s in a
musical frivolity called “A Stranger in New
York.” Also, the “Liars” cease from lying at
the Royal, and the prevaricators are at rest.
Pinero’s “ Trelawny of the Wells ” takes the place
of these inaccurate people, who, with the kindly
purpose, of helping a tottering sister out of the
mud of disrepute, told the thing that wasn’t there,
and took a baseless and incompetent affidavit.
The same night the “Three Musketeers”leave off
at the Criterion to make room for Sardou’s “Flesh
and the Devil. ” There is a very reasonable amount
of flesh in the present production—the white,
smooth flesh of Milady when a great part of her
dress comes off in the struggle with d’Artagnan
and the brand on her shoulder is revealed ; and if
there is more flesh in the next one, and the quality
keeps up, this paper looks forward to it with
hope.
• * * *
At this late hour The Bulletin has an
idea for improving Bernard Fspinasse’s fine ver-
sion of the “ Three Musketeers. ” There is a sort
of boom in the “Musketeers” just now, and by-
and-bye, in the slow process of time, it will spread
out into the bakblox, where they like their tragedy
thick and gruesome and slab. So if this scribe
were Mr. Espinasse he would add another pro-
logue for the special benefit of the man far out.
The present prologue goes back beyond the con-
fines of Dumas’ story to the marriage of Athos
and the lady with ithe brand on her shoulder,
but what the bush audience would like would
be to go away to the remote beginning
and see the branding of the lady. It
needn’t take long ; just a dungeon and a
lady with her left arm and shoulder bare chained
to the wall, and evidently expecting something.
Then the big executioner and his assistant could
stalk in with the hot iron, and while the assistant
held the offender the big man could dab the iron
on her. After that there would be a little cloud
of smoke, an odour of burning adventuress (the
property-man could wave an overdone chop in the
wings), a great howl and contortion, and a faint,
the curtain would come down, and the real drama
would begin. The “ Three Musketeers,” with the
branding of Milady done in public to begin with,
and, perhaps, her execution done in public for a
conclusion, should boom greatly in the saltbush
country.
** * *
Ctiarles Holloway’s tremendously explo-
sive. drama, “ A Soldier and a Man, ” is now in its
fourth week at Sydney Lyceum. The theory that
a gentleman who has got down to the lowest
depths of gin and cloves, and is in the last stage of
weeping baby-drunk, can regenerate himself by
disembowelling the casual Zulu appeals so strongly
to the public soul that the performance will rage
for a while longer. At the Opera House “ The
Guv’nor ” takes the place of “ Dr. Bill ” on Satur-
day, and on the same night Orpheus M’Adoo’s
“ Georgia Minstrels and Celebrated Alabama
Cake-Walkers” open at the Palace. And, inas-
much as the present writer doesn’t know what
Sydney wants with five new shows in one night,
he has serious thoughts of dropping the whole lot
and taking to drink.
** * *
At Sydney Royal Standard, Manager West
still supplies wholesome amusement at bed-rock
prices. The little theatre is a boon and a blessing
to the £2 per week man, who can take his wife and
her mother and have three plush-covered front
seats for 3s. The latest production is the ever-
green “ Colleen Bawn ” of Dion Boucicault, and
the ancient J. P. West does that faithful old
scoundrel, Danny Mann, and does him very well.
The Standard audience is composed largely of the
gushing child of nature as he is in the back streets;
it weeps publicly at the pathetic parts, and makes
encouraging remarks to the troubled hero, and
wants to frankly shy its empty lemonade bottle
at the head of Mr. Corrigan, the usurer. The
man who plays the villain to a shilling, sixpenny
and threepenny audience has a hard row to hoe ;
the threepenny part of the house in particular
takes him seriously, and wants his scalp. Still the
little theatre gives good value for the money, and
any person who makes two men smile where
before one got drunk and the other loafed against
a post at a corner is a reformer in his own small
way.
** * *
The attitude at Sydney Tivoli is chiefly
one of patient waiting for certain trick bicycle
performers and a lady whistler booked by the
Moana. Meanwhile, Miss Ida Holbein sooths
the impatience with a good song and nice man-
doljn,. music, and the Leslie Brothers make much
mirth by extracting music from wood, playing de-
lightful tunes on bells and all sorts and conditions
of instruments, and startling the audience with a
revelation of what fearful shapes and sizes the
human mouth can be twisted into.
** * *
Melbourne shows at time of writing are
much the same as last week, and the wintry
managers mutter in a' sad Shakespearian tone,
Heigho ! for the wind and the rain.” At the
however, there was to be a re-appearance
of Peggy Pryde last Monday night, and although
she has already made one farewell speech on the
same premises, the festive little firework will help
to intensify the rush for Cinquevalli’s last nights.
this time is definitely announced to catch
the boat for London on June 27, and Cinquevalli
will probably be off to Sydney meanwhile with
his hat "*nbrella, cannon-ball, potato, washing-
tub, ant "lerous other features of a popularity
which doesn t wane. On Saturday the versatile
juggler introduced some new feats, and the house
shouted at hnn more than ever.
