“ The King’s Musketeer ’ is to clank into
evidence at the Princess’s next Saturday (17th)
according to promise. The list of 30 names on
the programme starts with Cecil Ward, as D’Ar-
tagnan, and ends with Miss Henrietta Watson, as
the branded lady of Dumas’ capacious narrative.
Ine crowd of intermediate characters will include
Plimmer, Gustus Glover, Geo. Majeroni, Haw-
trey, Miss Hughes, Miss Raymond, Miss Mor-
tyne, and other people who are none the less
acceptable for not being new, and who will, any-
way, look rather new in the correctly-detailed
costumes of a time when sassiety used to fight in
the streets. Melbourne is prepared to welcome a
drama that, has blood and warmth in it, and
Ih® King’s Musketeer” sounds very suitable
to the occasion. Also, it will have the ad-
vantage of forming a strong contrast to
Hie forty Thieves,” which finishes on Friday.
I rom Hooley to D’Artagnan is a far cry.
#* * *
Dante has left St. George’s Hall, and the
art of magic, simple and compound, is just now
testing the feeling of suburban constituencies at
sundry town halls. But at the Royal next door,
Bland Holt continues to show “ How London
Lives ’’ in five acts. The beautiful first scene,
with its convincing background and real tank-
water, is worthy of all the praise it gets, but Bland
Holt has so accustomed his * 4 3,2, 1, and six-
penny ” patrons to feasts of spectacular effect,
that they accept one of the finest stage-settings
on Australian record as a casual circumstance.
The truest word in “How London Lives” is
spoken by a low-comedy picnicker, who looks
around at the spot selected for landing the save-
loys and beer, and says— ‘ ‘ This is a, bit of alright. ”
In truth, it is a lovely place for sitting on a bloater
by accident.
*« * *
Melbourne’s present musical taste is wild
and uncertain. Miss Elsie Hall, a most deserving
little lady, has been playing her pianoforte with
very poor cash results, notwithstanding that she
is a duly certificated representative of Native
Talent, with some European honors to her credit.
People who don’t warm to Elsie seem disposed to
work up enthusiasm over Miss Una Bourne. The
newer damsel appeared at Scherek’s Orchestral
Concert at the Town Hall last Saturday afternoon
and played, from memory, the pianoforte part, in
a Schumann concerto. The performance, together
with the golden hair a-hanging down Miss Una's
back, struck a fair-sized audience in its tenderest
feelings, and Melbourne is evidently going to hear
more of this bright young artist. Therefore she
may as well receive an early caution against the
habit of looking at her audience whilst her fingers
are at work, by way of intimating how easy it all
is. This habit is disrespectful to the instrument
which provides the tune. Apart from Miss
Bourne’s success, the Scherek orchestra gave a
charming entertainment.
** * *
Fritz Mueller, an 11-year-old lad who is
going back to his native Germany to develope the
music that is in him, takes his first and only fare-
well benefit at Melb. Town Hall on Saturday
night. Fritz, on his little own, would have been
nearly big enough to fill the building at ss. a head,
and, with one song from Amy Castles thrown in,
the boy-pianist is bound to get as much benefit as
the* hall will hold. A thoughtful, old-fashioned
composer in knickerbockers is Fritz Mueller. He
strikes one as belonging to the rare order of genius
which never feels tempted to go upon the burst,
and his only trouble hereafter will be in taking
care not to die young.
#* * #
The Hellers are playing to fashionable
audiences at Brisbane Opera House, and Brisbane
audiences are seldom fashionable unless they get a
free ticket. The escape of Dreyfus ; and the man
whose beard weeps half-crowns, though personally
he hasn’t the price of a pint about him at the
close of the show ; and the Burnt Woman are
among Heller’s sensations. The lady is tempted
by Mephistopheles who offers her a barrelful of
bank-notes on certain conditions which probably
can’t be fully explained in public, though they
might be written out and shown to a committee
of discreet persons over 60. But. knowing that
the bank has burst, she refuses the notes, where-
upon the fiend puts her on a pedestal and sets her
on fire. She is reduced to ashes, and her tempter
coldly wraps his tail around him and departs.
The Burnt Woman was going well in Brisbane at
latest advices.
*# * *
The cable-news that Ada Crossley has
warbled at Windsor Castle, and Knocked ” the
Prince of Wales and other Royal Familians,
comes close upon the heels of a letter received by
The Bulletin from a perfervid admirer of the
Vic. contralto. The correspondent in question
(an Australian) had just been to a ballad-concert
where Crossley and Clara Butt both appeared, and
his screed was to the effect that Miss C.’s “un-
assuming manner” of singing “Sunshine and
Rain ” (good old S. and R.) shone out against the
“ vulgar stageyism ” of Miss B. Also, the loyal
Australian critic considered that Miss Butt “bel-
lowed ” her songs, and was received with rapture
soMy on aeccmnt of her being English. Which
view of the rivalry between the two contralto ladies
doesnt harmonise with the fact that Melba
achieved her celebrity on the Australian ticket.
X tie tact of the matter seems to be that the 6ft.
Dutt has the more imposing voice and stage pre-
sence, and Crossley is a more finished vocalist in a
somewhat smaller way. The latter Victorian girl
had the bad luck to meet a phenomenal voice in
Clara Butt s, whereas her compatriot, Melba, was
fortunate in getting to the front at a time when
soprano “stars” were not of much magnitude.
T here have been no great successors to Christina
-Nilsson, Tietjens, and the Albani of other days,
and even now Patti, at 56 years of age, is a higher-
priced singer than Melba. As Carlyle Smith
lately told the Argus, “ When estimating the
positions won by Australian artists abroad, we
are occasionally a trifle out in our perspective.”
At Poverty Point.
Wolf : Heard little Fritz Muller play one of his own
compositions t’other nijfht. It was somewhat difficult to
follow, and we fancied we detected unconscious filchings
from the masters; but. much of it was yood stuff, and
we listened with interest. When it was finished Fritz
jumped from his seat. “ What was it—did you know?”
We shook our heads feebly, the youthful trenius regard-
ing us with gentle scorn. “You did not know! Could
you not hear —it was the death of Beesmark.” “ I
thought I heard the funeral inarch in one part," sug 1 -
gested someone. “Aeh!” he waved his small hand
impatiently. “But did you not hear the trouble coming ?
Death fighting for lieem, the noise of the peoples. Bah !”
he smiled, “ I could not make it sound to you as I hear it
in my mind. I will play something you do know.” And
he seated himself and dashed off a smart something,
more on a level with our limited comprehension. Fritz’s
first teacher was a peculiar lady with spiritualistic beliefs.
She assured Fritz, his parents, and others, that Beetho-
ven’s spirit had come to dwell in the lad, and that any
leaching was unnecessary. Beethoven had come to visit
the world again in the chubby person of little Fritz.
Fortunately, Beethoven’s medium was soon set to un-
limited finger exercise and the study of harmony.
“ Constance Clyde ” : Sense of English decorum pro-
bably necessitates that D’Artagnan’s Constance should
be translated from a frisky matron to an innocent
damsel; also (though French literature shrieks at the in-
novation) that the garcon’s intentions, as a matter of
course, should be honorable. Likewise, English chivalry
forbids that the thrilling end of Milady should occur even
behind the scenes. The so-called manliness of the
Britisher revolts against the clubbing together of three
French gentlemen to slay a woman, even though she has
killed the mistress of one of them and done her cheerful
best to kill the three. By-the-bye, a point ! When the
headsman narrates the crimes of Milady the listeners
merely shrink a little when they hear of her stealing the
sacred silverware of the church, and keep their shrieks
till they are told of the murder she committed. Accord-
ing to the sentiment of the age should not the cup-steal-
ing business have formed the climax? It was much more
awful, then, to annex the pots and pans of the church
than merely to kill a man.
“8.5.” ; Because Amy Castles does not shake and
quiver with nervousness on the platform the general
public j’umps to the conclusion that she is too self-
possessed to have the genuine artistic temperament. The
apparent self-possession is due to her strong effort of
will, in the first place, and, after the first notes are sung,
to absorption in the music. Before going on to the plat-
form the young singer is often attacked with severe sick-
ness; and after leaving the Town Hall on the night of her
first concert she seemed almost stupefied—unable to talk
or eat or take part in anything going on around her.
Surely no other term but “nervousness” can be applied
to this.
When Amy Castles was singing in Bendigo, unknown
outside that town, she was reckoned the second best vocal
product of the local Convent of Mercy, Miss Minnie
English, daughter of the local police-sergeant, being the
top-notcher, in Bendigo estimation. What has become
of Miss English vocally is not known, but her friends
should lose no time in getting her on a platform and bill-
ing her as the associate and rival of Amy Castles.
“ Joe ” Castles, father of Amy of that ilk, is head com-
positor of Bendigo Independent— a short, stout, ruddy-
faced Kilmore native of 45, who looks at least 10 years
younger. A smart and willing boxer and a fair all-round
athlete, Joe Castles is one of the most prominent figures
in Bendigo, and was for a time captain of the cricket club
run by the Independent employes. At smoke-nights and
concerts his excellent tenor-voice is invariably on tap.
Mrs. Castles is also deemed something of a singer in the
goldfields city. “Joe” places Amy third among his
three daughters, and predicts that the two younger ones
will metaphorically mop the floor with their big sister
when they get going properly. But who ever yet heard
of a vocalist who had not a younger sister still more
promising ?
Sydney Freeman’s Journal has got the Castles fever
very badly indeed. It straightly insinuates that the
recent cabling of accounts of Melba’s successes are due to
what she has heard of “ her new rival.” Whatever Miss
Castles’ pretensions may be worth, such imbecile drivel
as this can do her no good ; and, as for the preposterous
assumption that The Bulletin cares twopence whether
the young lady is Catholic or Protestant or Cali-
thumpian, it is only necessary to point out that, had
Miss Castles not happened to be of the true believers,
The Bulletin or any other paper might have criticised
her a? it pleased without the ghost of a yelp from
malignant sectarianism.
Dr. Summers (is it Dr. Joe Summers, brother of the
late sculptor?) was running a very mixed sort of musical
entertainment at Perth (W.A.) last month. On the pro-
gramme, among many floridly-described items, there was
“ A Dramatic Scene—The Bush Skeleton.” During this
scene the Town Hall was “in. total darkness, excepting
the stage, on which by limelight the Skeleton appeared
reclining on Sands of Gold, with picks, shovels, &c., at
his feet.” An invisible singer then warbled to the accom-
paniment of a mystic orchestra :
“ Tons of bright metal I proudly won,
Which passed me on—Excelsior 1”
“St. Patrick and the Snakes” was another leading
feature of the show. They do enj’oy themselves at Perth.
The “ Liars” at the Royal is a comedy by Jones.
Which tells about, the way a married lady went astray—
At least she nearly did it—on a pleasant summer day,
By dining with an African philanthropist out late
In a bower beside a river. There were flowers ana silver
plate,
And beneath the gas-globe’s glitter he told of golden
shores
In Africa, where the unmitigated lion roars.
And breathing in her ear he asked with low impassioned
tones
That she would bolt with him, in this same comedy by
Jones.
But a stranger finds them dining there in Jones’s
comed-i/,
The philanthropist is hugging her j’ust as he passes by ;
And the lady in affright makes a scared retreat by night—
She hasn’t any dinner, for she leaves it in her flight;
She persuades some kindly friends to stand by her and
swear
That there wasn’t any dinner, and, besides, she wasn’t
there;
That the bower was all a legend, and the victuals were a
mist,
And there never had been any African philanthropist.
But no one seems to heed them, and obscene birds pick
the bones
Of the lady’s reputation in this comedy of Jones.
And here an inspiration strikes the heroine of Jones.
She j'ust tells things as they be, with no embroidery
Of lie, or frill, or fiction on the little history
How she was half-beguiled by tales of Afric’s storied
shore,
And was half-inclined to go there just to hear the lion
roar.
The dinner is forgiven ; the case is then and there dis
missed ;
No one even stops to kick the African philanthropist;
And the Liars who so nobly stood by their troubled
friend,
She doesn’t even thank them when her troubles reach an
end,
And they probably feel sorry that they listened to the
moans
Of an ungrateful heroine in this comedy by Jones.
As to the story that manager Williamson offered to
educate Miss Amy Castles in Paris for three years on con-
dition that she should play a two years’ engagement with
him thereafter, the facts of the case appear to be that
J.C.VV. never gave a thought to Miss Amy’s vocal possi-
bilities in the future. He merely considered her as a
present Boom, and his proposal was to pay her £6O a
week for one song nightly in “ The Forty Thieves” dnring
the Melbourne season of that pantomime.
A recent Bulletin par., mentioning the names of several
old Australian favorites who appear with Charles Arnold
in “ What Happened to Jones ” (at the Strand Theatre
London), ought to have made special mention of George
Encyl Lewis, who bosses the orchestra. Conductor Lewis
is the Australian native son of Mr. and Mrs. G. B. W
Lewis. Judging from sundry presentations made to him
during the run of “Jones,” he ranks as a considerable
factor in the show’s success. Talking of “Jones,” it is
not likely that Arnold will bring it along to this side of
the world before the first Easter in next century.
A one-time actor, said to have played with G. V.
Brooke, is now' a well-to-do gatherer of bones and bottles
at Footscray (Melb.) He wears his hair long, indulges in
stock Shakespearian quotations, and has a fine dramatic
style of placing the bottles in his sack.
Cinquevalli devoted his first evening in Melbourne to a
careful inspection of Dante’s show, and he liked it much.

and a useful comedian, at Melb. Queen’s Theatre.’
Old-man Capper wrote a vast number of plays, mostly in
five acts. A volume has been published.' He made a
good effort to reach the century, and, as carpenter at
Drury Lane, had known Byron and E. Kean.

Maoriland Steadily keeps up its record m
the matter of shipwrecks. The last case, that of
the Ohau, is the worst since the Wairarapa.
with all hands. The Straits are known to shell-
aC'>? the world over as the blowhole of the
Pacific. It it were not for the numerous shelter-
places along their shores the losses would be still
heavier. The Leeuwin is “not in it” for seas
with the Straits. Thirty years ago the Dunedin
foundered in exactly the same way, with 80 souls
, > rru„ ... a ■
aboard * The Ohau carried no passengers, so its
death-roll is less. Meanwhile the Perthshire is
waddling about the Pacific like a lame duck, with
a broken shaft.and practically no sail power.

Mephan Ferguson, engineer, toiler-maker, etc.,,
of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, has just secured a
contract for the manufacture and supply of all the bolts,
nuts and washers which may be required by the Victorian
railways during the next three years. He has also con-
tracted to supply all the carbonic acid gas necessary for
working the railway refrigerating and ice-making plant—
this is one of I. and E. Hall’s patent carbonic anhydride-
machines, tlje same system as has been adopted by the
Govts, of South Australia, Queensland, and Western Aus-
tralia. Mr. Ferguson has a large stock of these machines
(suitable for meat-preserving co.’s, dairies, butchers, and
others) on hand at present, which he is selling at greatly-
reduced prices.*

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Whenever MacLaurin or Dibbs or any other
fossil of the Cabbage-Tree Mob starts ou tlie question of
Federal finance be always quotes Tasmania as the typical
necessitous province—as the one which depends most on
customs revenue, and the one which will have to be
“assisted” and the one whose painful dependence on
customs makes a heavy Federal tariff imperative. Yet,
curiously enough, it is Queensland, and not Tasmania,
that mostly answers this description among the eastern
mates The following figures on the subject are mostly
taken from Coghlan’l “Statistics of the Seven Colonies”
for 1891-98 N.S.W. Tasmania. Q’nsland.

Therefore, if the three provinces had all reduced
their customs and excise taxation as far as their surplus
would allow, N.S.W. would have required to raise £1 Is.
lOd per head per annum; Tasmania, £1 16s. lid. ;
Queensland, £2 13s. 9d. But if Tasmania had shoved as
much of its expenditure into loan charges as N.S.W., and
if Queensland had charged as little of its expenditure to
loans as N.S.W.—if they had all paid £1 4s. lOd. per head
of their expenditure out of loans as N.S.W. did, and then
reduced their customs and excise taxation as far as they
could while still making ends meet—N.S.W. would have
required to raise £1 Is. lOd. per head per annum in cus-
toms and excise ; Tasmania £1 Is. 6d. (4d. per head per
annum less than N.S.W.) ; and Queensland £3 ts. 7d.
And if these three provinces absolutely gave up borrow-
ing and agreed to pay all public-works expenditure out of
revenue, and made up the shortage with customs and ex-
cise, N.S.W. would need to raise out of customs and ex-
cise’£2 6s. Bd. per head per annum ; Tasmania £2 6s. 4d.;
Queensland £4 12s. sd. Also, incidentally, Victoria, with
no borrowing, would need to raise about £2 6s. And
these results are arrived at despite the fact that the Tas-
manian land-tax is only |d. in the £, against Id. in
N.S.W. Tasmania runs its State Government so very
much more cheaply than any other province that, despite
its smaller resources, it could afford to lend part of its
surplus to the “ necessitous” Cornstalk to keep him afloat.
The Dibbs-MacLaurin push has a mean, inces-
sant, undying grudge against Victoria because it is the
only State which has attempted to rival N.S.W., and the
only one which has grown a city that can overshadow
Sydney. And they regard Tasmania as Victoria’s satel-
lite, and Queensland as their own satellite. This is the
reason why they hold up Tasmania (which, on an abso-
lutely equal basis, tariff and otherwise, would be one of
the most solvent States in the Commonwealth) as the
awful example, while Queensland, which is the worst diffi-
culty among the Eastern States, is hardly mentioned.
Yet even Queensland, as it has all its possibilities of direct
taxation still before it, isn’t a difficulty. In fact, there is
no real financial difficulty anywhere.

The loud Fat Men who say that representa-
tion in the Federal Senate should go by population only
don’t act up to their creed in their own .business dealings.
Take the case of the Australian banks. In the A.J.S. the
man with 10 shares has one vote, and the man with 3000
shares has 33, so he has only one-ninth of the voting
power he should have. Or look at this table :

There is no discrepancy in the Federal Senate representa-
tion which comes within 1000 miles of this. The Fat
Man, when he is running his own business to malee it pay
has no such thing as equal representation. He gives the
biggest proportion of voting power to the smallest
partner—the one with least property.
In Dunedin alone the Maoriland Old-Age Pen-
sion system is saving £3l 16s. 6d. a week (over £l6OO a
year)’in charitable relief. In many cases the Old-Age
Pension costs no more than the charitable relief did. The
difference is that under the old system the recipient was
called a pauper and had to be ’umble. Under the new
one he is a respectable aged citizen, to whom the State is
making some acknowledgment for past services to the
country, and ’umbleness isn’t expected from him.
* * * *
The South Australian Co. —S. A. ’s great absentee
land-holding incubus—made a profit of £29,628 last year.
A 10 per cent, dividend absorbs £28,400, and the other
£1228 is carried to reserve. The great S.A. curse started
business in 1836 by getting a huge grant of S.A. land upon
giving its solemn word as a gentleman that it had no
mercenary purpose, and only proposed out of pure
philanthropy to help distressed and deserving Britishers
to make new homes in S.A. It kept its word so well that
for the last 14 years its dividend has ranged from 9 to 15
per cent., and whereas the paid-up capital is only
£284,000 the assets are valued at about £1,036,000 —an
accumulated profit of over £750,000. And this great
British lie and steal, which obtained its land bv false pre-
tences, complains of the S.A. graduated land-tax as an
outrage and a thing which tends to discourage honest
British enterprise.

In the advertising columns of this issue appears
the prospectus of W. E. Smith, Limited-a co. which is
being formed to take over the great printing and station-
ery business of Mr. VV. E. Smith, Bridge-street, Sydnej.
The business is a long-established one, and the piofits, for
the past nine years, have averaged nearly ' 7(100 a, year
equal to 10 per cent, on the proposed issue of *70,000
capital. Vendor asks only .- 5613 for «ie goodwill-
actually £387 less than the promoters of the limited
liabiiity floating residence co. ask for their mere idea.
The real property, which cost £33,971, is offered to the
co. at £23,971 ; there are no liabilities ; and vendor
guarantees full payment of the book-debts taken over.
Altogether Smith, Limited, looks a sound concern and a
fair investment.*
* * *
Broken Hill municipal council has now entered
into thorough-going opposition to B.H. Water hupp y.
Ltd., and is having surveys made of another available
watershed, feeding a natural reservoir. It is still hoped,
however, that the co. will sell out to Govt. ; but, ei en if
that is arranged, a second reservoir will he agitated tor.
The mines are now quite dependent upon sulphide ore,
and sulphide ore must undergo a preliminary local con-
centration ; and a large supply of water is necessary for
that. When local people and others interested think ot
what would happen if there was (say) a collapse ot the
embankment built across the creek and forming the reser-
voir, they grow uneasy.
* * *
Part of the Broken Hill labor bodies is shriek-
ing with the rest of them that this is a pretty good
Federal Bill, but not good enough—has several defects in
it, and hence should not be accepted. But that argument
has had a bad local knock. Broken Hill now depends
altogether upon the sulphides ; and, so far, they can only
get about 50 per cent, of the silver and /5 per cent, of the
lead out of the ore—the rest goes over the dump. Im-
provements are gradually being made, resulting in a
better recovery ; wherefore the local howlers are being
invited to also agitate for the shutting-down of all the
mines and the mills until the improvements have been
completed. » *’ *
The success of the Continental people in treat-
ing B.H. zinc-ore, followed by the local erection of
machinery to concentrate the material, has already taken
two or three leading Australian metallurgists to the
Patents Office. Hitherto the thing generally aimed at
was a process for leaching out the zinc ; now the expen-
ments are trending towards a mechanical (most often a
magnetic) separation' of the zinc from the gangue, with
the primary object of reducing the bulk for export pur-
poses. Of course, the work cannot be clean, hut it will
serve as a beginning. It is reckoned that there are 700,000
tons of crushed material now dumped on the surface of
B.H. mines, assaying 20 per cent, zinc and over (zinc is
worth E 26 per ton), besides silver and lead. With the
exception of small parcels, all this has hitherto been re-
garded as waste, for the present anyhow.
Horse-haulage and machine-drill work are being
steadily extended in-Broken Hill mines, where the sul-
phide bodies are big and have to be handled wholesale.
Formerly the machine-drills were used only on country
roc k—in driving, crosscutting, and so on. But now the
ore is brought down that way, too. Block 10, which has
just started the new plan, finds that the output per man
has jumped from 1-2 ton per shift (eight hours) to 1’45
ton—a big difference where 300 men are concerned.
. * * *
Once more the perpetually-disputed question of
mine-taxation for municipal purposes bobs up—this time
at Broken Hill. B.H. Proprietary has been paying the
local council about £2OOO a year, but this year (possibly
on account of every one of the last four seats contested
being- collared by Labor aldermen) the co. refuses to pay
anything. There will probably be a fight, though the
Courts will not find much in the Municipalities Act of
N.S.W. to help them decide on what basis mines ought to
be assessed —and that really is the point at issue.
* * * *
The expert who came from London a few
months ago to report on the Gallyinont mine (N.S.W.),
administered its death-blow in reporting there was
nothing to jusl ify a reconstruction. It had none of the
characteristics of a div.-payer. A circular issued to
shareholders says samples taken from the mine were
tampered with, and regrets that general-manager Eissler,
who took a number of samples himself, did not discover
this ; it was remarkable that he also failed to discover
that the reefs, instead of carrying long shoots of ore, were
made up of short shoots with long blanks, of barren
ground between—hence his estimates were wholly
fallacious. The mine is now exploited by tributors, and
though the co. does not expect to derive much income
from the arrangement it thinks there is a possibility of
some fresh discovery being made. Total loss on this
transaction must have been upwards of £lOO,OOO.
* * * *
N.S.W. Mines Minister Cook has been a-going
it Said at the opening of Mr. Garland’s dredge on the
Macquarie River that he often wondered why Victoria,
with only one metal (gold) to mine, was so far ahead
of N.S.W. with its incomparable mineral resources.
Allowing for exceptions, the Vic. mines were worked for
what they were worth as mines. In N.S.W. the mine was
often the' last consideration. The first was to get gold out
of the people’s pockets ; it was all hoodie, boodle, gamble,
gamble, with the result that the mining industry had
been dragged down to insignificance, and had been
made a by-word and a reproach. . It was difficult
to suggest a remedy, but it was to be hoped the
good sense of the community would prevail, and little’
further countenance would be given to palpable boodle-
dum. Amongst other things, Mr. Cook remarked that
over 20,000 acres had been applied for for dredging pur-
poses in N.S.W., hut very few leases had been issued to
date. The business had been worked up into a boom in
which the curse of over-capitalisation has asserted itself.
One co. had a capital of £40,000, and the market-value ot
the shares is nearly double that amount. In another in-
stance shares were quoted at a premium, although the
lease had not been issued. He saw enough to convince
him they were dredging for gold in Sydney more than on
the river flats. City flats, not country flats. *
The biggest shareholder in Great Cobar outside
the lucky tributors is John Downer, Q.C., of Adelaide,
who obstinately refuses to sell out and derides the stereo-
typed “ buyers 705.” of the stock-exchange list. J.D.
believes that the tributor? can sell if they choose for say
£l4 a share and he means to be in at the finish.

Hillgrove (N.S.W.) Eleanora: If the official
tonnage is correct, it has either very tough stone or
tough management. Baker’s Creek mine has splendid
gold in No. 12 level. Reef 9in., country soft. Upper
portion of mine not looking too well, and ore-reserves
rapidly exhausting. Raker’s Creek Consols is adding to
plant and preparing to sink. Mine looking fairly well.— —
Sunlight and West Sunlight about the same—both rather
sick.
• • * •
Gibraltar (N.S.W.) manager Ei.ssler, in his re-
port, placed the mine’s ore-reserves at 21,000 tons, con-
taining 44,3000 z. Actual yield for year showed a little
over loz. per ton, and there is little profit in that, the
vein being small and ground very hard. Altogether, the
' Anglo-Australian co. is having a bad time.
* • • *
Mr. G. B. B. Elliott has given the Arous a
great yarn about the richness of the Inverell (N.S.W.)
diamond-fields, and produces 530 carats of small diamonds
in support of it. He declares that in a short time S.
Africa will be nowhere. It is hard to place faith in any-
one who talks like that. Though the fields have been
known for years, the total diamond-output does not equal
that taken out of one South African mine a month.
• • • *
The nasty knock to the Westralian market last
week was attributed to the trouble in the Transvaal,
Rand stocks having slumped—Westralians followed “ in
sympathy.” Had they boomed it .would certainly have
been said the Transvaal trouble had diverted millions
into the W.A. market. That would have been a more
sensible reason. Anyhow, Dr. Jamieson had more to do
with the creation of the Westralian “ Wild Cat ” boom
four years ago than any other individual, for suspending
speculation in one place meant starting it in another.
Westralians have never been so inflated as in these last
three months, and during that time Rands have been in a
bad way. At time of writing, Westralians were slowly
moving upwards again, but, as sure as night follows day,
so sure is an awful slump coming. Not so much, perhaps,
in the great mines as in the small ones surrounding them.
As usual, N.S.W. will be the greatest victim in proportion
to the number of investors. They are coming in at the
top, which means they will be left in, and are paying
shillings for shares they would not look at at pence.
Not long ago Tuckanarra —a sleepy little mining
camp on the Murchison -provided a sensation in the
shape of a 240 z. per ton crushing from Boyd’s claim, but
since then the Govt, battery has done scarcely anything
except to burst the hopes of other leas“-holders. Two of
the latest crushings : Ross and Guthrie’s claim, 10 tons
for soz. 18dwt. Guthrie’s quartz claim, 11 tons for 3oz.
Bdwt.
* * * *
“ Tom the Ragcr,’ an original character on
Murchison (W.A.) goldfield, has just sold his interest in
one of the Peak Hill leases for £15,000, thereby giving a
hard knock to Darlington Simpson, chairman of the big co.
“ The Rager ” is a thin, wiry old Irishman, minus several
fingers, and gifted with a husky, jerky voice that at times
rises to the majesty of a foghorn. His first appearance
on Australian goldfields dates back as far as the first rush
to Bendigo, ever since which he has “ followed the dig-
gings.” He was one of the first in the swim in N.Q., and
later on turned up at ill-fated Kimberley, and made a
memorable journey across the biggest part of Westralia —
right down to the present Murchison goldfield, accom-
panied only by his dog Paddy. Subsequently Tom got
into one of the richest claims at Peak Hill, and resisted
the blandishments of Darlington Simpson, who had in-
duced all the surrounding leaseholders to transfer then-
claims in consideration of a three months’ option, on terms.
Tom demanded bright shining shekels in exchange for
his verv dotty signature. Simpson laughed him to scorn,
and “The Rager’s” dander rose. So, for nearly two
years, Peak Hill Goldfields, Limited, 250,000 shares (ap-
parently more or .less pooled in London and recently
quoted at £0), have unavailingly tried to deal. The
original offer for Tom’s interest was only £3OOO at three
months, and a share interest.
t m * * *
It would seem that the well-boosted Peak-hill
(W.A.) Goldfields, Ltd. has got into a very tight place
indeed through the failure of the boss to secure “Tom
the Rager's ” interests in the North Star and Polar Star
leases, into which, it is averred, the whole of the aurifer-
ous deposit is. underlying. An effectual “dead-set”
seems to have been made against the co., for it is printed
in W.A. that, “as the other claims to the eastward are
pretty nearly worked out, it becomes imperatively neces-
sary to acquire the interests in question to give the co. an
opportunity of working profitably. The position is ren-
dered still more serious by Mr. Lloyd having purchased on
behalf of English capitalists, who are likely to severely
squeeze the co. In every way it is a pretty kettle of fish,
which must form anything but a delectable dish for those
who have placed their money in the co.’s care. Divs. are
impossible until this matter is squared, and it looks about
as easy at present to square the circle as to square Lloyd
and Go.” In a note to the prospectus the promoters ap-
pear to have set forth that included in the contract of
purchase by the co. were 24 claims or leases, for which
contracts had been concluded except for certain fractions
of said claims, described as “ too small to materially affect
the prospects of the co.” Now it transpires that said
fractions, which have been bought over the co.’s head,
despite its representative’s strenuous exertions to secure
them, “ include five-sixteenths of the. Polar Star and North
Star,’formerly held by Tom O’Meehan, and now the pro-
perty of Mr. Llovd ; three-tenths of the Margaretting and
one-sixth of the Margaretting South, also held by Mr.
Lloyd. The smallness of these interests may be guaged
from the fact that Mr. Lloyd gave £15,000 cash for O’Mee-
han’s interest alone.”

There are so many mishaps, and breakages, and
delays and leakages in connection with Westralian public
batteries that some people are beginning to wonder
whether it is seriously intended that they should be a
success. Men who. ought to know reckon up that in some
cases more is being paid out in attendants’ wages than is
being received in crushing
Lake View Consols, W. A., has had a real boom
crushing. “Over 1) ton of gold” is the heading under
which May output (33,0000 z.) is given in the papers. The
value is set down at £96,000, which is obviously too little,
and apparently applies to the shipped ore alone ; it should
be about £35,000 more. No-allusion to the fact that the
gold from shipped ore is nearly three times as great as
that ireated locally. The latter ore is permanent, and
the other of spasmodic occurrence, so it can hardly be in-
ferred that a ton and a quarter of gold is to represent the
monthly output in future.

An English co. offers to buy the celebrated
Golden Gate mine, Mathinna (Tas.), for £400,000 (equal to
£l2 10s. a share). A controlling interest in the mine,
21,685 shares, is held by the executors of the late W. H.
Brock, whose real estate, apart from his mining interest,
is worth £82,000, and his personal interest another
£12,000. Deceased's will provided that no sale should be
made until his youngest child (now eight) attained
majority, and Tas. Supreme Court doubts whether it can
set aside this provision.

The Emu Bay (Tas.) Co.’s line is now finished as
far as Rosebery where it is to stop awhile until the co.
gets its second" wind. The N. Mt. Lyell Co.’s line and the
projected construction of a line from Strahan to the Big
Lyell’s line terminus have somewhat spoilt the look of
things for the Emu Bay Co. *
The South Tharsis Copper Co., Mt. Lyell,
have lately entered into a contract with the Austral Otis
Engineering Co., South Melbourne, to supply a complete
crushing and concentrating plant for treatment of their
ores, which are mostly low grade. The plant is to be a
very complete one and comprises compound steam-engine
and boiler of 100 horse-power, 3 rock-breakers, 2 sets Roger
rolls, 8 4-compartment eccentric jiggs, 4 improved buddies
and Otis circular vanner, with the necessary trommels,
&c., for sizing and classifying the ore. The plant
has a capacity of 100 tons per day, with provision
for 200 tons. The Otis Co. are also supplying a self-acting
tramway from the mine to the mill: also, a pumping plant
to supply the mill with water from the dam.

Down at the bottom of the Barambogie (Vie.)
miners’strike or lockout—it doesn’t matter much which
it is called—is the old, old trouble of the non-recognition
of mining apprentices. The miner’s is a skilled calling;
and yet in Australia the apprentice system is unknown.
Sometimes a youngster gets some sort of training as a
trucker ; quite as often it is as a full-blown miner that a
man goes down a shaft for the first time in his life. If he
doesn’t own up to his mate then an accident is likely to
happen—and the mate is as likely to be the victim as the
other. The owners know, of course ; and sometimes, as
at Barambogie, they put “ improvers ” on at work need-
ing comparativelydittle skill, offering less than standard
pay for it. And then there is trouble. It should not be
impossible to arrange an apprentice system ; but so far
all suggestions that way have been promptly knocked out.

The vagaries of fruit-growing are illustrated in
a couple of recent Melb. Age items. At Castlemaine
Warden’ Court, June 2, several growers deposed that they
had done well out of fruit crops from waste land taken
up after the diggers had done with it. One obtained
last year from one acre no less than two tons of straw-
berries, which averaged 4£. per lb., equal to £B4 per acre.
Another su'd he had obtained, for 15 years past, from
£lOO to £l5O per acre from fruit. That there is big
money in fruit export is most certain, but a letter in same
paper a day or two previous shows up a very weak spot
in the business, and one that Minister Taverner —in the
intervals of entertaining pseudo-royalty, and swearing
that “ Empiresh all one sholid—hie—homogeneity which
will shtand or fall together ’’—might do worse than attend
to. The writer, a Doncaster (Melb.) fruit-grower, gives
account-sales of some fruit exported by him. One hun-
dred and fifty cases of apples, at various prices, realised
£59 4s. ; dock-dues, insurance, commission, etc., came to
£Blos., leaving £5O 14s. Then came the staggering item
—“ Ocean freight, at 4s. Id. per case, £3O 12s, ud.,” leav-
ing but £2O, out of nearly £6O, for the grower. Seeing
that apples are a good carrying fruit; and that the ship-
ping co’s. are not over-careful of fruit cargoes as a rule,
it is’no wonder the grower prefers the low price ruling
locally to risking his produce on the London market for
the sole profit of the steamer people.
South Australia has only one private line, the
Gienelg railway, and the Kingston Govt, has been for
years trying to' buy it. The co. hangs on, however, so
the State is now resorting to its legal powers of compul-
sory purchase. The Glenelg co.’s little 20-mile monopoly
lies in the suburbs of Adelaide, and the necessity for its
purchase arises from the exorbitant fares charged by the
much-execrated concern. It has a nominal paid-up capi-
tal of £164,500 (£94,000 ordinary and £70,500 preference),
but of this only £117,500 was really paid in cash, the rest
being inflation. For years it has paid 10 per cent, on its
preference capital and'from 3 to 5 per cent, on ordinary
capital, making an average of about 7 per cent, all round.
The State, being content with 3J per cent., could give
considerably reduced fares, but there are legal complica-
tions in the’way of the purchase.

All the Australian Parliaments should give
their early attention to inventing a law to throttle the
ghoulish time-payment monster. Why the person who
sells £lO worth (alleged) of ramshackle tables and chairs,
payable in 20 weekly instalments of 10s., should be
allowed to seize and keep the lot if the last 10s. is a few
days overdue, instead of realising at auction only the half-
sov. still due and absolutely necessary expenses, passes
the comprehension of civilised man.

There was a long delay in the telegraphing
of the Birthday honors. The delay betokened
some hitch at 'Orae-probably differences of
opinion in ’igh circles. The M‘Eacharn knight-
hood most unexpectedly did not arrive. Yet it
made sure of in the proper quarters. The
Melbourne Mayor has been told that it is “ All
right, my boy.”
• • * •
After the knighting of editor Bonython,
of A. Advertiser, and S.M. Herald proprietor
Mister James,’ the hearts of several Australian
dailies were fluttering over the delay in the publica-
tion of this year’s Birthday “ honors.” Which
recalls the anecdote re “ Joe ” Syme— not Jos.—
when he was in the Age office (he sold out
for something like <£150,000, to which he has
considerably added in Mt. Lyell and other specu-
lations). Joe came in one 25th May, and, with a
puzzled face, remarked to the accountant, “Look
here, Rogers, I’ve received a letter from Govt.
House saying they’ve got a knighthood therefor
me. I hardly know what to do about it—surely
the thing must be meant for David ” (his uncle,
now sole proprietor of the Age). Rogers, reckoned
always one of the shrewdest of Melb. men, “fell
in ’ straight away. “No ! ” he yelled, excitedly.
You stick to it yourself. They’ll have to give
David another, if there’s any mistake. Don’t you
give it lip ! ” And, for 24 hours, Rogers went
about his work firmly convinced that Joe Syme
was getting a knighthood on his advice.

He Chief Justice Way’s baronetcy, a point
is to be noticed. Of late years the title-mongers
at the Government offices in London have take,.
to doling out hereditary titles to those who do not
usually come in for such when there is a reasonable
prospect that there will be no heirs. Thus it was
recently remarked, when Mr. Justice Hawkins
retired from the English Bench and was made u
peer, that it Was most unusual to confer a peerage,
on a puisne Judge. Why was it done ? Because
Hawkins was a childless old man. And why a
baronetcy instead of a G.C.M.G. star for Cliief
Justice Way ?

The proposal to call the new C.E. diocese
-in the extreme North “New Australia, ” should
be promptly squashed on many accounts. The
region comprises the tropical belt of land cover-
ing thousands of miles in N.Q. and the N. Terri-
tory, and its main population at present is
Chinese, Japanese, and aboriginal. The term
“New Australia ” bears no application to such a
mixture ; nor does it fit the tract of country or its
characteristics ; and, further, its appropriation is
theftuous. Still more, it adds another to the long
list of geographical blunders whereby two or more
places are made to wear the same label. Why
shouldn’t the new gaiteree simply call himself
Bishop of Carpentaria or Port Darwin ? Primate
Smith sanctions “New Australia,” and is pre-
pared to start the show as soon as a capital of
<£lo,ooo is secured; Collectors are now busy on
the subject in England, and the remaining £4OOO
is expected to roll in speedily.
* * * *
Judges Griffith and Chubb dissented from
the majority of members of Queensland law-
reform commission which recommends that the
death-penalty be no longer inflicted for rape. The
argument generally used to support the retention
of hanging for rape is that it is specially neces-
sary to protect lonely women in the bush. Which
would perhaps be good reasoning but for the fact
that a man might as well be hanged for a sheep
as for a lamb—might as well chance swinging for
the murder of the woman he had outraged. Dead
women tell no tales.
Ihe probability that the recent stealing of
5000 sovs. from the R.M.S. Alameda was long
fore-planned recalls the clever scoop once made
by a gang who took to a S. African diamond-
fields a choice assortment of safes, sold them all,
and, having ostensibly departed, came surrep-
titiously back and cleaned-out said safes by means
of master-keys.
* * * *
Re. the Alameda gold-robbery. It is not
so very difficult to commit a gold-robbery on a
steamer ; the real difficulty is to get the “swag ”
safely ashore. Under present arrangements, the
filthy lucre is kept in an alleged strong-room away
down in the dark innards of the ship, where, if he
can stand the odor of bilge-water and the deceased
rat, there is nothing to prevent the frolicsome
pirate rolling in the treasure. The coin is put
down in that dark, secluded place so as to make
sure that the pirate won’t be disturbed while
breaking in. The keys of this alleged strong-
room are kept by the captain and chief officer,
who either hang them in their cabins or carry them
about in their clothes. Sometimes they arb kept
in a safe, and even then the man who has the key
of the safe must go to sleep sometimes, and once a
man is in a gentle sleep it is easy to assist him
into a deep slumber till an impression of the great,
ungainly, treasure-room key is taken. It is im-
possible to successfully hide a treasure-room key,
as it is always a huge, heavy construction that
is only fit to be humped around in a wheelbarrow.
On the return trip there are at least three people
who have strong-room keys, sometimes four, and
then, as the locks are never by any chance
changed, some day there is a mysterious gold
robbery. Any co. that wants to absolutely pre-
vent gold-robberies should build on deck a strong
steel cage covered by two other strong steel cages
a few square feet larger, the two keys of which
should be kept by the managers at the two
terminal ports; also the locks thereon should be
changed every trip. And an electric-light should
glare right on the treasure from'sunset to sunrise.
This may not absolutely annihilate the gold-
robbery but it will reduce it to such a degree of
rarity that it can be safely provided against as an
“Act. of God.” When the treasure is on deck,
and visible through the bars of the cage, it could
hardly go without being missed. And it would be
about as easy to. steal it as to steal a man’s socks
off him while he is walking.

new departures carried out by merely and ex-
clusively official agencies,” which means that he

thropists another chance of failing as they have
failed before. They fail because Bible isn’t work,

bread, and faith doesn’t spell food—not nowadays,
anyhow. When the bush cleric, who is a profes-
sional believer, can hardly make his own daily
victuals, what chance is there for the purely
amateur believer ?

* * * *
Apropos of the Loch Sloy survivors, it is
curious how helpless shipwrecked sailors often are
when they get ashore. Years ago a ship, bound
for Rockhampton, was lost on Curtis Island, then
a cattle-run. Crew landed safely, and wandered
about. Two post-and-rail fences crossed the
island. The sailors struck both of them more
than once, and got over them. If they had had
sense enough to follow either fence to the slip-
panel, they would have struck the track to the
home-station. As it was, they were found, half-
starved, by a stockman who came across their
tracks.

A curious result of the latter-day rivalry
of S.M. Herald and Telegraph. Neither will
print its second edition (which circulates in the
city) until the cricket cables come out. If they
are late the papers are late. One morning during
the test-match Sydney did not get its paper until
7-30 ! The public blame the news-agents who can
only curse. They deputationised the newspaper
managers, but no modus vivendi can be arrived at.
Yet both journals bemoan the interest taken by
young Australia in athletics to the neglect of
more material things.

Eragedy
FOR THE BULLETIN
BY J. S. S.
Mehitabel de Courcy James
Had several dozen rustic flames.
From early spring to fading fall
She was a sister to them ail;
From fall to spring, through smile and pout,
They squired the maiden week about.
Her conduct’s cause we thus may trace—
The James’s were a mountain race;
And all the summer, sad to tell,
Their boarders squired Mehitabel—
While several dozen mountaineers
Sat round on fences, shedding tears.
Years passed. One eve in early May
The maiden churchwards took her way,
Expectant of the usual man:
Forthwith that once-devoted clan
Began with phrases most profuse
Unitedly to make excuse.
Reggie and Ted were “going out";
Jack suffered from incipient gout;
Ralph had turned Quaker, Mike turned Jew —
Something was queer with all the crew;
Edgar, moi e youthful and more curt,
Spoke out: “Miss James, we loathe a flirt!”
His bitter sneer, so undeserved,
Left the shy maiden quite unnerved;
She faltered, pined, went home to bed—
And in the morning she was dead.
The village mourned—“ Farewell! f arewell!
To much maligned Mehitabel! ’’
Now, every bitter winter’s night
(Most of the rhymes for this are trite)
Along the cemetery posts
Sit several dozen grieving ghosts,
And sneeze remorsefully —“ Farewell!
Mehit—ahit—ahhitabel! ’’

“There are among the Sydney unem-,
ployed scores of men who bluntly refuse to work,
says Premier Reid. That gentle parson, Sam
Marsden, writing to Gov. King 93 years ago,
reported :
This morning- one of the men refused to assist and told
me he would not work. He said he was a free man and
would not work. I immediately ordered him to be
punished with as many lashes as Mr. Arndale thought he
could bear.
Same complaint—but not quite the same cure,
then and now.
* * * *
To Theßulletin from northern N.S.W. :—
There lived here for many years a man who had a
flourishing cabinet-making and undertaking business,
was an alderman, and, altogether, a personage in his
little circle. In burying a Chinaman he was helped by
his son, who, having a sore hand when dealing with the
defunct, contracted leprosy, and was eventually sent to
the lazaret, where he still is, unless happily dead. Ever
since the nature of his disease became known, however,
the townspeople have strictly boycotted his parents with
the idea of driving them from the district. Their fore-
man started a rival business, and took away all the trade,
and even the religious (?) bodies declined to give him
work, although in his prosperity none had been readier
than he to aid the local charities. He and his wife
nevertheless struggled on until, a few months ago, the
climax came. The poor old man contracted blood-poison-
ing, resulting in an ugly sore on a finger, whereupon again
arose the cry of “ Leprosy ! ” driving the unhappy pair at
last away from the town, and now they are in Sydney,
utterly destitute, at the ages, respectively, of 70 and t>s,
while doubtless the Christian community up North con-
tinues cheerfully to employ the local Chinese to do its
washing and hawk its vegetables. Vote for Federation .
Ay ! for only a United Australia, with single voice, can
relieve us from the Plague-bringer.

-“Slab”: Be opium-eater lining clothing
with “seconds” (B. 15/3/’99). Chinese nearly
always have a supply planted on some part
of their body when “run in.” Knew one
Chow while waiting for police (for stabbing
a countryman), make his supply of the drug
into little balls and plant them about dif-
ferent parts of his body. Also, once knew
an aboriginal snatch a £1 note from a white
man in the street, make a bee-line
Chinese garden, invest the lot in ‘ twang, plas-
ter it over the most inconspicuous parts ot his
anatomy, come-back and be locked up.

Twenty years ago N.S.W. had an annual
.record of under 20 divorces. Last year it recorded
245, and promises to exceed this in 1899.
A S.A. commissioner is now in England
spending £lO,OOO under the Elder bequest in
buying pictures for Adelaide Art Gallery. Ap-
parently not one cent is to be spent in Australia.
On top of this comes the rumor that it has been
decided to send to England for a bust of Thomas
Elder, despite the fact that the prize design fell to
a Sydney man. *
Another postal anomaly under provincial-
ism. It costs a half-penny to send a paper from
Silverton to Cockbnrn. But if the paper is
carried on past Cockburn, round by Adelaide and
Melbourne and on to Sydney, the charge is
nothing at all.

A big effort is being made to set up a
foundlings’ home in Melbourne, where there is
much need of strong competition with the common
™ garden baby-dealer. Unfortunately the prowl-
ing prudes are pushing the cause, and the publio
has lost confidence in the stamp of female in whom
charity is merely another form of curiosity.
The female bosses of Melb. Women’s Hospital
wax shrill in their efforts to excuse the labelling of
married and unmarried patients in the maternity
ward, and keep on doing it. When women have
the managing of it, charity uncovers a multitude
of sins.
One authority writing on above subject declares
that women who have no license of their own
make their sins respectable by appealing to desti-
tute widowers ; these latter hire out their wives
marriage-certificates and their names to women
who have pressing need for temporary refuge
under an alias.

Edifying hunk from a recent War Cry :
It is the registration-room that tests the quality of the
converts, and in this instance the results were Uod-glon-
fyinu'. ... A lassie who had backslidden, owing to her
unwillingness to offer for the work, came out heart-
broken. took off her engagement-ring, promised God to
give up her fianci, and interviewed the Commandant
straight away.
Note that this “God-glorifying result” was a
direct outcome of Booth and the Nose their
eloquence. The young man is to be congratulated
who thus escapes being tied-up to so ill-balanced
a being as “the heart-broken lassie,” but what
about the hideous absurdity of representing God
as glorified by such hysterically-perverted sexu-
ality ? Truly “ God ” —the God of Booth and the
Nose— does “ move in a mysterious way ! ”
From Melb. Age report of a Wesleyan globe-
trotter’s sermon :
“ For a minute or two the preacher wandered from his
subject, and, as he expressed it, ‘dreamed with his eyes
open.' . . . ‘ When I get to heaven, if my wife and chil-
dren are here I will come back and have a look at them ;
yes I’ll give one of the guardian angels a holiday, he
remarked, as though musing. ‘ And I’ll see James and
John up there,’ he continued, ‘ and I’ll say—“ How did
you fellows come to go to sleep when the transfiguration
took place ? ’ ”
This pitiable drivel actually appeared alongside
the foreign cable news in Victoria’s most widely-
circulated paper !

There is a Salvation Army “ Home'” at
Wellington, M.L.- most large towns are cursed
with a Salvarmy Home —and there the poor or
repentant woman is taken in and set to work
washing. .And for washing, starching, and ironmg
a s hirt the holy sweaters give her one penny. The
hardest and meanest private sinner generally gives
2d., and is called a fiend of iniquity for doing it,
but the Harmy gives half the sum and gets up a
reputation for charity thereon. Truly the Big
Drum covers a multitude of sin and oppression.
Maoriland law, by the way, actually exempts the
Salvation sweating dens from the operation oi the
Factories Act on the ground that they are “charit-
able institutions ”1! !
There are many curious ways of getting
killed. At Wellington (M.L.), some workmen
were blasting rock to make room for the
foundations of a new house. One
blast went off with extra energy,
and a small bit of rock went
through the bathroom window
of an adjacent house. A
young woman was standing
up in the bath, and the
rock knocked her senseless,
whereupon she fell down
in the water and pro-
ceeded to drown eom-
f ortably. Somebody
fished her out just in
time, and she is re-
covering from the
drowning, but is suf-
fering from skull-
fracture.
A reliable indica-
tion of returning pros-
perity in Melb. is the
long lines of cabs that
are to be found waiting
every early morning at
the top end of Lonsdale-
street where the local
Flossie has her beer and
habitation. A year or two
ago times were so hard
that the locality was as
moral as a church choir;
but things seem to have had
an upward tendency since,
and the writer, who passes
the spot on his way from the
office at two o’clock every morn-
ing, has watched the gradual ex-
pansion of the trade in this
neighborhood with a genial
altruism.

When a German man-o’-wars-man was
recently fined £5 for striking a Sydney policeman
a representative of the German Consulate applied
for a reduction of the penalty on the ground that
the man’s pay was only about Is. a month. At
this rate it would take over 8 years to pay the £o.
Poor Deutscher ! A fine being severe in propor-
tion to the finee’s means, 10s. would probably
meet the case.

A REFUTATION.
(Being a correction of the singular error of one Thomas
Moore).
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
O ! his brain was obscured in a casual eclipse
That bard who could warble: “ How sweet to recline,
And to know that I sighed upon innocent lips
That had never been sighed on by any but mine.”
For the joy of the chase—it has long been confessed—
Is the ardour that comes of a perilous run ;
It’s the risk and the rivalry give it a zest
And lend us a laurel when danger is done.
Why—it’s nothing to know that we’ve captured the Fair
With the ruby- red lips and the Juno-like eyes,
Unless there are other gallants, we’re aware,
Who would venture a neck in the noose for the prize.
For the sweetest of joys that a fond lover sips
In the arms of a maiden, all tender and true,
Is to know,—“ as he sighs on her innocent lips ”
There ore those — if they could —who would sigh on
them too !
Brisbane.
P. LUFTIG.

voice will be transferred to Europe for artistic train-

graceful curves and flowing outlines of the female
form divine. A pair of P.D.’s cultivates the figure

A dog-show offers a deliberate challenge to
many accepted feminine conventions. The
woman who is somebody out there is not the
woman who gets her frocks from Paris and her
husbands anywhere; nor the woman who leads
society with a string of diamonds; nor the woman
whose ancestors came over to Sydney with the
conquerors. Out there the only woman who is
somebody is the pale little insignificant, in a rusty
hat, who puts her hand fearlessly into a dog-ca<re
and pats her dog on the head. And the woman
who is envied by every other woman is the woman
whose hand is licked humbly by a red-eyed
mastiff. And the woman with an unimpeachable
social status is the woman who is on calling terms
with the most dogs. Every dog has his day. Also
some women have their day at a dog show.
• A f*other fete that His Ex. has promised to open
m this same Exhibition Building is the Con-
sumptive Home Bazaar. August 10th is the date
ot the opening ceremony, which promises to be
unique. The usual internal disturbances have
followed the long succession of meetings re this
Bazaar, but things seem to be straightening out
now. A fine amusement committee includes all
our leading theatrical men, and, by the joint con-
ftont of the committee itself, no women.
i'b® Press Bazaar thinks it now sees what
it is going to do. The introduction of a business-
like artist into the circle has helped to clear away
a little of the fog that always wrapped this section
like a garment. Feasible suggestions now fly
round. But I am sorry to say the Chinese
Hekald has backslidden.
An orphanage that deserves infinite considera-
tion is the Waitara Home for Foundlings a bir-
red cottage in the pure air of the North’ Shore
Line, where the little ones are tenderly cared for
if the orphanage is less discreet than those con-
tinental places with a crib on the porch that
moves automatically into the house after a child
has been placed in it, and allows the mother to
leave unobserved, it is only because its funds
are limited and its size. On all hands the
careful working of the institution is hiMfiv
commended. To help the funds, Mrs. Tom
Hughes, Miss O’Brien, Mrs. Slattery and other
ladies worked up a little dance at Paddington
Town Hall last Wednesday. Although a rush of
other attractions ensnared people who would other-
wise have gone, there .was a fair attendance, and
a better sale of tickets. Lady Darley and a party
came on later from Mrs. Hotham’s theatricals.
It was for this charity that some clever little
Chatswood girls worked up a dolly bazaar lately.
I heir charity eventuated in the front garden, and
was a pretty, childish spectacle, with all its little
girl buyers and little girl sellers. The takings of
the bazaar totted up £7.

My second visit to Friedenthal’s concerts found
a house packed to the doors. Up in the gallery
half-a-dozen women crossed their knees under
great books of music, and followed the “ Moon-
light Sonata * note by note. They gave away the
impression that they played that sonata them-
selves. But it is hard to imagine what impres-
sions they took in. Moreover, Friedenthal is an
artist to,listen to, not to follow in a music-book
He has a way of making a pause last his pleasure'
and is not barbarously orthodox in the matter of
chords. At this concert, he played Schumann’s
™ Va1 ’ tbe bauntin g Chopin “Prelude in D.
■tlat, and three Grieg pieoes, and delighted
everyone with his particularly limpid touch. Of
course he used a Bechstein—a magnificent s'peci-
men—as his tour is in connection with the Bech-
stein houses.

Mrs. Hotham’s theatricals drew a heavily-
dressed house to the Palace on Wednesday. The
actors were mainly well-known society people
who knew their parts and faced the footlights with
great nerve. The only drawback was that the
women elocuted chiefly on the edge of their top
notes, mistaking shrillness for distinctness. What
a rare thing is the native speaking-voice that can
steer between guttural and shriek when required
to carry ! Mrs. Hotham and Miss Elsie Austin
threw themselves into the play-“ The School-

The Season’s music is beginning to flow. First
comes “ Hommage k Beauchamp ” (Nicholson and
Co.), a downright, unmistakable title, anyway
that doesn’t pretend to be named after Lord Beau-
champ accidentally. F. R. Wynne-Jones is the
composer of this brilliant red and white valse
which is almost as glowingly colored within as
without. It will, probably, be one of the leading
waltzes of the season. Despite the awfulness of
the title, it deserves to lead, for the sway-
ing seductive movement is delightful to dance to,
and there are also snappy movements that will
lend themselves to the new hoppy style of waltz-
ing practiced by H.E., to whom the homage and
the waltz are due. “A Coon’s Sweetheart”
rather an ordinary song, by the same composer
comes from Paling and Co. Lottie Moore has
scored big successes with this ditty.
At such a time, when all music should be in-
spired by Beauchamp or Federation, George E.
Vincent’s “ Alma Polka Mazurka ” (Paling and
Co.) seems like an anachronism, and the fact 5 that
it is a polka-mazurka adds to the seeming. For
those who dance mazurkas this may be a good one
but who does dance them ? No self-respecting
programme confesses to anything more frivolous
than a polka. From Paling and Co. also comes a
new song, “The'Golden'City,” by A. C. Brillat,
which distinguishes itself from other Golden
Cities by mentioning that its walls are Phantasy.
All the others known to musical people affect to
be built of real heavenly gold. This is rather a
good song with a rippling accompaniment, cross-
hands and a distinct melody. Glen and Co. send
me another new song—“ First Love,” by G. S. de
Chanta!’ already popularised by Amy Castles in
Melbourne. A picture of Amy in a sailor blouse
and her hair down, and a firm backhand signa-
ture, embellish the cover of the otherwise cheap
little song.
In “Sons of Britannia” from Paling and Co.
composed by John H. Nicholson, all the injunc-
tions fitting to the cause—Help ye the Helpless,

There was a nice little tea-party at the new Art
Depot last week, with the Misses Wyndham and
Woodward as hostesses. This is the newly-started
depot, you will remember, that sells women’s work
on a commission basis. I saw some lovely specimens
of work scattered round—all kinds of handicraft,
trimmed hats, paintings, afternoon tea-cloths,'
waiting to be sold. The party was chiefly girlish
—Miss Grace Noble, Miss See, Miss Slattery,
MissTolly Hordern, and others. Louise Hepner,
the Belle of New York, who was one of the guests,
revealed a broad but fetching ’Murkan accent as
she discoursed in a sprightly way of the awful
ii ln ? S we sa .y * n Australia. The company gener-
ally had an idea that it was the other way, but
some of our slang, as translated by Miss Hepner
into ordinary American meanings, must hit
Americans almost as hard as their slang hits us.
Unfortunately, I can’t give examples (they are too
broad for narrow print like this) but nobody who
listened to the gay conversationalist will ever
forget what not to say to a Yankee.
A band of devoted Sydney women have leagued
together to help the Federal cause. Singularly
enough, they are all wives of ardent Federalists :
Ihe Mayoress, in the chair; Mrs. Barton, Mrs
Wise, Mrs. John See, and Mrs. Meeks, vice-
presidents ; Mrs. Herbert Harris, treasurer. The
league proposes to work for Federation in a quiet,
subterranean way; a sensible scheme, and much
more liable to assist than a campaign of women
speechmers.

I saw a simple but very effective cape at the
roughs, the other night. It reached to the
knees, and was built upon seven flounces of sea-
blue moonlit cashmere on a dark mauve silk
mmg. Many rows of silk machine-stitching
trimmed the flounces finely, a high collar stood
out to show little overlapping lace frills within
and a long scarf to the feet of blue mousseline de
soie studied with tiny mock amethysts, finished
this dream up delightfully.

Writes my Melbourne correspondent: —
“Governor Brassey now has four aides—Lord
Hl ? aV IP ’ L °M d Rlchard Nevill, Mr. Pakenham,
and Mr. Cecil and the result is a shocking waste
of energy—the only thing that ever gets wasted at
Grovernment House. The gallant four buzz about
like flies on a widow-pane, apparently not knowing
what to do, where to go, or how to manage any-
thing. Lord Nevill is fairly useful, of course, for
the wan identity has had experience, and is not
without tact, but the others seem to spend most
of their tune running to the 1 old hand ’ in search
of instructions. Everybody is agreed that it was
a mistake to let Capt. Wallington retire from the
service. The placid, unruffled calm of that diplo-
matic person, his waxen official smile, and his
way of saying non-committal nothings in the most
satisfying manner in the world were the makings
of Lord Hopetoun’s success. Capt. W., if allowed
a tree hand, might even have popularised the pre-
sent reign.
“Lord Hindlip is a pale, narrow-shouldered,
unkissable-looking young man, who appears to feel
lost for the present. However, a lord of any kind
is pretty sure to grow out of that in the course of
time.

from the outer darkness of surrounding ‘ shires ’

balls and sich.
“Due in Melbourne shortly are the Earl and
Countess of Huntingdon. The earl is only 31. He
belongs to one of the real old lordling families—
his title dating from 1529, so that if you stick a
pin into him blood should trickle out of the color
of Reckitt s blue. His family is chiefly notable
however, because one of the women of it founded
the Countess of Huntingdon connection, a reli-
gious body still somewhat numerous in England.
He and his wife are simply coming on a holiday
jaunt, and not m order to attach themselves to
any gubernatorial push.
“A gilded johnny, with a pronounced weakness
for the smiles of ‘ other fellows’ girls, ’ is said to
have met his match lately in a smart young
matron, one of the very Naicest, and a relative
of an Australian Governor. The bachelor
admirer begged permission to present his new
goddess with a souvenir—some little trifle in
the way of jewellery—and she was graciously
pleased to intimate that he might send it along.
Johnny rushed off to the jeweller, and, having
selected a liberal assortment of costly articles for
the lady to choose from, sent them along with his
card. Next day he received a charming little
note, thanking him for his lovely presents. The
grateful recipient had chosen to keep the lot.
“ Mount Morganaire D’Arcy and his bride (ex-
Mrs. Nutting, nee Boucicault) are to live in the
London residence formerly occupied by Georgians*
Countess of Dudley.
“Some dogs have their day. When the new
Mrs. D’Arcy’s dead-and-gone-pa edited Rock-
hampton Argus, the family were as poor as the
family of the typical editor, or even poorer; but
even at the lowest tide they kept a stiff upper lip,
ignoring most of their fellow-townsfolk as no-
bodies, though Mr. D’Arcy and his first wife (then
Rockhampton residents) and the Archers were
amongst the few favored exceptions. A northern
fruiterer who grumbled at a daughter of the B.
clan for propping her Trilby on the ledge of his
shop-window while she leisurely tied her shoe-
lace, was told haughtily that had she chosen
to use his head as a footstool, he should have
considered himself highly honored. Later on,
when Melbourne’s chief dressmaker tried to bounce
Mrs. Nutting into paying for a race-gown of
faulty fit, the doughty debtor let the case go into
court, drew tears from magisterial eyes by dis-
playing her dear little waist in the hateful em-
brace of a wrinkled bodice—and won her point.
“That day defendant’s mother, Mrs. Bouci-
cault, was in court—the stateliest of old ladies, and
m her day a Sydney belle. She was sister of Charles
Clarke, a byegone Bananaland gold commissioner,
still represented in the northern province by his
descendants, the Ted Shaws of Diamantina Gates.
Soon after his marriage, editor Boucicault took
his wife ’Ome to see if her beauty and his theatri-
cal connections could help her to a stage success.
The devoted pair soon came back to Australia.
To the end of his life poor old A. L. 8., of the
velveteen jacket and Bedford cords, maintained
(1) that not one of his four more-or-less dashing
daughters could hold a candle to her mother; (2)
his eldest girl had refused three governors; (3) he
himself would have died rich, had he been born
north of the Tweed.

function, girl-graduates, as usual, looked less
beautiful than brainy. The handsomest lady
student ever entered at Melb. ’Varsity is said to
have been Miss Katie Renniek, M.A. Her Arts
course finished, Miss Renniek accepted an appoint-
ment at Brisbane Girls’ Grammar School and went
north, where she met and married a lawyer.
“Private letters from Paris say that Mme.
Marchesi—who, by the way, is looking very frail
—thinks highly of Regina Nagel’s voice, and says
that she has a great future before her. Australia
will soon become proverbial as a recruiting-ground
for singers for ’Ome.
“Miss Spence, the proportional-votist, has been
Spending some time in Melbourne. Her lectures
are good and brainy, but it is wonderful how she
to drag proportional voting into every-
thing- She could not speak on the Babylonian
Captivity, or the Pyramids, without finishing up
with something about electoral reform. At her
lecture on ‘ The Social Atmosphere and Crime ’
before an audience of women, the dear old lady
made everybody sit up by an allusion to the
recollections of ‘ the man I live with in Adelaide ’;
but before elevated eyebrows had had time to dis-
arrange the set of bonnets, she added, ‘ who is
eighty years of age. ’ That, of course, made all the
difference. The recollections of the old gentle-
man merely concerned bull-baiting and cock-
fighting in England.
Ihe cacchinative bachelor Chief-Secretary is
at last adapting himself to the usages of Society
and the dress of the Gilded. His Apollo collar is
a nightmare of the past, so also is the farmer’s
broad Sunday tie. The one and only Peacock
now takes on fine plumage, wears a frock suit,
‘ Geisha ’ tie, and all. There surely must be a
woman in the case.

A Sydney newspaper rejected a proffered
artiole, the other day, detailing a list of the re-
versed judgments of an occupant of the Supreme
Court Bench. The paper feared attachment for
contempt.
The story goes that a more-or-less recently-
bebaubled Australian, whom most people took to
be a bachelor, was really a widower. He was, it
is said, married many years ago, and now has a
son at an English university.
Mr. GEO. GORDON, “the Firm’s” scenic artist,
who died on Monday at Melbourne, as the result
of an accident.
E. P. Nesbit slowly recovers his practice
at the S.A. Bar, and no doubt will get a big
shove-on by the warm praise which that good
judge of defences, Boucaut, lately bestowed on his
handling of a local will-case.
Premier Reid says he months ago recom-
mended Mayor Harris to Gov. Hampden for a
title. That is all right, but what about three
other would-be notabilities who were similarly re-
commended but have got “no forrader ” ?
Capt. Russell, leader of M.L. Tory party,
at a Thames meeting: “ Ladies and gentlemen,
where does the Premier’s surplus come from?
Ladies and gentlemen, it comes out of your own
breeclies’-pockets.” Then the men guffawed, and
150 ladies fried in their own blushes.
Ben Tillett and Randall, Queensland im-
migration touts, should be quarrelsome bedfellows
in London. Randall’s bogeys are Socialism and
Radicalism. Not so long ago he publicly an-
nounced that Queensland doesn’t want any immi-
grant who would read Reynolds’s Newspaper.
Chief-Justice Way’s baronetcy comes to
him after at least 10 years’ waiting. It is stated
that his ambition is to receive a baxony and a seat
in the Lords. Way is, however, in very delicate
health as a result of his recent serious illness, and
he would probably be satisfied with the Federal
Chief-J usticeship.
That brainy lawyer, John Dennistoun
Wood, who, 40 years ago, was Vic. Minister of
Justice, has just been elected a municipal coun-
cillor at Both well, Tas. He is now very, very old,
but there was a time ! Away in the sixties, he
whaled C. E. Jones for writing about him in Col-
lingwood Observer, and paid the £lO fine like a
man.
Tasmanian Premier Braddon says that
whatever fight he has in him (at a pinch he is as
full of fight as a cartload of wild cats) he got from
his mother, who was a Limerick woman and the
lineal descendant of one of the women who carried
ammunition to Sarsfield at the siege, and threw
bricks from the wall-tops at Ginkell and his crowd.
This month the Premier is 70 years of age.
Ex-District Court Judge Dowling, who is
80 vears of age this year, arrived in N.S.W. with
his” father (Chief Justice Dowling) 71 years ago.
He has been 53 years at the Bar, and just 50 years
ago was made Judge Advocate General at Port
Curtis by Mr. Gladstone. Judge Dowling was one
of the last to see Sir John Franklin in the flesh.
He was in London when Franklin left for the icy
North, and he exchanged greetings as the navi-
gator’s ship moved off.
Melb. Age is very severe upon the late
James Service for his economy even in death
The ex-Minister, whose estate far exceeded
.£200,000, left nothing to public charity, and gave
special directions to his executors that any sur-
plus provisions which happened to be in his house
at the time of his death should be retained in the
family circle. ,
J. F. A. Rawlinson, Gov. Gormanston s
secretary (who was educated by Card. Newman at
his Birmingham school), is now touring Australia,
and putting up at the Govt. Houses in turn, dur-
ing the absence on leave of his chief. Mr. Raw-
linson has sporting proclivities. He rented
Chokebore, and ran him in last Hobart Cup, but
the brute didn’t win.
The fine philosophical leaders (with a tinge
of religion in them) which the Melb. Age has
been 'publishing for a few weeks on Saturdays,
are from the pen of Alfred Deakm. What with
H. H. Champion’s biting epigrammatic style, and
DeakinV fine, nervous English, the Age leading
columns are sometimes as brilliant as anything in
modern daily journalism.
Chief Secretary Peacock told a Melb.
Pleasant Sunday Afternoon crowd how, in his
youthful days, he gave up studying at the Uni-
versity because his father had got into financial
difficulties, and toiled as a grocer’s assistant from
7a m. till as late as midnight on Saturday. Pea-
cock didn’t spoil the yarn by adding— m ordinary
play-actor style—that he sat down upon his basket,
one cold winter’s night, and vowed to pass a Shops
and Factories Act if ever he got into Parliament.
At John Norton’s recent Gosford meeting
his matter and manner —to the surprise of his
hearers —savored so much of the pulpit that at the
conclusion of his address instead of applauding
the audience waited with bated breath and bowed
heads, and a still, small voice was heard— Pro-
nounce the benediction, brother I “i a
newly-made father made his way to the platform
and paralysed King Jackyby asking when he could
make it convenient to call round and christen the
babe !
J. W. Hackett, M.P., barrister and news-
paper proprietor, of Perth (W.A.), first settled in
Melbourne. He landed from Dublin 25 years ago,
and brought letters to old J udge Molesworth,
before whom he appeared a few times ; but he
failed to prosper at the Vic. Bar. The University
befriended him better. But, when Westralia
shook off the Crown shackles, John W. went
there and took up land. The Hacketts were
big people in the -Irish Church before Gladstone
knocked it on the head.

“ H.E.M.” : Was at Govt. House (British
Papua) one night when Gov. Macgregor was tell-
ing a yarn about a prisoner (a murderer), who
jumped out of a boat in which he was being
taken to the s.s. Merrie England. The cor-
poral’s cartridge jammed in his rifle, and he
could neither draw it out nor shove it in, so
he (Gov. Mac.) had to fire at the escaping
prisoner himself. “And did you hit him,
sir?” asked Captain C.—-—. The old
Governor gave the captain a look of
withering scorn, and replied, “ I neevar
meosed a mon at ten yards in me life.”
Ex-Mayor W. Smith, who died
at Collingwood the other day, was a
partner in building and contracting
with Charles .Tardine Don, the first
laborist who got a seat in Vic. As-
sembly, in the early sixties. Don was
the member who said he represented
the “purloins of Collingwood,” and
who declared he’d “drive the squat-
ters across the Murray with their
own stockwhips.” When Don died (a
widower), Smith kept and reared his
young ohildren. Afterwards, they
were sent home to relatives in Scot-
land. In the meantime, landlord
Snadden, at the Buck’s Head, Fitz-
rov. took charge of a valuable gold
watch which had been presented to C. J.
Don by political admirers. When Don s
eldest son came of age he revisited Victoria,
and his father’s watch was handed to him at the
Buck’s Head by Presbyterian Minister B. Hamil-
ton, in the presence of the Smiths, Snaddens,
Kidneys, and a select company of Scots and
whisky-bottles.
From an Adelaide paper :
Mr O A. Oox, representing London capitalists, has
taken up 340 sq. miles of country near Charlotte YVaters,
with the object of establishing a goat farm. The skins of
thp animals are to be used in glove-making.
Twenty-five years ago “Charley” Cox was law-
reporter for Melb. Age, but eventually, conclud-
ing there was “ no money ” in the press, set up as
a skin-merchant. He went on and did well, and
became a well-known “city man’’-and now he
is to be a king over 340 sq. miles of territory with
a dense population of nannies and billies.

REV. HORSLEY: “ This horse you sold me, Mr. Brown, as perfectly sound, is indisposed in his
front legs—he was on his knees three times yesterday. ”
MR. BROWN: “ Yes; ’e’s what you call a praying ’orse. I saved him up for you on that
