Just prior to the elevation of a certain
lawyer to the bench —from which he was forced,
by newspaper reports of proceedings in his court,
to resign—he was seen in one of the corridors of
the court buildings surrounded by a number of
criers and messengers, who were being invited to
feel the solidity of the calf of his leg. Still the
Crown Law authorities, in their stupidity, wanted
to make him a Judge.
De Bomford, who was incarcerated 20
years ago for shooting his sister-in-law in Tas-
mania, has just been released. After his arrest
he appeared mad—found some feathers in his cell,
and, at inspection time, was found blowing them
about in the air ; the jury brought in a “ Queen s
pleasure ” verdict. His friends were passing rich.
For 20 years he has had private apartments at
the invalid depot near Hobart, where tie received
male and female visitors in a magnificent wine,
cake-and-cigarette style. Told, as evidence of his
temper, that once, many years ago, the bailms
seized his live stock, among them a favorite pony.
To block the seizure he shot the pony dead, lhe
bailiffs then left, suddenly.
“ Yarra Yarra,” one of the finest stations
in Riverina, was first taken up and stocked by
Father Therry, the first Arch-Priest of Sydney
and founder of St. Mary’s Cathedral. Bather
Therry knew the points of good cattle, and his
brands were a guarantee
in their day. He sold
the station for .£20,000,
most of which cash he
put into the building of
old St. Mary’s.

clined a birthday honor.” What sort of honor

That ex-Melb. Metropolitan Fire Brigade’s
official is said to be living in luxury on the
Brigade’s money in Honolulu. Now that Ha,wau
is part of the U.S. the fugitive can be extradited,
and sundry detectives and law-officers are urging
the Brigade to “put up the stuff.” Govt, is
willingto go halves in the expense, but the Brigade
remembers the bill for extraditing Butler and
shakes its head, and prefers to let the free and
glorious U.S.A. keep the dear departed.
The other day Vic. J ustice Hood turned
up on the bench with a peculiar kind of wig, a
sort of cross between sheep-skin and horse-hide ;
not unlike the front part of Will Whitburn’s dog a
dress in the De Rougemont skit. It seems that
Hood’s pet cockatoo or prize pup got hold of his
wig and breakfasted off it. Judge’s wigs are
not made in Victoria and, consequently, Hood,
J., is forced to appear in a temporary struc-
ture while the real thing goes to England for alter-
ations and repair.
That fluent brewer Nicholas Fitzgerald has
sat in the Vic. House of 48 Rich Landlords and
obstructed democratic legislation for 28 years.
He is now the oldest-established obstructionist in
the Chamber, and hopes were entertained that his
long continuance in one place, and the number of
things he had helped to prevent, and the solid
manner in which he hadn’t done anything, would
have been recognised by his gracious sovereign
last month. But, somewhow, Nicholas the Beer-
monger was overlooked.

Take the dooty aff the mate fer th’ poor warrikin

Memories of a sensational Adelaide insur-
ance conspiracy case, just upon 20 years old, were
revived t’other day by the death of one of the
principals—a most popular professional man who
stepped out of prison into a carriage provided by
his then wealthy father-in-law, and so was taken
back to his old practice. In the couple of years
or so the practice had pretty well gone to pieces;
but he battled most pluckily, never quitting the
suburb where it all happened, and deserved some-
thing better than to die while still under 50.
“ M‘G.” : “ Steele Rudd’s” “ Dad,” who
had not been to town for 40 years, is scarcely less
enterprising than old Pat Nihill, a West. Melb.
milk-vendor, who died a few years back. Pat.
arrived in Melb. in the goldfields days, when all
the population was gathered at the west end, and
from that day until his demise in ’92, he never
went south of Bourke-street or east of Elizabeth-
street, except to cross the latter thoroughfare on
Sundays on his way to St. Francis’ church.
Nihill lived through the days of the Exhibition
without knowing more of it than the shadowy
dome a mile away, and Australia outside the
bounds of West Melb. was a sealed book to him.
The recent reported offer of theM.L. Chief-
Justiceship to solicitor H. D. Bell, stirs again the
old wish of certain Sydney solicitors to have their
branch remembered in appointments to the
N.S.W. District and Supreme Court Bench. On
no equitable ground is the present N.S.W. system
of limiting such appointments to members of the
Bar intelligible. A solicitor of a few years’ stand-
ing may at present drop the lower and claim
admission to the upper branch ; the barrister may
similarly change his clothes and his outlook—thus
practically demonstrating that one profession is
as good as the other. Yet a barrister may be
called on any day to act as a Judge, whereas a
solicitor may not. The anomaly is sufficiently
patent to call for removal without controversy.
But all the same, it stays. “It stays,” said a
cadaverous, horse-haired individual the other day,
“ because of the disreputable characters of most
of the six-and-eightpennies. The judgment-seat
must be kept above suspicion.” There may be
something in that.


Medical item
Dear Bulletin. —Can’t you suggest some simple remedy
for our doleful friend, “ Disconsolate Tabitha” (or Sydney
D.T.)? The principal symptoms of her complaint seem
to be(1) A Pilcher meeting with overwhelming vote
against the Bill. (2) A Heydon-Dibbs meeting and unani-
mous vote against the Bill. (3) A MacLaurin meeting
most extraordinary and enthusiastic. (4) A Carruthers
meeting disclosing a scene of wild disorder and interrup-
tion. (5) A Barton-Reid meeting, wherein the demons
of rage and despair are let loose. I fancy it is a compli-
cated case of blind staggers and nightmare, aggravated
by intermittent housemaid’s knee.
Of the many applications of Science to every-
day life which are now looked upon as necessary to com-
fort and enjoyment, probably there is none which claims
a greater share of attention than that which is called
“the black art”—to wit, photography. The use? to
which it is put in commerce alone are so varied that it is
well nigh impossible to find the trade, art or profession
which is not in some way under obligations to the camera
and lens. The number of people who follow photography
either as a business or a pastime is ever on the increase,
while to meet their wants is the constant aim of quite an
army of inventors, specialists and dealers. Occupying a
prominent position among such is the well-known firm of
this city, Harrington and Co., Ltd., whose particular aim
is to supply the thousand and one articles which are at
some time or other required by photographers. Their
stock contains everything new, interesting and useful in
the photographic trade, and should be inspected by any-
one who thinks of commencing this interesting pursuit,
as well as by the experienced worker who is up to the
numerous wrinkles in vogue amongst the “ knights of the
camera.”*

PUBLIC NOTICE.
The Bulletin proposes to drop the weary subject
of Federation so far as next issue is concerned.
Correspondents are hereby informed that Federa-
tion paragraphs sent in next week won’t be used.
This paper goes to press on Tuesday evening, just
before the result of the Referendum will be
known, and owing to its habit of coming out in
three capitals on the same day, it doesn’t reach
the public eye till Thursday morning. Therefore,
at time of going to press next week the staff won’t
know whether to whoop over a great victory, or
to explain that the defeat isn’t really so bad as it
looks, and (as J. Want did at last Referendum)
that the fact of the biggest vote being on your
side really proves that the people are on our side.
The Bulletin has an impression that Premier
Reid fixed the Referendum for a Tuesday, instead
of the much more convenient Saturday, out of
premeditated malice, and for the express purpose
of making The Bulletin “feel bad.”
A Musical Prodigy.
Master Fritz Garl Muller, whose likeness is
here printed, was born at Goerlitz, in Prussia,
and left Germany when two years old, since liv-
ing in Melbourne—for the last nine years. He has
developed into a fine, strong-looking lad, with an
artist’s passion for music. He is the son of Frie-
drich Ferdinand Muller, of Kew ; is 11 years o’d,
and has a brother (aged 8) and a sister of 10. His
first idea of music came to him from the Salvarmy
band, when he was four years old. Following in
the footsteps of his elders, he started a boys’
Salvation Army, the members of which he
called together by aid of drum ; and to these
he delivered short addresses or sang German
songs, frequently attracting large crowds, who
listened to the gifted youngster with great inte-
rest. But all this was dropped when serious
study began to take up his time, and his marvel-
lous talent for music really demanded careful
training. Already it is a rare treat to listen to
this little lad as he plays difficult pieces and
charming compositions of his own, really making
the piano speak. He has a beautiful, long-fingered,
singularly flexible hand, which gives a firm grasp
and yet can play the lightest passages most deli-
cately. Fritz is the pupil of Miss Burkett, of
Collins-street, Melb., and he certainly does her
teaching very great credit. There is a movement
on foot to get young Muller to Europe, so that he
may study under leading masters. For that
reason it is proposed that he should give concerts,
and, if possible, thus raise the needed funds.
Despite partial rains in various localities,
Western N.S. W. as a whole is still groaning at the
feet of ruthless King Drought. One squatter has
lost 35,000 sheep out of 50,000.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. 11. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems. *

Aboriginalities.
The luck of it. Teetulpa is an abandoned
o. Aus. diggings 100 miles from everywhere. A
fossicker poking about in a creek there lit his
pipe, and threw the still-burning match behind
som ebody came along and took
him 200 miles to a hospital. The match had
dropped, in the vast wilderness, right on top of a
keg of powder, thrown away as damaged and use-
less six years ago, and since buried level with the
surface by drifting sand.

Good old Blanky has lately been on its
trial at ’ome. Recently at Blackburn (Lancashire)
Police-court a man who had used “ blanky ” was
charged with obscene language. Counsel for
defence denied that the word was obscene, and
argued that it was merely a corruption of the
anoient oath “By’rLady.” This phrase, counsel
averred, had long lost its original meaning, and
had become the adjective generally used by the
lower classes to express either admiration or eon-
_ The Bench declined to accept this view
fTfy inflicted a fine. Hope still for Australia—
“My by’rlady oath ! ”
* * * •
Perkins was the new manager sent up to
Yancomindah Station by the mortgage co. Pie
was a good accountant ; and they did say he had
once seen sheep being driven to market. He was
going to exercise economy—that was his first,
second, and third instruction ; also the last. A
sheep was killed ;he would take the leg—the rest
could go to the hut. The second leg was nearly
finished ; and, as he had notified that no beast
was to be slaughtered without his permission, it
was intimated that another sheep must die.
“Why?” “ Out of mutton !” “Eh? I’ve only
had two legs—who’s had the others ? ” And then
it was explained that the breed out there was
peculiar ; owing to the drought, or something, the
sheep, though still four-footed, produced only a
couple of legs of mutton. And now Perkins has
written the directors advising that a few sound
wethers be got to bring the flocks up to the old
standard.

“ A.A.A.” : “Ferdinand’s” experience
with the grasshopper in the horse’s ear (B. 13/5/’99)
recalls an oM northern horse-dealer’s trick, that
has enabled him to sell many a pronounced jibber
as a first-class “harness.” Just previous to starting
him off he would place a very long pin, head-down-
wards, in the animal’s ear, and, before the horse
would have become used to the irritation and could
remember to jib, the trial would be satisfactorily
over. Never known to fail, and is safer than the
grasshopper dodge.
• * . *
“W.” : When a horse falls, turns sulky,
and, as is often the case, will not try to get up,
chew up a piece of paper and ram it into his ear.
Then he will scramble up for a certainty. Never
knew it to fail.

“ Wal.” : The Straits mutton-birds lay but
one egg, bub will keep on laying if the nesb is
cleared. No doubt a merciful dispensation of
.Providence, for if each female mutton-bird laid
and hatched a clutch of eggs, there would be no
room for anything else to live on this earth.

_I n the Furneaux Group, where the mutton-
birds come from, they tell a story of a strong-
armed widow settleress who, with her daughter,
killed 1700 snakes in a year on one small island.
The serpents were wiped out to make it safe to
run sheep on the islet.

“ Dabton ” : A certain Western under-
taker is a perfect ghoul. He haunts the local
hospital waiting for the demise of persons de-
spaired of; and when one of these disappoints
him he considers himself personally aggrieved.
In one instance, having heard that the patient
would not last two hours, he hied him to the
clergyman to snap up the burying job. The latter
rebuked him for his callousness, but the “ planter”
nevertheless presented himself at the hospital as
clerically authorised, and arranged the funeral
for 3 next day. Meanwhile the man died, and
the clergyman gave the job to another under-
taker, who arranged to bury at 11, forenoon; so
that when No. 1 turned up with his hearse there
was no corpse, and the disappointed one, after
“performing” a bit, was fain to drown his
chagrin in whisky.
* * * *
“U.”: Some bushwhackers don’t trouble
about trifles. Was once talking to one in Gipps-
land, when he stooped, picked a. handful of bloated
ground-leeches out of his socks, casually threw
them away, and went on talking in entire in-
difference to a huge swamp-fly boring at the base
of his nose. When I drew his attention to it, he
shot out his under-lip and blew upwards, making
the fly merely transfer operations to his neck.
Knew another that used to slumber peacefully in
a tent that swarmed with soldier-ants with bills
on them like tomtits.
* • * *
Yarrum : Re snake-sounds :
One night, at Bungonia, was talking to publican when
we heard a clear and prolonged squeak. Publican said it
was made by snake. Took a lantern and a waddy, and
we went down paddock. Hadn’t gone far before we heard
squeak again. Companion held up the light, looked
round, and presently found a big brown snake up against
a stump. Settled him, went on past stump, and almost
walked upon female snake coming towards us. Settled
her, and publican explained that the snake at the stump
had been calling for mate. He had often thus killed a
pair, he said. Sound was very like the shriek of ’possum
being worried by dogs.

This hails from Cloncurry and is vouched
for by the local organ as true. Northern man sent
his blackboy to creek for water. Nigger returned
in great fright, saying, “Me been see-em big
fellow man longa waterhole.” Boss went and
discovered carpet snake 20ft. long; slew him;
disembowelled him ; and found a five-foot kanga-
roo inside. Now, ye southern Munchausens !

“N.N.8.”: The aboriginal stone axes
mentioned by “ J.A.” (B. 27/5/'99) are probably
made of diorite, which need not be a local stone,
as blacks who couldn’t get suitable material
locally used to barter with certain tribes that were
practically manufacturers, such as the Darkenung
tribe (Hawkesbury-Wollombi, N.S.W.). They had
a large quarry, called a “ mogo ” (word means
“ stone axe ”) beyond the Wollombi. Their axes,
however, were made of basalt, and, through bar-
tering, have been found hundreds of miles inland.
“Tommy J.P.” : Re swaggie, fly and
box of matches (8., 20/5/’99). Once saw an
old German exhibiting an educated blowfly
in Davoren’s P. of W. Hotel, Grenfell,
N.S.W. Charged Is. each to see the show.
Blowfly had a few dabs of paint on its tmdy
by way of uniform. German impaled'fly
on large pin stuck through piece of cork,
and as it twisted its feet in its agony he
gave it a wee straw musket, with which it
used to shoulder arms, port arms, present
arms, in the most ludicrous fashion. Then
he gave it a pair of tiny dumb-bells (straws
with knobs of cork), and it did physical
drill. The secret was that the orders
given cleverly coincided with the tortured
insect’s movements. I have never looked a
blowfly in the face since.

“ G.E.A.”: Bob and I were riding
home one moonlight night in the “ coupl-
ing” season, when snakes travel around
after dark, and presently saw a big fellow
stretched across the track, and got down to
demolish him. Bob was “fit,” and took
risks. He jumped on the reptile, and they
got badly mixed. Then there was a yell—
lie had been bitten ! Got a match and found
wound on his shin —two distinct punctures,
and we hastened back to town, where he
was “treated.” Before dinner-time the
next day the doctor pronounced the patient
“out of danger,” and we left for home once
more. Found the defunct “ snake ” hanging
over a rail. It was an eel! Then I ex-
amined Bob’s only spur, and found but
two darts on the rowel. Bob asked me, “as
a favor,” to keep it quiet, and I hereby do
so !
• • * *
A pathetic spectacle in drought-
stricken N.S.W. districts is the horse that
is not reckoned worth feeding, or sending
away—the waster, or the honest old chap
who has worked hard for years and is at
last broken down and useless. The unfor-
tunate animal exists for a time on. dead
leaves, bark, &c., and at last becomes so
weak that it knows if it lies down at night
it won’t have strength to rise again ; so the
wretched creature keeps on its legs until
sheer exhaustion pulls it down—and then
the crows !
“ Paul ” : There is a Papuan snake which
whistles when disturbed or when going to bite.
Knew a Govt, official who was out shooting with
a Papuan when they heard this warning whistle,
and while they were looking up into the trees,
thinking it was a bird, the boy was bitten on the
foot, and died in 30min. This snake is black,
with white belly and white rings near tail.
Whistle represents “ hiss ” of ordinary snake.
* * « *
“ F.P.” : Any reader ever seen a “ cus-
cus ” (Papuan ’possum)? Looks like cross be-
tween native bear and a ’possum ; color, white
with yellow bands on shoulders and hind-quarters.
Large round eyes, and “ring tail.” Easily
tamed.

What is the record journey for’a parson on
a marrying expedition? Without having any
data to go upon, present writer reckons that Rev.
Burton, of Norseman, ought to be in the running.
A couple of couples rusticating at Balladonia,
which is alleged to be somewhere in the Great
Cat Country between Norseman and Eucla,
couldn’t wait any longer, so Burton sprinted
across on his bike—lso miles of desert, mostly
sand. His machine broke down 14 miles from
Balladonia, and he got tired of pushing it; other-
wise, after opening the doors of the Balladonian
paradise, he had intended running across to
Eucla to see if there was anything in his line of
business there.

Thus aB. correspondent: Lately, in far-
western N.S.W., where the drought still lies
heavy, sat down on a once-almost-famous sheep-
station to a dinner of goat. Boss explained after-
wards that the mutton hadn’t come up—he was
buying from the next station. So far as I was
concerned/it didn’t matter; one ; has to be less
hungry than one generally is out here to tell the
difference between leg of goat and leg of sheep,
especially when you don’t expect the latter to
carry any fat.

Some Obvious Facts.
Q , LABOR LEADER DAWSON .
Dear Bulletin. — I have been reading the
“obvious facts ” of Sydney Daily Telegraph,
and desire, in return, to present a little set of
niy own :
(1) Clause 62 of the Commonwealth Bill provides that in
the event of Federation Sydney Telegraph shall have its
telegrams from the Federal capital franked FREE, whilst
all ot her Australian papers shall be charged DOUBLE
RATES. . , ,
(2) It .is provided by clause 105 of the Bill that under
Federation the duty on Queensland sugar shall be trebled
and a bonus of £lOO shall be paid to the Colonial Sugar
Refininsr Co. for every ton of sugar made at its mills. In
arriving at this result, Dr. MacLaurin’s methods of com-
putation have (teen followed.
(3) It is provided in the Commonwealth Bill (see clause
78) that the first Ministry of the Commonwealth shall be
selected only from Doddering Idiots, Chronic Liars, Effete
Politicians, and Narrow-minded Provincialists. This
gives the anti-Bill party AN ABSOLUTE MONOPOLY.
(4) The population of Australia at the last census was
7 343 6364, of which 6,326,4321 were men, 486 were old
women, and the balance of 1,426,893)4 consisted of Dr.
MacLaurin’s widow. In the event of Federation not
being carried, the increased cost per head will be £7 2s.
6d., and the population will either become deceased or
HAVE TO LIVE ON SAUSAGES MADE OUT OF GEORGE PILE’S
Dog. Therefore, vote FOR the Bill.
(5) Coghlan’s statistics show that during 1898 the
Savings Bank surplus dwindled down to £154 3s. Bd. In
1892 the surplus was 1.1,432,346. (See Jones on “ How to
Cook Figures,” p.p. 17,143-4.) This clearly proves that
Dibbs either forgot to lock the safe when he went down
the street to curse Federation, or that the Telegraph
editor added up the figures. The obvious moral is VOIE
FOR FEDERATION. . .
What do the anli-Billites say in reply to these obvious
facts? They DARE NOT ANSWER THEM 1-Dorge
Gibbs.

Jock was a Geordie,
and after his recruit-
drill found himself do-
ing guard-duty for the
first time. Tired of
tramping to and fro, he
was standing easy when
an old gentleman in a

Pilcher, Q.C., warns the people of N.S.W.
that Federation will bring interprovincial Free-
trade, and that “will allow Victoria to swamp
us.” The unfortunate barrister is three years
behind the time. In 1896 N.S.W. abolished its
duties on almost everything which Victoria pro-
duces, and allowed that province to swamp N.S.
Wales as much as it liked. And now Federation
will abolish Victoria’s border duties, and allow
N.S.W. to swamp Victoria a little in return.
Pilcher has his facts upside-down, that’s all.
VOTE !
Uncord your votes, men, every one—
The unit for the Union !
A fine, vigorous lie from Sydney Telegraph
the paper which will lose £5OOO a year in
extra telegraphic expenses, to say nothing of
what it may be out of pocket through the pos-
sible abolition of free newspaper postage, if
the present Bill, or any other Bill which doesn t
fix the Federal capital in Sydney, is carried
Amongst the arguments of the organ of the Blank-
jheque Party we find the following “ Even if all the six
colonies join the Federation, the two populous ones, New
South Wales and Victoria, will have about two-thirds of
the votes in the popular Chamber.” This is a deception.
The two States would have more than twice the popula-
tion of the other four, but would require five more votes
to <dve them twice the voting power, even in the popular
Chamber. But in the Veto Chamber they would have a,
clear two to one against them. At an eventual joint
sitting, with 674 per cent, of the total population, they
would hold under 64£ per cent, of the voting power, and
the “ absolute majority ” clause would step in and block
them completely. — Telegraph, 10th June.
There will be 110 votes at a joint sitting. An
absolute majority—s 6 votes, or anything over
50 per cent, of the voting power —will carry
the day. And the £5OOO a year paper solemnly
tells its readers that N.S.\V . and Victoria, with
per cent, of the voting power (59 votes out
of 110) will be defeated, and that the other four
provinces, with per cent, of the voting
power (51 votes out of 110) will win. In other
words that 51 votes are more that 59, and
per cent, is more than 54i| per cent. Is the
Telegraph a raving lunatic, or what? Even
its arithmetic about the lower House is wrong.
N.S.W. and Victoria will have 47 votes out of
74. They only want two more votes to make a
two-thirds majority.

Dear Bulletin. —He this Federal capital
question, there are several cases in point. The
United States commenced business with its Con-
gress at New York, and afterwards removed it to
the partially-built new city of Washington. This
proves, I suppose, that if the Australian Parlia-
ment first meets at Melbourne it can’t possibly
remove to a new city in N.S.W. The Maonland
capital used to be Auckland, but it was removed
to Wellington ; this also proves that you can t get
a Parliament to change its location, and if the
Commonwealth Legislature once sits m Mel-
bourne nothing can shift it. The English capital
has shifted from Winchester to London; and the
Parliament of Upper Canada used to hang out at
Niagara, though, grievous to relate, it changed
its quarters later on ; and that of Lower Canada
shifted in 1849 from Montreal to Quebec, ihese
and other painful episodes will doubtless show you
that when once a Parliament or a Government
is located comfortably anywhere (in Melbourne or
elsewhere) it is ridiculous to suppose that it will
remove. —T.S.
It is amazing how little sense it takes to
make an anti-Federal M.L.A. Witness this re-
mark of Frank Cotton in his recent Disumonist
speech at Camperdown :
The first Federal Parliament would be elected on the
existing unequal State franchise, and he had no doubt that
South Australia would obtain a very unfair advantage by
reason of the women’s franchise there doubling the number
of voters.
And it is such a simple proposition, after all.
Each province is allotted a certain number of re-
presentatives according to its population—men,
women, and children. At the first I ederal elec-
tion each State retains its present franchise. S.A.
will have seven representatives if it decides to re-
strict its franchise to men with over £lO,OOO a
year and there are only two dozen voters in the
CABBAGE-TREE JACK ON HIS TRAVELS .
whole province. It will have seven representatives
if it gives all men a vote, or if it gives all men
and all women a vote. And if it goes further, and
extends its franchise to children, infants, dogs,
goats, pigs, iguanas and cockatoos, still it will
have just seven representatives. Yet a presum-
ably sane man like Cotton, also a hundred other
anti-Federalists, can’t understand a little thing
like that.
Dr. MacLaurin, in a recent speech on the
blessings of disunion, asked why N.S.W. should
federate with a poor broken-up place like las-
mania, that had an accumulated deficit of £350,000.
And an angry voice comes from the island re-
marking that Dr. MacLaurin is an ignorant and
blatant person ; that his figures are two years old ;
that Tasmania’s shortage is now down to £12o,UOO;
and that, seeing that Dr. Mae’s own province has
a deficit of .£2,soo,ooo—twice as much per head
as Tasmania’s—he doesn’t know what he is talk-
ing about.
Auckland Herald’s corres-
pondent in Samoa indulged in some
tall writing about the war. In his
latest letter he describes a visit to
the plantation where one of the
scrimmages took place. After de-
scribing his emotions on viewing
the spot where so many heroes fell,
he goes on to observe that patches
of their pants were still visible on
the barbed-wire fence through which
they had—well —retreated.
Hobart possesses a harmless
old citizeness who imagines herself
Queen of Europe, Asia, America,
and their d pendencies ; and at-
tends all outdoor society and State
functions bedecked with red gloves,
fancy scarves, brass chains, and
Brummagem gimcracks of all sorts.
For years the old dame has been
marching round giving orders to all
and sundry, and posing as Direc-
tress-General of the Universe, but
nobody in Hobart ever says her
nay. The other day when the Gor-
manston carriage arrived at the
wharf with the departing Vice-
regal party it was met on the out-
skirts of the crowd by Premier
Braddon and one or two high offi-
cials. Lady Gormanston alighted,
greeted the officials, then slipped
round to the back of the carriage,
where the Queen of All Things
stood waiting in awful majesty,
shook the old lady’s hand, enquired
what she could do for her among
the kings and queens of Europe,
and wished her a kindly farewell.
E. J. Brady makes his cavil
at Newbolt clearer —
Though Drake was buried in a leaden
coffin, I cannot really believe Newbolt ig-
norant of a well-known historical fact
when he swings the gallant admiral to
his rest in a shotted hammock in Nombre
de Dios Bay. “ Drake’s Drum ”is writ-
ten in the Devonshire dialect, and seems
intended as a simple Plymouth lad’s song of the great
captain. It is in keeping, then, with Newbolt s treatment
that the unlettered singer should speak of Drake as
'down atween the round shot, “ waiting in his hammock
till the Great Armada comes.’’ In any case, I should not
like to say that the quality of the verses in question was
lessened by the veritable dictum of even a dozen reliable
authorities. I regard an offence against Art as more
culpable than a poetio breaoh of fact, or perhaps morals.
Newbolt, or any other writer, is “ welcome to knock holes
in mv verses,” but it must all be but as musketry-fire to
the cannon-shot with which (I believe in common with
most writers) I can always perforate my own work.
Instead of condemning Newbolt for melodiously burying
Drake in a hammock, instead of his leaden comn, I
should, with kindred feeling, be inclined for an extension
of poetio license sufficient to permit his being buried in a
hollow log, providing always that the poetic intermen
were artistic and decently metrical.
The party which asks N.S.W. in general
to reject Federation because the Bill doesn’t pro-
vide for the Federal capital being in Sydney is
really addressing the people outside Sydney thus.
The address has never been put m proper form
before, but it is time that it should be :
Fellow countrymen : There are about 670,000 of you
outside the 100-mile radius, and the Federal capital is to
be planted among you somewhere. You are requested bo
say that you are utterly unfit to have the capital among
you Also that you won’t federate unless Australia in
general recognises that you are too low and mean and
infected to have the oapital anywhere near you. You are
JACK WINT AND THE OTHER SPHINX.
urged to strike for the glorious privilege of being declared
infected, and-to insist on your unfitness and general
degradation being recognised. The community which
won't strike in defence of its own right to be kicked, and
won't maintain the priceless privilege of being fired out,
is lost Fellow-countrymen, the other provinces have
declared that you are fit and worthy people to have the
capital of all Australia among you. You will have an
opportunity, on June 20, to declare, by voting against
the Bill, that you are not. You will have an opportunity
to say with emphasis that you are so degraded that you
oan’t be trusted with a capital (tremendous cheering).
Therefore rise up and demand that you be uisqual ] lied,
and defend your incapacity to the last (deafening
applause).
Secret-Caucus Labor members Holman
and Hughes are still trying to persuade them-
selves and the N.S.W. public that the alleged
‘ ‘ Secret Conference Bill ” is undemocratic. Should
that Bill be defeated, these gentlemen ought to
combine with Messrs. M‘Launn and Pilcher to
make the Constitution of N.S. W. half as demo-
cratic as the one they are opposing.

The loss of some of its more effective argu-
ments does not hurt the Cabbage-tree Mob nearly
so much as the defection of nearly all its best
speakers. Ashton is, perhaps, chief, and his elo-
quent and trenchant addresses are converting
many of those whom he helped to influence last
year. At Parramatta, last week, speaking on Dr.
MacLaurin’s 225. 6d. per head and the law
average, he remarked that the doctor reminded
him of the expert who was called in at an inquest
on a man found dead in a 7ft* hole in a shallow
lake. The witness laughed at the idea that death
was caused by drowning—“for,” said he, “the
average depth of the sheet of water is only 2ft.
That the Bill will pass is grudgingly ad-
mitted by the anti-Bill leaders, but they rely on a
faint ray of hope that the majority will be small
enough to justify them again upholding minority
rule. Jawbone Neild, who was so abusive over
the “ reasonable ” in the rivers clause, at his Pad-
dington meeting, replied diplomatically, but for-
getfully, to a query that he would support the Bui
in the Assembly if it obtained a reasonable majo-
rity at the Referendum. The audience thereupon
severely guyed the majah (now colonel), and,
driven into a corner, he confessed that, if the Bill
was successful by 10,000, it would be his duty to
assist it in its passage towards Royal assent.

Prophet “ Zadkiel ” kills Queen Victoria
in 1899, and passes the crown to Wales for the
next nine years.
Glassey, M.L.A., after years of more or less
futile effort as leader of Q. Labor-party, has been
deposed and Dawson elected in his stead. The new
leader will probably not come quite up to expecta-
tions—few leaders do. Dawson is an Australian
native with a fair share of political and other
knowledge ; a ready, pungent and fluent
speaker, who knows the value of silence, and, if he
doesn’t fall asleep too often in times of crises, will
do material damage to the Boodle phalanx. He is
a specialist in mining, has dabbled in journalism,
and is neither teetotaller nor pietist. It will be in-
teresting to watch how Glassey, who has so long
led the orchestra, will now handle the big drum.

The Australian.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Once more this Autumn-earth is ripe,
Parturient of another type.
While with the Past old nations merge
Uis foot is on the Future’s verge ;
rhey_watch him, as they huddle pent,
Striding a spacious continent,
Above the level desert’s marge
Looming in his aloofness large.
No flower with fragile sweetness graced
A lank weed wrestling with the waste.
Pallid of face and gaunt of limb,
Ihe sweetness withered out of him.
Sombre, indomitable, wan,
The juices dried, the glad youth gone.
A little weary from his birth ;
His laugh the spectre of a mirth.
Bitter beneath a bitter sky,
To Nature he has no reply.
Wanton, perhaps, and cruel. Yes,
Is not his sun more merciless ?
Joy his such niggard dole to give,
He laughs, a child, glad just to live.
So drab and neutral is his day
He gleans a splendour in the grey.
And from his life’s monotony
He lifts a subtle melody.
When earth so poor a banquet makes
His pleasures at a gulp he takes.
The feast is his to the last crumb;
Drink while he can, the drought will come.
His heart a sudden tropic flower,
He loves and loathes within an hour.
Yet you who by the pools abide,
Judge not the man who swerves aside.
He sees beyond your hazy fears ;
He roads the desert of the years.
Rearing his cities in the sand,
He builds where even God has banned.
With green a continent he crowns,
And stars a wilderness with towns.
His gyves of steel the great plain wears;
With roads the distances he snares.
A child given a world for toy,
To build a nation, or destroy.
His childish features frozen stern,
A nation’s task he has to learn.
From feeble tribes to federate
One splendid, peace-encompassed State.
What if there be no goal to reach ?
Tne road lies open, dawns beseech !
Enough that he lay down his load
A little further on the road.
So, toward undreamt-of destinies
He slouches down the centuries !
N.S.W.
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.

Influenza and Colds.
Nothing so suddenly obstructs the perspiration as sudden transitions
from heat to cold. Heat rarifies the blood, quickens the circulation, and
increases the perspiration ; but when these are suddenly checked the con-
sequences must be bad. The most common cause of disease is obstructed
perspiration, or what commonly goes by the name of catching cold. Cough..,
colds, sore throat, &c., if attended to m time are easily subdued, but, if
allowed to run their own course, generally prove the forerunner ofmore
dangerous diseases. Nine-tenths of the consumptives date their affliction
from a neglected cold, and the diseases that are caused by wet feet damp
clothes, or exposure are more numerous than are generally supposed. One
of the most efficacious medicines for the prevention of diseases of the throat
and lungs is BILE BEA.NS.
Constipation.
This is a disease which may be looked upon as the mainspring of more
than half the ills which afflict mankind. It consists in an irregular and
insufficient action of the bowels, and its commonness is remarkable, especi-
ally among women. Though there are other causes of constipation the
principal one is an irregularity of habit in attending to the laws of nature.
Insufficient liver action will also produce it. Constipation is not !° e *'
tremely difficult to cure as many people imagine. A few doses of Bile
Beans taken as directed, will effectually remove the present trouble, and,
if strict regularity of habit be joined to a course of the Beans, constipa-
tion will not, except in very occasional eases, be found to recur. Lt
member regularity is absolutely necessary to good health, and, while BILE
BEANS will be found a great help and will cure most obstinate cases of
constipation, they cannot overcome the effects of wilful neglect.

Softgoods Sargood, M.L.C., of Melbourne,
beamed recently on the people of Perth, W.A.,
and had the gall to tell them, among other good
advice and copy-book headlines:
They had a duty of their own to perform, and that was
to go to the poll. He had been engaged in politics, and
he had noticed the apathy of the electors in the other
colonies. He asked them, in justice to themselves and
their members, to be sure and record their votes.
In Sargood’s own province the people have been
going to the poll in vast numbers for years to
support every Govt, which declared for Oiie-
man-one-vote, and on Sargood’s recommen-
dation Sargood’s House of 48 Rich
Landlords has refused the popular
demand every time. But the iron-
ical big tailor tells them it is
their duty to keep on going
to the poll just the same !
As for the apathy,
however, Sargood is
right. A less-apa-
thetic province
would have

S.A. Parliament meets June 22, and King-
ston’s following now promises to be as solid as in
last Assembly. Oppositionists are at loggerheads
over the leadership, the rival candidates being
Downer and Darling. Downer has never com-
manded solid Tory support, and the chances are
in favor of his being supplanted by his younger
rival.
V. L. Solomon displaces Downer as leader
of S.A. Opposition. John Darling, jun., who
would have been a certainty, declined the position
for private reasons. The personally-popular Solo-
mon is a good debater, and the only Oppositionist
anything like a match for Kingston in repartee.
He is the fourth successive leader elected by the
S.A. Tories since Kingston’s accession to power
m ’93.

Queensland labor-leader Dawson was one
of those who demanded that there should be a
minimum affirmative vote of 25,000 before Queens-
land accepted Federation. So the Northern pro-
vince saw the amazing spectacle of Premier
Dickson, head of the Boodle Ministry, fighting’
for majority rule, and the Labor-leader doing his
utmost against it. Dickson came out on top, and
for the first time The Bulletin’s sympathies are
unreservedly with the land-shark Cabinet.
The anti-Federal Frank Cotton thinks
It would be more judicious to see how far they could
go with the machinery of the Federal Council which
could easily accomplish 37 out of the 39 matters that this
13ill was designed to perform.
Curious, this sudden desire of so many
bogus Democrats to entrust large powers
to the Federal Council, where each province
has equal representation (not in one' House
only but in the whole Council, for it is all
one House), and these representatives are not
even elected by the people but are appointed by
the Ministries. Theanti-Federalistalways admires
every kind of Federation except the one available
at the moment. This is the very first time the
Cotton crowd had a kind word to say for the poor
dilapidated Council.

April Chambers Journal, in an article on
England’s last territory-steal—the Kowloon Penin-
sula, near Hongkong—remarks that “ our recently-
acquired fellow-subjects . . . make a living where
a white man would starve . . . and may yet do
good in Australia.” J. Bull can’t forget his Aus-
traha-as-a-rubbish-tip tradition.
Australian banking laws are wailing in-
valids calling for the Federal physician. Pro-
vincial treatment makes them worse and worse.
Some provinces make the notes a first charge on
u n £ ass^s > others don’t. No two provinces
charge the same note-tax—some charge none at
all. Queensland has taken over the bank note
issue and made it a State affair. Victoria has a
law which requires that certain important' par-
ticulars shall be shown in the published balance-
sheets, but no other province has followed its wise
example. The note for which was paid £1 in the
province of issue is only worth 19s. Gd. across the
border. All these things will be made uniform
under Federation. “ One nation, one flag, one
destiny is only half the story. “ One bank-note,
one postage-stamp, one postal-note” is another
feature well worth remembering.
WEAKENING TO THE INTELLECT.
JIM (to Bill): What the dooce is wrong with
Jack to-night?"
BILL : "'E's drinkin’ soft stuff, an’ ’e can’t argue
a hit.”
Q. Premier Dickson, in making his Federal
Enabling Bill a non-party question—and thus in-
citing every form of opposition—manifested both
apathy and cowardice : apathy for the cause of
i ederation and fear of his three anti-Federal
colleagues, Barlow, Gray and Chataway. Dick-
son fears to tamper with his Cabinet in ease the
thing falls in on him. An intelligent and spiney
premier would have gleefully seized the chance of
getting rid of the three dead-weights named.
That bogus Liberal, Groom, M.L.A., it is
wired, “maintains the right of Queensland Par-
liament to amend the Federal Constitution Bill.”
Of course it, or any other Parliament, has a right
to amend the Federal Constitution Bill as much
as it pleases. Nobody disputes that. The only
trouble is that when it has amended the Bill there
will be no Federation; the only way there can be
a J ederation is by all the provinces accepting
the same Bill. Queensland will have to amend
Groom.
Premier Kingston definitely announces
that, if S. A. Leg. Council again rejects the House-
hold buffrage Bill, Parliament will be immediately
dissolved. This comes as a surprise to Tories, who
have repeatedly taunted the Govt, with being
afraid to again appeal to the people. With house-
hold suffrage 104,000 electors would secure the
franchise . for the Council. As Kingston indig-
nantly points out, S. A. has been governed for the
last five years by 47,000 people—the minority of
Assembly-electors who possess the extra property-
vote for the Council.
Ex-Opppsition-leader Lyne is about the
most forlorn politician in N.S.W. just now. He
could not soar beyond a series of barren fights
against Premier Reid for office. Barton displaced
him as Opposition leader and Lyne became a
dissatisfied soul with increased leanings toward
provincialism. When he sulked in silence nobody
troubled him for guidance. And when he did
announce himself, it was again too late, for new-
ohum MacLaurin had jumped the leadership of
the Disunion-at-any-Price party. A man who
can miss two chances such as those in six months
is a hopeless case.
Last year the farmers in Tas. Assembly
fought as one enraged cookie against any reduc-
tion Of the Stock Tax, and the House of Landlords
was . only induced to agree to a three months’
abolition of the Tax under threat of a public
rising of meat-consumers. But although the Tax
has been in operation since Dec. last, the shortage
of meat in the island has gone on increasing, and
a meat famine seemed imminent until Parliament
met the other day and hurriedly resolved by 28
to 7 that the tax should be repealed. The
graziers voted with the ayes ; they now want to
import store stock.
A cry from Junee :
Dear Bulletin.— l am a commonplace person desirous
of voting the right way on Federal referendum day ; and
as lam a bit hazy on one point I appeal to you to cast
a ray of light there. The financial clauses of the Bill are
easily understandable, Daily Telegraph correspondents
having clearly pointed out that 3s. 7d. is 225. 6d., and Dr
MacLaurin having sworn that the Federal tariff will be
15 per cent., while Lyne says it will be 34J4 per cent,
ana they both say that Coghlan says it will be 27 per
cent. The financial arrangement is good enough for me
and I shall vote “Yes” to that part of the Bill. But
here comes my difficulty : Senators shall be elected by
the whole province voting as one constituency. Well
analysis of the voting at the late S.A. and 5. elections
clearly show that, properly organised, the Labor-party will
have no difficulty in securing a majority in the Senate.
Now, ardently as I desire Federation, I cannot contem-
plate a Labor Senate without a shudder. Just imagine
Senator Holman bringing in a bill to nationalise all coal-
mines, and all the coaslal shipping* of Australasia ; or
Senator H. George proposing an alteration of the Con-
stitution providing for the abolition of the Lower House
the members of which, he says, are ancient fossils, hide-
bound Tories, rusty clogs on the wheels of progress, &c.
Kindly tell me if the Senate—which is almost certain to
be entirely composed of Labor rev olutionaries—can carry
into effect legislation of this diabolical character. My
reading of the Bill says they can.—Yours, &c., Salt Fish.

(principally Scandinavians) who have
been rooted in Maoriland for 25 to
30 years, are disqualified because they
failed to take out letters of naturali-
sation in their younger days. Pre-
mier Seddon now proposes to wipe
out the proviso re naturalisation,
holding, very sensibly, that the man
who has put in 25 or 30 or 40 years’
steady residence and is otherwise
qualified is a good enough citizen
to pass muster.
Ex-Q. Premier Nelson has established a
record for impudence. He had the mountainous
effrontery to rise from his presidential chair in Q.
Council and object to the Commonwealth Bill,
‘ because Queensland had no hand in the framing
of the measure ! ” And, as all Australia knows,
Nelson was the man who prevented his province
sending representatives to the Federal Con-
vention ! Q. Federation League should prepare
big posters for referendum day, emphasising the
fact that the cause of unity is opposed by Nelson,
Barlow and Unmaek. Those three names should
work like magic among Democratic electors.
A recent scare heading in Sydney Tele-
graph reads—“ What the Conference Bill means
(with its Braddon Blot).” And then follows this,
among the rest: —
Out of every £lOO to be raised by uniform taxation,
N.S.W. will pay £37 10s. ; other provinces, £62 10s.
And when you figure this out according to the
latest population estimates it just amounts to the
awful fact that N.S.W. will pay as much per head
as its neighbours !! The Secret Conference paper
is just putting its great flat foot down on its own
assertion that N.S.W. is to pay more than its
share of taxation. The King-street paper should
swear off telling the truth ; almost every time it
does the results are disastrous.
THE TROUBLES OF VICE-ROYALTY .
From a publicly-exhibited Sidney phrenological
chart of Earl Beauchamps “His Excellency it
finely organized. The fulness at back head indi-
cates love of children and animals; it also influences
him to take an interest in philanthropic movements,
particularly orphanages.”
Apropos of the Disunion party’s spectre in
general, and Dr. MacLaurin’s spectre in particular,
of the alleged heavy tariff which must be imposed
under the Braddon Blot, this is worth quoting.
It is a clause from page 4 of the report of that
famous “Committee on Federal Finance,” of
which MacLaurin, French, and Bruce Smith were
the three members :
As regards the raising of revenue, there is NOTHING
laid down in the Bill prescribing the mode' by which the
Federal Parliament shall do.so. After the establishment
of uniform tariff the Parliament is free to impose direct
taxation and relieve customs, or to rely wholly on the
latter.
Whereby Dr. MacLaurin knocks out his own
apparition in one round.

An Ardent Appeal
Is often made to the public by the clergy for
assistance in worthy cases of affliction, but in the
following, the well-known reverend merely makes
a statement in the hope that it will be read, and
benefit those who may be suffering from liver
disease. He writes : “ I have pleasure in saying
that I have derived much benefit from the use of
Warner’s Safe Cure.”
WM. RIDING,
(Presbyterian Minister.)

round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heeJ OC.
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to. If you have a long thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a
view of introducing his new and simple system into the

has decided to supply his patrons at the lowest cash price.

Government Notices.
Federal Constitution.
Submission to the Electors of
New South Wales.
IT
is hereby notified for general information that a
Writ has this day been 1 issued in connection with the
question of the submission of the Australasian Federal
Constitution to the Electors of New South Wales for
acceptance or rejection; and that the following arrange-
ments have been made for taking the Poll in the perilous
of the aforesaid Colony known as the Electoral Districts
as follows, under the Parliamentary Electorates and
Elections Act of 1893.
Polling Places:
ANNANDALE: Glebe Town Hall; St. Aidan’s Hall,
Johnston-street, Annandale.
ASHFIELD : Ashfield Hall, late School of Arts, Ash-
field ; Congregational School Hall, Croydon ; Hall, Carlton
Crescent, Summer. Hill.
BALMAIN NORTH : Oddfellows’ Hall, Balmain.
BALMAIN SOUTH : St. Thomas’ Schoolroom, corner
of Darling-street and Weston Road; Central Hall, Darl-
ing-sti eet and Birchgrove Road.
BOTANY: North Botany Town Hall; Botany School
of Arts; Botany Road, near M’Evoy-street; Banks’
Meadow.
BURWOOD : North side of the Railway line ; south side
of the Railway-line ; near sourh boundary Municipality of
Concord; near Gasworks, Mortlake; in or near Town
Hall, Five Dock ; at or near the Concord Railway Station.
CANTERBURY : Canterbury ; Bankstown ; Enfield ;
Strathfield ; Cabramatta ; Liverpool; St. John’s Park ;
South Bankstown.
DARLINGTON : Ahercrombie-street.
GLEBE : The Record Reign Hall, Glebe ; The Glebe.
GRANVILLE : Auburn Council Chambers ; Granville
School of Arts ; New Town Hall, Rookwood; Merrylands;
at or near the Sydney Meat Preserving Company's Works
(Auburn).

Park.

Livingstone Road, near New Canterbury Road ; Ren wick-
street, The Warren ; Enmore Road, near Juliet-street;
Warded Road, near Marrickv ille Road.

Pretty well any sort of weakness or in-
capacitation is a crime in Victoria. Recently,
a young man, suffering from brain-tuberculosis,
was arrested for alleged insulting behaviour, and
given a month’s gaol, at the end of which he was
removed insensible to the hospital, where he
promptly died.
The Turner Government's programme for
the coming Vic. session includes a re-enactment
on a larger scale of the expiring Factories Act
with its minimum-wage proviso, a Referendum
Rill, an Old Age Pensions Bill, that oft-defeated
measure for the abolition of the plural property
vote, and a Usury Suppression Bill. But unless
the placid, comfortable Victorian people get
up with a ‘gibber” in each hand and inform
their House of 48 Rich Landlords that these
measures must pass, the chances are that the
Counci l wdl throw out four of them, and amend
to rea( J that interest mustn’t exceed
lo8(J per cent, per annunr.

Bert A. Levy, the black-and-white artist,
has severed his connection with the Alba Studios
and is now managing the Tosca Studios, Strand
Arcade, George-st. end. In a fortnight’s time an
entirety new show of artistic and up-to-date work
will be on view at this studio.*
Manifold are the uses of the country pro-
gress committee. White Cliffs institution (N.S.W.
backbloeks) lately decided to have a referendum
on the question whether the local doctor was a
satisfactory sawbones. But the doc.’s friends
guffawed so much at the proposal that, at the
eleventh hour, there were “ negotiations,” and the
appeal to the people ” was abandoned.

for the trots at the late Dubbo meetings, says that Alice
H. should not have been placed on scratch ; that Venus
should have had better than ssec. from What ; and that
Mr. Buekland resigned from Dubbo Trotting Club on
fcbe handicapping. As a matter of fact, the
trouble was in allowing Alice H. to trot in saddle when
the conditions stipulated harness, and it was on that
grievance that Mr. Buekland resigned. With regard to
placing Alice H. on scratch, she" was an unperfonued
maiden and was put on scratch with the other maidens
barring Miss Syntax, who was ssec. behind on account of
having shown the possession of some pace. Why Alice
H. should have been at a disadvantage with the other

The Duke of Westminster, who did the trick
with Flying Fox, has won more Derbies than any other
owner on the historic list. When the rich man first took
to racmg in his middle age he was said to have started a
stud for the purpose of making it over to his eldest son
but the son showed no interest in the game, whereas ids
starchy progen, lor became an enthusiast, knd stuck to
the gee-gees. Bend ’Or, the first one to carry the West
minster colors to the front, was ridden by Fred Archer
who came with an extraordinary late rush and beat a
better horse (Robert the Devil) by a head. The iockev
v, on the race that time. A couple of years later the Duke
W. had the luck to land the big classic event with a mare
named Shotover. Only three mares have won the Eng-

of Federation :
m COST OF A PLOUGH.
Take first a plough.
Cost in London £<i 10 0
Importing charges (freight, eto. j !!. ... 010 0
Cost in warehouse 0 0
Warehouseman’s profit, say, 25 per cent. '... 015 0
Charge to retailer £3 15 0
Retailer’s profit, say, 33 1-3 per cent. ... !.. 15 0
Cost to Public ... ... jgs o 0
Equals 100 per cent, above London invoice cost.
Under Federal tariff.
Cost in London ... £2 10 0
Importing charges (freight, etc.) 0 10 0
Duty, 25 per cent, on £2 15s 013 0
Cost in warehouse ... ... £3 13 9
Warehouseman's profit, say, 25 percent. 0 18 5
Cost to retailer ... ... £4 12 2
Retailer’s profit, say, 33 1-3 per cent. 110 9
Cost to public £a 2 11
Equal to 145 4-5 per cent, above London cost.
The additional retail price being £1 2s. lid., though
he revenue will only benefit to the extent of 13s. 9d.
According to the Sabbath tipster, of course, it
is so utterly impossible to establish an industry
in N.B. W. that the poor helpless Cornstalk will
pay £6 2s. lid. for a plough selling in London
at £2 10s., or go without it (and revert to
ploughing with a forked log) rather than
attempt to manufacture for "himself ! The
raving anti-Bill organ has taken the existing
Vic. duty as its basis, and it follows that a
plough obtainable in Sydney at £5 must now
c ? s . t * n Melbourne ,£6 2s. lid., but as a matter-
of-fact there are more Victorian-made ploughs
m use in N.S. YV. than any other kind. The
Victorian didn’t go on paying the extra £1 2s.
11<1. ; he started manufacturing, and he now
competes in Sydney against the imported
article. On another page of same issue there
is a two-column article on the decadence of the
trade of Freetrade Britain through the goods
of Protected America and Germany undersell-
ing her in her markets. Some extracts :
The Americans are underselling our manufactures of
steel and iron in the markets of the world, and actually
exporting thousands of pounds’ worth of machinery to
England, where, a short time ago, American locomotives
were absolutely unknown.
The Atbara bridge, ordered about February 1, was de-
livered aboard ship complete on March 7. The Americans
are making thirty locomotives for the Midland Railway,
and twenty locomotives for the Great Northern Railway,
and the first of these, ordered early in the year, will be
running in England before the spring is over.
Seven years ago, when Germany wanted a good ship,
she was obliged to come to England for it, and now she is
building bigger and faster steamers than those laid down
in British yards.
If Protection makes things so dear and so ruin-
ous, how are these things thus ?

Plyspeck Politics.
There’s some rather unseemly squabbling
over the disposal of the money subscribed for a
memorial to the late old Jack Robertson, i hose
who favor the establishment of a memorial bursary
at Sydney University don’t think that any part
of the coin should be spent on a monument.
Scientific agriculture is the subject favored tor
the bursary.
The Sydney anti-Federal party proposes
to import Higgins, the Vic. lawyer, a second time,
to deliver anti-Federal speeches, and its organ,
the Telegraph, has obtained the services of •
Lormer, the well-known dreary Melbourne J.Jr.,
to write against Federalism in its columns—wit-
ness his tremendous diatribe in last Tuesdays
issue. Also, the Disunion party tried to get the
aid of John Downer, of S.A. And yet bow
furiously the Telegraph and the anti-Federal
party used to curse about the purely-imaginary
assistance which they said the Federal party was
obtaining from the other provinces—principally
Victoria, By the way, Higgins has done one
good service : he has made G. Dibbs a courteous
gentleman —occasionally. At the ordinary anti-
Bill meeting Dibbs used to go on something awful
about the general blastedness and bankruptcy
and want of principle of the people across the
border. But a mild, scared, polite, apologetic ;
Dibbs took the chair at Higgins’s meeting and j
tried to look as if he regarded the Victorian as a .
man and a brother. Apparently Dibbs only
speaks evil of his neighbors behind their backs.
The soporific Wilks, M.L.A., is of
opinion that if Federation means closing
the port of Sydney, “ which is fast becoming
the emporium of Australasia,” it is too much
to pay for union. The Wilks person, like a very
large section of the Sydney people, doesn’t wait
to hunt up facts and find out whether Sydney
(which no one proposes to close up) is “ becoming
the emporium ”or not. As N.S.W. is obviously
the greatest and richest place on earth, and it is a
self-evident fact that it must be ahead of every
other place in everything, Wilks just takes things
for granted. In 1891, when N.S.W. was at the
top of its glory, it had per cent, of the total
trade of Australia, while at the end of 1898 it had
less than 33J per cent. This is a very large slice
of the trade, but, all the same, Sydney isn’t “ fast
becoming the emporium ; ” in fact, it is rather less
of an emporium than it was seven years ago.
The crafty, but benighted Anti-Feds, are
working all the ropes of prejudice, sectarianism
and misrepresentation to beat the Constitution
Bill at the Referendum. They tell the democrats
that Federation means perpetuation of black
labor, and they warn the sugar planter that the
Federal Parliament will ruin his industry by
depriving him of the “cheap and reliable they
tell the people of C.Q. that separation will be iin-
gossible under Federation, and they caution the
outh that unity spells disintegration ; they rouse
the Yellow Pup by whispering that the Chief-
Justice will be a R.C., and stir up the R.C.’s to
demand some form of State recognition; the
farmer is to lose his farm, the merchant his ware-
house, the manufacturer his factory, the worker his
billet, the squatter his station, the swagman his
swag—the devil his due. The only things that
will remain are desolation, famishing families,
mortgages, ticks, bloated politicians and ab-
originals. '
A few words from Hansart/ by Pilcher,
M.L.C. (N.S.W.), now engaged in defending the
alleged last ditch of democracy in company with
Secret Caucus members McGowan, Holman and
Hughes. On the Bill for mining on private lands,
this great Democrat said (Hansard, vol. LXIX.
pp. 684-5): —
We all know how the Bill came into existence, who
clamors for it, and how it is to be used (Hon. G. H. Cox :
“It is one of the planks of the Labor platform.”) Of
course, we know that. ... If we did not know the
history of the Bill we might suppose from what the hon.
gentleman in charge of the Bill says that the measure
was introduced for the benefit of all classes. Of course it
was not. It is no good trying to humbug hon. members.
... I admit that if Capital wanted the Bill, we ought to
pass it, but Capital does not want, and has never asked/or
U. . . . We are simply offering a sop to Cerberus.
Yet now Pilcher is capering round N.S.W. in
company with the aforesaid Cerberus in the
alleged interests of Democracy ! ! !
The Sydney anti - Federalists have a
strangely one-eyed way of looking at things. One
far-seeing proviso in the Federation Bill is that,
for 10 years, till the new order of things has got
into working-order, the Federal Govt, may lend
money (at interest) to any State which finds itself
in temporary embarrassment. This will enable
the central Government to lend Tasmania or Vic-
toria’s surplus to N.S.W., if N.S.W. happens to
need it; it is an all-round proviso, by which any
State may benefit. Considering that N.S.W.
has been for many years the biggest borrower
among the States, it is quite likely it will be the
chief borrower under this proviso. But the
-Sydney anti-Federalists invariably talk of this as
a clause under which N.S.W. (which has the
biggest deficit in Australasia) is to lend its sur-
plus ” to its neighbors. The wild, weird cry of
the State with the biggest deficit about how its
surplus is going to be annexed is a remarkable
hunk of Chinese vanity.
Concerning an interesting Westralian rail-
way matter: —
Wilkie Bros, paralysed a crowd of contractors by ten-
dering for the Coolgardie railway miles below depart-
mental estimates. They got it, and made a big pile—
anything under just £300,000 would be near it. When
the Mullewa-Cue line—about 190 miles— was secured by
Baxter and Prince for a little over £4OO per mile—nearly
a fourth less than Govt, estimate —it was at once seen that
the luckless public using the railway would be called on
to make up the difference. It was not long ere the first
section was opened and then the contractors showed their
hand The teamsters loading for the Murchison found
thev could do better by taking delivery of goods at Mul-
lewa—bead of the Govt, line—and went on strike. Baxter
and Prince then started their own horse-teams from
the end of their first section and were masters of
the situation —for a time, at least. They started a carry-
ing firm under the name of Prince, Baxter and Go., and
had a virtual monopoly until the teamsters knuckled
under The carrying trade was, however, killed and in
due course the rails were laid right into One, nearly a
year before contract time. Of course both passenger
and goods-traffic rates were enormously higher than those
on Govt, lines, but it was clearly a case of the public
grinning and bearing it, while John Forrest pointed to a
wondering world the spectacle of the cheapest railway
Tver constructed in Australia. Now for the sequel.
Baxter and Prince are claiming over £lOO,OOO for extras
and loss occasioned by faulty plans and the usual sbort-
s"ghtedness of "he engineering branch but over and
above all, they claim to have sustained loss through
the wholesale exemptions granted by Govt, while
their contract was in progress ! This is about the
most novel claim that the Forrest Govt, has yet had to
consider and it brings them face to face with a phase of
the Question they have always shirked—whether granting
exemption to English co.’s is more advantageous than
compelling them to observe the labor conditions, and so
nnkno , wn o men^wltile a^tlie’’la i’^ U ol'llroten jllll
case J Vtough state of affairs-for the arbitrator, at any
rate.
Tas Parliament, which met on May 30, rushed
the Great Western Railway Rights Restitution
Bill through in double-quick time; then the
weary Braddon was suddenly made to sit up
bv a want-of-confidence blow aimed by Opposi-
tion leader Stafford Bird. The ostensible
reason for the onslaught was the unprepared-
ness of the Govt, in the matter of public busi-
ness. Braddon expected the debate on the
Address-in-Reply and on the Great Western
Syndicate’s Bill to last at least a week, whereas
it lasted two days, and the voice of the unem-
ployed legislator was loud in the land. But
the real trouble was the appointment of Miles
to the Lands and Mines portfolio. Miles was
always considered a strenuous Oppositionist,
and has long been a thorn in Braddon s
weariness. The appointment is held to be
unconstitutional, unprecedented, and altogether
disgraceful; but, windy absurdities aside,
the Premier, by the appointment, placed a
weapon in the hands of his enemies which a
less-tired autocrat would have never dreamed
of doing. Braddon’s Ministry was returned hve
years ago to square the finances and to promote
the principle of land-value taxation. Ihe
finances have certainly been squared, but in-
stead of any extra taxation of the AB,OOU,UUU
worth of land-values, lands and incomes
have been relieved to some extent. For
the past year or two Braddon has
been understood to promise to alter all this,
but he seems to have met Parliament with no
financial proposals whatever, and, so far from
redeeming his ancient promise to make a Trea-
surer’s grab for a larger slice of the Tas. land-
lords’ unearned land values, he has taken into
his Ministry the man of all others in the
Assembly who is most bitterly antagonistic to
any such proposal. Miles’s one good point is
that he is an advocate of a “ wealth-tax, but
even this little semblance of democratic prin-
ciple is too hazy in Miles’s case to be worth
much. Opposition - leader Bird objects a
little to Miles’s principles, and much more
to being himself passed over by Braddon
when there was a portfolio going. The
rank-and-file of the Opposition, including
the dreadful Dobson clan, require no spurring
to make them charge full tilt any day at Brad-
don and all his works—the Dobsons regard
Braddon as a dangerous red revolutionist. It
was really thought that the division on the no-
confidence motion would be close, and that a
Dreadful Dobson Ministry was in sight. But
the Opposition underestimated Braddon. The
weary autocrat beat them by 25 votes to 10.
And as Braddon, Tory as he is, is vastly better
than the Dobson push, the result is just as well
for Tasmania.


4 Baby Lion I
Did you ever see one? It is as playful and
harmless as a kitten. You might have one
with you for weeks and not suspect danger.
But, beware! Some day it will surely
spring, and the fight for life is at hand.
It is just so with a cough. You may carry
one with you for weeks and not think of
danger. But the danger is there. Some day
the fight will be on, and it is a question which
will win, Consumption or You.

INVALIDS
AND
The aced.
From recent S.M. Herald leader on things in
Sydney 6
It is distressing to read in this report some of the in-
stances given of the consequences proceeding from this
state of things. The lady-inspector gives examples of
women who are unable to spend more than three shillings
a week on food, while the average rate seems to be some-
thing like a shilling per day. In the past four years the
J'' er J? e weekly wage of these women has decreased from
25s. 8d. to 15s. '2|d. Makers of Oxford shirts, selling at
Is. lid. upwards, are paid from 2s. 6d. to 4s. per dozen.
An , instance is mentioned of a woman who made
moieskin trousers at 6d. per pair, but since Christmas
there had been a fall of £d. This woman was asked
what she lived upon. “ Bread and black tea," was the
answer; “I have bought sixpenny-worth of damaged
potatoes since Christmas, half a pound of butter in four
or five weeks, and when I feel very w’eak I get two or
three penny-worth of the cheapest meat.” We have no
desire to enlarge on these painful revelations, which
speak for themselves while reminding us once more of the
silent misery that goes on unceasingly ' in various forms
and shapes wherever men and women'struggle fora liveli-
hood together.
Is this the glorious result of the Freetrade the
Sydney dailies have continuously preached ?
Five years ago the “same old George” in-
augurated his “ jolly and fat ” policy, and the
rate of pay of an already underpaid class of
female slaves has shrunk from 25s. 8d. to
15s. 2£d. per week in that time. And the
Foreign-trade organ has “no desire to enlarge
on these painful revelations.”
“ M.B.” on the lodge-doctor question :—
“ C.C.” (29/4/’99) howls with lodge-secretary fervor in
favor of the club-system, but his arguments are quite un-
sound when generally applied. In his case the bargain
seems fair enough—he being apparently unmarried—but
the lodge patient to whom a medico naturally objects is
the well-to-do person possessing a sickly wife and a
string of weedy children, with power to add to the
number. “C.C.” accentuates the fact that the doctor
examines the applicant before admission and can
thereby protect, himself against chronic invalids. This
sounds very nice, but he does not mention that in the
great majority of lodges the candidate’s wife is not put to
the test. The family is never examined. Also, in a
powerful lodge, things are so managed that the doctor
who refuses many applicants is soon boycotted, and sees
his list dwindling rapidly in favor of his less-scrupulous
colleagues. In addition to the members’ family, the
lodges are taking in his dependent relations, so that ere
long we may expect a single fee to include attendance
on the next - door neighbors and the housedog.
There is a gambling element in it, but the odds
are heavily in favor of the lodge. Ikjsy Mo lays the
odds as “C.C.” suggests, but the gentle son of Israel
will not lay them for a single stake against the separate
fancies of the bettor, his wife, family and dependent
relatives. Which is exactly what the lodges wish from
their sweated surgeons. A premium of even 14s. a year
for a healthy single man would not be objected to by the
majority of club-doctors, but it is absurd to expect that
without sweating it can be made to include perhaps 20
persons of all ages. Which is the point so conveniently
missed by the club advocates.
A giddy young woman of “ Sowth ” Melb.
was found drunk on Queen’s birthday in an
empty house in that suburb in company with a
couple of beery sailors. On being find 5s. next
morning the lady asked for time to hunt up her
husband who, she was sure, would pay ! The
police assured the bench that the charming damsel
was a bride of less than a month, which so melted
the susceptible J.P. that she was allowed to depart
in peace. The happy bridegroom has not yet
been heard from.

Agent-General for “ VITADATIO.”
Dear Sir,—
In sending you this testimonial, I feel it will no more
IfP re * en v s m y ca ® e than a st - Bernard pup represents the
size of his great parents, still, small as it is, it is the
truth, and that s the main thing.
You know I have been twice to England to try and get
ease from my sufferings, and was there treated' bv some
of the best medical skill in the world, but all'to no
purpose. They told me at last that nothing could be
fS , even t 0 ease my pain (except to inject morphia), so
L had to g° on suffering, with no hope of ever getting
better. No one but a man like yourself can realise the
gloom and misery, yea, agony of such a state, but you
m bee i‘ "j 1 ®|milar plight, know how one in such a
case longs for death, and often feels inclined to do some
desperate act to be rid of such an existence.
I have tried all different treatments, Allopathic,
Homoeopathic, Hydropathic and Electropathic, I also
have tried Russian, Electric and Turkish Baths, and
others too numerous to menrion. One gentleman in
Sydney had me under the Crisis Treatment for six
months and when I would not let him torture me any
longer, he declared that if I allowed my sore, raw bodv
to dry, I should soon be a dead man. I told him that
death would rescue me from suffering, so I would face it.
I parted, telling him that if Satan was hard driven to find
some special torture to punish his most hated victims, I
thought that he was the man that could teach him
(Satan) some wrinkles.
When returning from England three years ago, God
knows that I never expected to land in Australia, I had
then given up all doctors, and just meant to let myself
die , I thought, surely I have tried enough and spent
enough, so will try no more. Thus I was just suffering
on till death should release me. Everything I did
whether eat, walk, talk, or sleep, was a burden to me. ’
Many who read this will remember how I then some-
tinies screamed with agony night and day. I went over
to Tasmania; here I first heard of “Vitadatio,” and I
may tell you that (if it had not been for reading the
declaration made by four gentlemen, who signed their
names, referring to the genuineness of your cure of Hy-
datids, in Invercargill, New Zealand), all the people in
this world would not have induced me to again start
taking physic, as I had been taking medicines for 13
years, and was getting worse.
I could not get “ Vitadatio ” in Burnie (Tasmania), so
had to wait till I came back to Melbourne. I procured
my first bottle at the Mutual Store, Flinders-street, Mel-
bourne.. I took five large bottles. It certainly strength-
ened me, but it did not take away my severe pain, so,
like many, I gave it up.
i A i )ol r , n foUr . months afterwards, Mr. Button, of Oat-
Jands, lasmania, introduced me to you (Mr. Palmer) and
your remarkable case, being so much like my own, led
me to go in for “Vitadatio," if it, should take my last
penny. I drank, as you know, 2 bottles weekly, yea, one
day I drank a large size bottle. I found it was food and
everything to me.
A well-known doctor with whom I was friendly con-
gratulated me on my improved looks each time he saw
me, so I kept on taking “ Vitadatio,” and, as you remem-
ber, I came every other day to your Head Office ; and so,
by seeing you, got your encouragement, which kept me
taking the medicine. I endured the pain and drank the
remedy, until the disease began to come away. Soon it
came away so fast that jl have got bottles full of the
disease three or four times a week, which I brought to
This, that had puzzled the doctors, when inside me
now puzzled them as they saw it in the bottles ; one even
said that it was the “lining of my bowels” coming awav.
in fact, I understand one of the doctors sent a letter to a
leading morning paper to that effect. I should think he
would now withdraw it. I said, “I hope I shall soon be
free from all such ‘lining’ as it is taking awav the
pain, and since the time I lost the “lining 6t my
bowels ” (as the doctors called it), I have put on a stone
weight of flesh, ano “ Hallelujah,” I can now sleep, eat
walk, talk and run, and that with pleasure (should the
same doctor see me now, instead of wanting ine to make
my will, as he did then, I think he would pass me for anv
Life Insurance Office). y
I have been cured of the following :
1. Running sores in the legs, of over 40years’standing.
To show the thoroughness of the cure, a small dog bit me
on the very place five u'eeks ago, which caused me some
sound and tW ° dayS ° f Pa ' n ’ but is now thoroughly
2. p iles, that for years were a trouble to me, are now
a thing of the past—from them I am perfectly free
3. Singing in my head, which was a constant nuisance
to me for seven (7) years, has completely left me.
4. Some small growths on the skin, which often troubled
me, have withered away, leaving only a stain on the skin.
th’J « v-7i e !P e ?, k i 8 as 80Und a 8 can be - I did not

only for the dreadful agony which I suffered, on account
