The Stick and the Crust.
A stick and a crust of bread. Like the hands of
a clock these two articles told the time o’ day for
nearly a year in a certain man’s life. Yet, unlike
the hands oi a clock, they were not visible at once.
When he needed the stick he had no use for the
crust ; and when the crust was welcome he had no
further occasion for the stick.
Albeit he was a young fellow of twenty-six, you
would be wrong in supposing this stick to have
b en in the nature of a weapon for attack or de-
fence. In that case the crust and the stick would
have harmonised. As it was, they did not. For
the stick was a support, not a cluh.
Now, when a man feels the pressure of eighty or
ninety years, he is apt to want a travelling com-
panion of that sort ; but one in the very heyday of
youth, not suffering from any injury and not con-
stitutionally feeble, or malformed, should com-
monly be able to walk without a stick. And so
this young man had always done up to the time
when he fell out with the crust and with all that
the crust stood for or represented.
His own account of the circumstances runs
thus:—“Up to October, 1893, I bad been a
strong, healthy, and active man. Then I com-
menced to feel weak and out of sorts. I was
heavy, tired, and had no ambition or energy.
What had come over me I could not imagine. I
had a foul, nasty taste in the mouth, and was
constantly spitting up a thick, dirty phlegm. My
appetite, left me, and what little I ate lay on my
stomach like lead, causing me great pain about
the chest. A short, distressing cough settled
upon me and troubled me day and night.
“ At night my sleep was disturbed and broken
with night sweats and frightful dreams. I had
great pain at the left side around the heart, and
my breathing was hurried and short. Next I
began to spit blood, and was greatly alarmed at
it. I wasted away rapidly, losing over a stone
weight in a month, and became so weak that I
was unable to rise on my feet without assistance.
“Although only a young man of twenty-six, I
was obliged to hobble about with a stick , and could
walk hut a short distance even at that. Worried
and anxious, I attended the Yoik County Hospital,
where the doctors sounded me, and said I was in
a consumption.”
Here we have another of the serious and often
fatal mistakes Uat are made in cases like this.
Misled by symptoms which in some respects re-
semble those of consumption, medical men hastily
decide that the lungs are affected, treat the patient
perfunctorily for the hopeless disease he is not
afflicted with, and leave the result to chance.
Hence he often dies of dyspepsia and its complica-
tions - his true disease—which, unlike consump-
tion, is easily curable by the remedy our friend
finally employed.
u They gave me cod-liver oil,” he continued,
“and medicines, but I got no better. Indeed, I
was so low-spirited and miserable I didn’t care
what became* of me. As time passed, I grew
weaker and weaker.
“ After I had endured ten months of this, Mr.
R. W. Dickinson, the chemist in Walmgate, ad-
vised me to try Mother Seigel’s Syrup. After
taking it a few days I felt muoh better, my appe-
tite reviving and my food giving me no pain. I
continued to take this medicine only, and soon
the cough and breathing trouble left me and I
began to gain strength and flesh. When I had
taken three bottles I was as strong as ever, and
could eat and enjoy even a drif crust. I have
since had good health. You are liberty to publish
this letter and refer all inquirers to me. (Signed)
Isaiah Lewis, 124 Walmgate, York, April Bth,
1894.”
If the reader wonders how a man could suffer
so much, become so emaciated and weak, and be
pushed so near the grave’s edge through what is
sometimes flippantly called “mere indigestion,”
he has yet to learn that the digestion is the arbiter
of life and death. The “crust” (food), enjoyed
and digested, means life and strength. Rejected
it means the “stick,” to supplement swift-coming
weakness ; and then the prone position, when help
is vain. Mother Seigel’s Syrup enabled Mr. Lewis
to substitute the. crust for the stick. It cured his
dyspepsia.*

EIGHT YEARS’ SUFFERING.
AT TIMES
A PERFECT CRIPPLE.
Teralba to the fore with a Wonderful
Cure.
The Case of
Mrs. W. FULTON.
(For the “ Newcastle Hemhl”)
News of the wonderful recovery of Mrs.
W. Fulton, of Teralba, havin' readied
Newcastle, a reporter was despatched to
investigate the case, with instructions to
get the plain truth. His enquiries in the
township before seeing Mrs. Fulton went to
prove that, in this case, Clements Tonic
had performed a really wonderful cure, and
this will be conclusively proved by the facts
related by the lady herself in the presence
of one of her sons and a neighbour.
Mrs. Fulton, a widow now for 13 years,
is about the oldest resident in Teralba., and
is well known as a respectable, bard-
working woman and the mother of a large
family. When the reporter explained his
mission to Mrs. Fulton she extended her
hand and accorded him the warmest of
warm welcomes.
“ Yes, I am delighted you have called,’*
was her first remark when we had got
seated. “A gentleman,” she added,” called
the other day, and I told him that I knew
from practical experience more about the
virtues of the preparation than any other
living woman. I told him about the
benefits I had derived from Clements
Tonic. ”
“Might we again go into the details of
your illuess ?” suggested the reporter.
“With pleasure, sir,” replied Mrs. Fulton.
“I have often said to the neighbours here
that I ought to write into the paper about
my wonderful cure. Now'that you are here
I shall tell you everything, and I should
feel graceful to you if you publish my
account ”
“I can tell you,” said Mrs. Fulton,
commencing her narrative, “ that I had
been bad for eight years off and on. I was
at timesa perfect cripple, suffering, I believe,
from sciatica; the pains used to attack me
in the chest, the small of the back and the
hip, the knee joints, and in the left ankle.
For months I could not walk, I must have
been six months laid up in bed, fairly
screaming with the pain. Many a time I
prayed for death. I took more medicines
than would scald a pig, and I spent pounds
upon pounds of money that was badly
needed for the household upon so-called
remedies and doctors’ fees. I could neither
eat nor sleep owing to the severity of the
pains. I wasted away to a mere shadow,
witli cold sweats still running off my
forehead under the strain of acute pain.
I was a martyr to indigestion for some
thirty years. This boy (pointing to bsr
son) and his married sister can tell you that
they have, at my request, laid on me to
kill the pain in my chest from indigestion.
There is no fiction in this story. All the
people in Teralba know bow I have suffered.
They all know, too, that ihe doctors gave me
up and told my sons that I could not
possibly get over it.”
“ I'es.” interjected the lady from next
door, who had been listening to all Mrs.
Fulton bad said, “ I know it for a fact that
Mrs. Fulton was given up by the doctors.”
“I was nothing but bone and skin,”
continued Mrs. Fulton, “when I was
re ommended to try Clements Tonic some
six or eight months ago. My son, Andrew,
an engine driver, wrote from Murrurundi,
where lie was then located, and told me to
try Clements Tonic. He said that one of
the engine drivers up there h-d suffered
much in the fame way as I was suffering,
and that Clements Tonic had cured him.
I must say I did not relish the idea of more
expense in patent medicines, but soma
friends peisuaded me to get a bottle, and I
thank heaven that I did so. I have since
had eight lar. e bottles and a few small
ones, ami I will never be without a bottle
as long as I have the means to buy one.”
“ Id ow soon did you experience relief?”
“The first bottle practically cured me,”
said Mrs. Fulton, in a voice that was deep
with emotional gratitude. “I shall never
forget the change that came over me, for I
believe I have suffered more than any other
woman of my age (57) who lia still to work
for a living.”
“Which left first—the indigestion or tin.
sciatica pains ? ”
“ Well I believe the relief first came in
the chest, because after a few doses I
became able to eat a little, and I remember
saying to my married daughter that I felt
able to digest my food. 1 notice this about
Clements Tonic that the more one takes of
it the bttter in health one becomes. Take
my case as an example. I have been
getting better and better with each bottle
until I am as you see me now—fat and
well.”
The reporter had to confess that it was
hard to believe that a wasted woman who
was looked upon as a chronic cripple could
possibly be the same plump and hearty
figure that he had just noticed coming dowr
the bill.
“It is the absolute gospel truth,” inter
jeettd the neighbour, “that a few months
back Mrs. Fulton was looked upon as an
incurable cripple, seeing that she had been
laid up for six months.”
“ Yes, indeed ! I never thought,” added
Mrs. Fulton, “ that I would ever he able
again to leave my bed, much less be able to
walk straight.”
“The troubles have now all disappeared,
I suppose ?”
“Yes, my general state of health i
better now than it has been for twentj
years past. I can eat well and relish mj
food. I can do hard work with any other
woman of my age, and I can enjoy the
benefits of a good night’s sleep—a thing that
has been unknown to me. I attribute it all
to the use of Clements Tonic, and I take it
that it will be just as effectual to the poor
sufferer. If you publish the facts as I have
given them to you I am sure the report will
he read with interest by all the people of
Teralba, because, as I said before, they all
know how bad I was. It was a great
triumph for Clements To ne, and Mr.
Clements can publish it how he likes.”

COUNTRY TRIAL ORPCR RESPCCTFULLY SOLICITED
The Circling Hearth-Fires.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My Countrymen, though we are young as yet
With little history, nought to show
Of lives enleagued against a foreign foe—
Torn flags and triumph, glory or regret ;
Still some things make our kinship sweet,
Some deeds inglorious but of royal worth,
As when with tireless arms and toiling feet
We felled the tree and tilled the earth.
’Tis no great way that we have travelled since
Our feet first shook the storied dust
Of England from them, when with love and trust
In one another, and large confidence
In God above, our ways were ta’en
’Neath alien skies—each keeping step in mind
And soul and purpose to one trumpet strain,
One urging music on the wind :
Yet tears of ours have wet the dust, have wooed
Some subtle green things from the ground—
Like violets—only violets never wound
Such tendrils round the heart; the solitude
Has seen young hearts with love entwine ;
And many gentle friends gone down to death
Have mingled with the dust, and made divine
The very soil we tread beneath.
Thus we have learned to love our country, learned
To treasure every inch from foam
To foam ; to title her with name of Home ;
To light in her regard a flame that burned
No land in vain, that calls the eyes
Of rnen to glory heights and old renown;
That wild winds cannot quench, nor thunder-skies
Make dim, nor many waters drown.
Six hearths are circled round our shores, and
round
The six hearths group a common race,
Though leagues divide; the one light on their face,
The same old songs and stories rise ; the sound
Of kindred voices and the dear
Old English tongue make music ; and men move
From hearth to hearth with little fear
Of aught, save open arms and love.
To keep these hearth-fires red, to keep the door
Of each house wide—that is our part!
Surely ’tis noble ! Surely, heart to heart,
God’s love upon us and one goal before
Is something worth ! Something to win
Our hearts to effort ! Something it were good
To garner soon ! And something ’twould be sin
To cast aside in wanton mood.
My Countrymen, hats off ! with heart and will
Thank God that you are free, and then
Arise, and don your Nationhood like men,
And, manlike, face the world for good or ill.
Peace be to you, and in the tide
Of years great plenty till Time’s course be run ;
Six Ploughmen in the same field side by side,
But, if need be, six Swords as one.
RODERIC QUINN.
The Revenge of the Mind.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was at the rare times when his spiritual, in-
tellectual nature was momentarily triumphan
that he tried to grapple with the problem o ns
own character. It had early been instilled into
him that reason was superior to instinct, ye in
his own case he saw the refutation of this theory.
Here, invariably, in his early manhoo > tie
mind-man paid homage to the flesh-man. 1 e
former —artistic, aesthetic —abhorred eveiytiing
that savored of grossness or sensuality ; baske in
the glow of poetry and art ; sought its delights in
the stars and the music of the silence. The latter
—essentially gross —craved unceasingly foi t ie
earth and its vileness, and invariably, after a
bitter fight with its antagonist, it obtained its de-
sire.
And this was the problem he was slow to solve :
why did reason ever succumb to instinct ; and
which was the real man —the flesh or the mind ?
The first flush of youthful manhood had left
him, when the flesh-man, seeking ever new de-
lights, saw and loved the woman whom he made
his wife. She was of a distinctly lower type than
himself. She had all his sensuality and grossness,
without any of his redeeming intellectuality ; but,
nob yet awakened to this fact, in the early time of
his marriage he was very satisfied with the woman
he had wedded.
His love for her became almost madness. He
forgot in its warmth all other objects in life ; he
was content to spend every moment of his time in
her company, and his happiness seemed complete
on the birth of his infant daughter.
As the years went by this love for mother and
daughter—only the one child was born to him—
continued unabated ; and then, when he was only
at that period of life when most men are strong
and vigorous, he found himself changing. The
elasticity began to desert his limbs ; the bright-
ness to go from his eyes, and the zest for life to
wane. What formerly he had delighted in he
now viewed with indifference, even disgust. He
secluded himself more and more from all outside
companionship, and his temper, always uncertain,
became more fitful than ever. A moodiness
settled upon him, and he realised that at length
the rule of the flesh-man had ended, and that the
mind-man reigned—despotic, unpitying, demand-
ing of him stern accouut of the doings of its pre-
decessor. ~ .
His days were passed now in brooding, brood-
ing. And a thing which surprised those who
noticed it, his love for wife and child grew cold
and colder as the months went by and his illness
increased, till it seemed that their very presence
was hateful to him. .
His wife, in her dull, narrow way, noticed the
change, and wondered at it. At first, she thought
it merely a passing phase of his illness ; then, as
his dislike intensified instead of departing, she
grew angry and cold, and blamed him bitterly for
his altered deameanor. She never doubted that
it was merely that he had grown tired of her; her
reason was too limited ever to suspeot that perhaps
a deeper motive lay somewhere.
And his daughter—she had grown into radiant
maidenhood. Prom every movement of her lithe,
graceful limbs appealed the vigor of an ardent,
passionate blood. In the rounded contour of the
cheek ; in the challenge of the laughing eyes ; in
the smile of the red, ripe lips, one saw life—life,
flushed and triumphant. She marked the change
in her father, and, possessing some of his intellect,
wondered wherein lay the cause. She tried, as
her girlish tact directed her, to win back his love.
That which she knew had the strongest appeal to
him, that she laid at his feet, only to find that her
efforts, instead of pleasing, irritated him, and far
from calling back that former love, rather intensi-
fied his hate. .
And day by day, as his weakness increased, the
man sat by himself, brooding, brooding.
* * * »
All day the man had been irritable and moody,
and his wife heard him, hour after hour, in the
room overhead pacing up and down, with scarcely
a rest in the monotony. And when night came
he lit the lamp, and, unlocking a drawer in his
desk, emptied the contents upon the table, and
sat down.
The blackness of his face deepened, as one by
one he examined the objects before him, and laid
them in a pile in the centre of the table. A letter,
in its faded ink and with its gilt edges turning
green, he tore into fragments, with a sneering
curse. A bunch of withered flowers, with a dis-
colored ribbon twisted about them, he crunched
and rolled in the palms of his hands, and cast the
dust upon the remnants of the letter. A tiny
locket with an indistinct miniature portrait made
him smile, only it was a smile that had no mirth
in it. And then he found a photograph. A little
baby face smiled out at him, and a pair of tiny
hands stretched forth as to embrace him. At the
sight, his face grew livid with anger, and he
started from the face in the frame as if it were a
thing of evil. He lifted the photograph to cast it
into the fire
“Father, see what mother gave me. It is
my birthday.” His daughter was smiling at
him from the open door —a radiant figure of life
and youth. In her outstretched hand she held a
golden chain and a trinket from which the lamp-
rays sprayed indiscriminately in showers of
brilliant light-atoms.
For an instant the man cowered away from her,
and his hands sought his face as if the vision
blinded him. Then a sudden, mad passion moved
him to action : a snarling curse rang from his
livid lips and he leapt to her side. He dashed
the glittering trinkets to the floor and crushed
them beneath his heel. Then his hands' darted
viciously forward . . . His wife, dozing by the
fire downstairs, heard something which caused
her, in very excess of terror, to leap up the stair-
case.
* * • *
It was after the passing of the long illness
brought upon her by the tragedy of that night
that his wife went to see him. The asylum
attendant told her as he conducted her down a
long, lighted corridor: “He is very quiet, and
seldom speaks—except to himself. He is quite
harmless now. When the days are fine and warm
lie walks by himself for hours about the grounds,
his head bowed and his hands clasped behind him,
thinking; brooding. At times a queer mood
comes to him, and then by his actions you would
judge that he re-enacts some deed that impressed
him in the past. He is in here.”
The attendant softly opened a door at the end
of the corridor and peeped in. Then He stepped
back, and, motioning the wife to approach, he
whispered, “Look now.”
The man was sitting by a table, his face
glowing with smouldering anger, ana his eyes
shining steadily. His hand* moved, one over the
other, as if tearing something; they swept for-
ward across the table as if gathering something
into a heap. He crunched and rolled his palms
together, and again made that sweeping forward
movement.
Next, he seemed to be looking at something in
his outstrete ed hand, and the watchers at the
door saw him shrink from it, as if struck in the
face. Turning at that instant, he saw them, and
started to his feet. His face lighted as he recog-
nised his wife. “ You have come at last,’’ he said,
monotonously—as if he had said it all before. “ I
have wished for that. I wanted to tell you why I
did it—why I killed her. It was the revolt of the
mind against the tyranny of the flesh ; revenge for
past insult and neglect. She came to me dancing,
with her beautiful face aglow ; hurrying to show
me her pretty things because it was her birthday.
Her birthday ! Did she know—or did you—how
I have cursed the day which saw her birth? I
am old and weak and'so very weary—and why?
The fault was hers. She robbed me of my strength
—my life. She came to me so strong ; so young ;
so beautiful ; but did she owe it to herself? She
owed it to me. To me, I tell you, whose life-
blood she sucked from my veins. It was
my vitality that gave the challenge to her
eyes. My blood flushed in her cheeks, and
crimsoned her lips, and lent grace to her limbs.
Why should I be so weak, and she so strong? Am
I not greater than she? It has been growing a
long time, this passion which has ended thus. In
my youth I was strong ; in my early manhood not
even the Immortals excelled me ; and now what do
you see me ? A cinder, lightless and cold ; the
fire has gone out. Naught remains to me but my
brain, and that is a torment—it thinks, remem-
bers, reproaches. ‘ Day by day I saw her grow-
ing stronger and fairer, and felt myself weakening
—dying. I wondered why she should laugh and
be beautiful. And then I knew. She had assumed
the life that was going from me. I saw in her the
embodiment of all my wasted vitality ; all the
vigor of my body was gathered within her. I
knew it, and it made me mad. And she came
when the devil was strongest. It was the anni-
versary of her birth—and the desire for revenge
came to me. I fed it upon memories of what had
been. See yonder—the fragments of torn paper ”
with a gesture towards the table, as if what he.
spoke of lay there. “Your letter one of
many which you sent me years ago. That
crushed flower you pinned in my coat on our
wedding-day. Pleasant reminiscences, were they
not, when you think to what they led ? The
miniature portrait of yourself is there too ; do you
remember it ? I have often cursed the face it re-
presents —yours; and you brought her into the
world ; my lost vitality vivified. And then that
little photograph! she as a baby, clad in her
pretty laced frock which you made for her, and
diademed by the red roses which I placed in the
flaxen curly hair of which we were so proud.
Cannot you see it even there? My life moving
her baby hand and wreathing the prattling lips
into that baby smile. My soul looking out from
her baby eyes. And she came when I was mad ;
came to tell me it was her birthday. Why did ,
she come ? ”
P. T. FREEMAN.

The Blind Eye of the Law.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It is generally considered an impossible thing to
marry another man’s wife. But this is a mistake.
It is possible in Maoriland ; More than that—it
has been done there.

Suddenly I’uku broke off her conversation with

cannot walk back to the kaianna, and he is too

—he will let you have it—and take the child back

Cruttenden got out of the koki and romped
awhile with Pepeha on the sand, and when Kura
had taken her p ace in the little craft, he waded
out and placed the child safely by his nurse, and
the woman and Pepeha soon disappeared from
the scene, rukutuku and the white man were
alone upon the beach.

Ruku had had his fill of war. He had returned
to Quiokau a, battered wreck, with a limp in his
a,,d mum l f ™ fyo. All his handsomeness
vy.is gone-one bullet had struck him obliquely in
the cheek, and had played havoc with his face,
and--another had smashed his ankle-bone. So
much for Rukus passion for war.
His wife scorned him ; she would hardly look
at him Ins face certainly formed her sufficient
“ 7 S ! said 1,6 was Hke a lame pukekohou-
ping about a swamp. Neither did he get anv
sympathy from the people of the kaian /a—they.
. a | Aft *“ stays too long from his mate,
another bird will vvarm his nest for him.” Even
his child avoided him, and, frightened at his dis-
figured face, ran to the white man.
In the good old pr e-pakeha days, Ruku would
have quickly squared things with a tomahawk
with such a man as Cruttenden, but, now, the
Spleen s writ rail through Maorilaud from end to
end, and Ruku knew only too well what the law
he had helped to establish would do if he “ tuki-
tukied a white man. So for a while he did
nothing but brood over bis sullied honor and
vanished prestige.
But the half-blind cripple was still a true toa : it
was more than flesh and blood could stand to see
with las remaining eye—another man usurp his
conjugal rights. And—Law or no Law-one dark
night, murder was all Rut committed in Tulcu-
tuku s ivharc.
Cruttenden was well versed in legal matters per-
tarning to the Maori, and knew just, how the case
stood in the Eye of the Law. And, one day,
when a cutter appeared off Omokau, he put off to
it in_a canoe, and stayed a long while conferring
with the skipper. ”
Next morning, the cutter sailed away with
Oruttenden and Tukutuku on hoard.
. * * •
The District Court of Bether was sitting, and
District Judge Vanderdiek sat upon the bench
In the box’ stood Ruku, with a big, half-healed
wound down his. right cheek, and his face uglier
than ever—Cruttenden had evidently come off
best in the midnight broil.
Rnku addressed the Court through an inter-
preter. “ .before Te Kpoti 'haute liack' from the
Chathams I was married to my wife. I am a
good subject of Queen Wikitoria: in fighting for
her I became what I now am—a man only good
for digging kumura. My wife has gone off with
a pakeha , who was sent to my kaianpa while I was
at the war. I want the Government to give her
back to me. When I tell her to come, she says
she is married to the pakehct according to the
proper ri(cn;/a of the white mart. If Igo and kill
that pakeha and take her, the Law will kill me
quick—no good ! Now, I want you, the Judge
who sees to these, things, to get my wife Tukutuka
and bring her back to me. I who have fought for
your Queen say, ‘Let the strong hand of your
Law give me back my wife from the pakeha who
stole her.”’
District Judge Vanderdick looked at his notes ;
mumbled to the Clerk of the Court:; examined his
notes again, and then said, “ When did the Maori
marry this woman ? ” And the answer came back
through the interpreter, “ I married her six years
before Te Kouti massacred the people of Poverty
Bay. I fought with R ipata against Te Kooti.”
“ But ask him how he married her? ”
“How did I marry?” exclaimed Ruku. “1
married her all right, in the way my ancestors
married before me. I think that a” very good
way.”
Judge Vanderdick fumbled with his notes. He
had never had such a case before. u Er—ah, the
fact is, •’ he said, “that is to say, I’m shuah this
is—ah—very awkward, very awkward indeed, but
the Law does not seem to make provision for such
a case. Ihe Law does not seem to—ah—recognise
the Maori.form of marriage. In the eye of the
Law, this man’s wife was not legally married till
she married this—ah—white man, of the name of
—er— yes, of—or—the name of Cruttenden, accord-
ing to the prescribed legal method. This is a
most unusual case : the Law seems to have over-
looked the status of such men as this-er—Roko-
roko. Eh ? . Ruku ? Yes, to be sure, his name
is Ruku. Tell Ruku, then, that the Law is
powerless to interfere on his behalf—er—ves, on
his behalf—yes, entirely powerless to interfere,”
When Ruku had fully grasped what the learned
Judge meant, he delivered himself thus: “You
tell your Judge his Law is like me—blind in one
eye. With its other eye it looks at the white man
it never sees the Maori unless he has been
d ring something wrong. Some day—l’m not sure
when—but some day, we Maori inen will knock
out its other eye,.and then your law will be blind
in both eyes—like your Judge there.” But the
interpreter failed to translate—he was too taken
aback at this flagrant contempt of court—and
Ruku limped out of The Court-house like a lame
.pukeko hopping outof a swamp.
. Cruttend n was left in indisputable posses-
sion of Tuku, his lawful wedded Wife, and tier
ten thousand acres of land. -
